The Red Page
In the Romance Country
It wasn't precisely a wonderful night of
stars, but it was the sort of night that might
be expected. The utmost vigilance has failed
to prevent such nights. A pale, stout, speech-
less Jupiter was sliding like a bootless ghost
into the west. For a time it lurked behind a
greyish, whitish, brownish puff of vapor,
whose shape .for the moment was that of a
drowned; horse, upside down. It was a
changeful shape. A little while before it
had the* form of a fallen palace. A little
later it was going to develop into an oak
on which a curate had committed suicide.
Indefinite shades of drab and slate and
yellow shifted about as decomposition of the
icurs set in. When it had sunk a little
furtu.. the stout, refulgent Jupiter dodged
from its ...chush to the safer cover of the
horizon.
At the other end of the sky a round, white,
addled moon swarmed into view. It had the
face of a despondent preacher. A drove of
asteroids and other truck and small deer and
trash of the heavens covered the zenith, and
between them were black spaces which sug-
gested the preacher’s garments.
A grove of pines stood up on a great
plain in the fashion of a meeting of credi-
tors, and a plausible, muffled wind dis-
coursed among the leaves. It had the voice
of one who might a wail unfold. Sometimes
it spoke through its nose to suggest the
Hebrew of fiction who was going to sell up
everybody when the story really commenced.
A tall Pharisee or Leicester Dedlock of a
tree stood apart. It was a family tree.
Also, it was an affair with large, shoe-like
roots, and a lank, long body, and a stiff
neck, and a faint whisker of branches with
the birds’-nests of old age in the whisker,
and a top that was akin to a bigoted head
and a plug-hat. Round its feet a creature
that hung down at each end and humped up
in the middle was eating scrub. It carried
an aspect of poverty which was its sole
luggage, and was perfectly nude. Without
committing itself to be a stag or a Jersey, it
filled its part as a link with pastoral pur-
suits, and was reminiscent of deer parks
and ancestral greatness.
This was the stage-setting, and it sug-
gested brooding calamity. It is put in to
save details about the downfall.
Things developed and flickered and
changed form and passed. From the top-
most branch of the Pharisee tree the fate of
the ancient house was hanging by a hair. It
had a bloated, suffocated look. The sus-
pense was dreadful. The family skeleton
also hung from the tree, or part of it did.
Some of the bones had been boiled for soup
when the glory departed, and nothing was
left save the pride of race—which was
mostly horse-race, with the proud family
horse running last —and the equity of re-
demption of a bone stud. Tlje rest of the
stud had been seized by the creditors, leav-
ing the stable empty. The third article on
the tree was a pair of pants with the family
seat worn out.
A new darkness passed across the face of
the addled moon. It was the cloud which
hung over the hero’s birth. The bar sinister
had closed at six. The wind had dropped
some time ago and been succeeded by the
profound silence of the person who knew the
real criminal but declined to commit him-
self, knowing the magistrate would do that,
anyhow. Then a breath of suspicion blew
fitfully through the boughs, and it became
necessary to put on a gas-mask on account
of the poisonous aspersion that rolled along
the ground like a wide Axminster boa-
constrictor.
Just here the story started in a real
hurry.
Rising suddenly out of nowhere a stupen-
dous preparatory lunatic-asylum developed
along the horizon. It was white-washed
as a sign of hypocrisy, or innocence,
or some other old thing. The height
of it was 20 storeys and the length
of it, till the ends became invisible,
was a mile. At each window appeared
a young and beautiful but idiotic female
countenance. Apparently the female was
nude and unmarried—at least her shoul-
ders and arms and her swelling bust
had nothing on. The swell of her bust
was so marked that it made navigation diffi-
cult. She gazed fixedly at nothing. One ear
was slightly larger than the other through
listening for the voice of undeveloped love.
Most of her was an orphan. She had no
distinguishing marks about her alabaster
form unless they were on her spine.
Every now and then one of the row of big
iron doors of the asylum opened, and a
beautiful but simple and trusting female of
the average age of 23 was shot out in a
single paragraph without any luggage.
There was no bruise on her hinder part to
suggest that she had been kicked out. Either
she was propelled by invisible forces or she
came voluntarily, knowing that the time
had arrived. She was immediately seized
by a dai’k, aquiline female of varying height
and by a sinister sort of man whose breath
destroyed the vegetation. They put a white
frock on her and let her hair down, and
fixed chains on her hands and feet. Wear-
ing a look of resignation, she held up each
foot to be chained—in fact, she was so
anxious to be good and submissive and per-
secuted that she tried to hold up both feet
at once. They told her partly that the man
was her uncle, whom she had never heard
of: also her guardian and trustee, and they
intimated, partly, that all was discovered.
V\ ithout their protection the results of her
past deeds would be terrible, in addition to
which the mystery would blow up like the
hd _ blowing off Vesuvius. As the price of
their silence, she was to live with them in
secrecy and be handy woman in all their
crimes. She assented, weeping, though she
hadn’t the least idea what they were going
to be silent about. A drove of obvious detec-
tives was passing at the time, but she didn’t
communicate with them.
These preliminaries being fixed up, she
was removed on foot, and, carrying a tear-
jug, into which she sobbed bitterly, to sus-
picious premises which happened to grow’ up
in the vicinity. After being shaken till her
chains rattled and her patrician but vacant
countenance flew in all directions, she was
solemnly warned of the penalty of rebellion
and locked up with only her spelling bust
for company. Her request for* praying-
materials only resulted in her being slapped
and deprived of her lunch by the aquiline
female. Though somewhat heated by per-
cussion, she was glad to know that the story
had commenced.
In order to provide her with exercise a
long road was laid out and covered with the
blue-metal of tribulation, and on this she
walked in her bare feet at intervals for a
varying number of years according to the
size of the bo£k. Sometimes she was har-
nessed to a cart containing plunder, and
took it to a receiver with a fatal nose. The
road was' lined with entanglements, and
large quantities of explosive machinations
were buried in the neighborhood. The tall
young man whose family had nothing left
but the equity of redemption of the bode
stud began to take notice of her and lo
holler to her across the entanglements. But
out of loyalty to her uncle, whom she knew
w r as no relative at all, and to the aquiline
female who shook her till her beautiful but
unintelligent countenance flew in all direc-
tions, she always assured him that she was
happy and driving in her own motor-car,
and that she didn’t really know what to do
with her money. Which was true, for she
hadn’t any.
The tall young man spent most of his
time digging for a job at the foot of the
family tree. It was a sandy soil with no
auriferous indications whatever. To divert
his attention a bottomless pit was dug by
evil-minded parties, and the edges of it were
buttered, and a notice was put up to the
effect that there was a high-salaried position
at the bottom. Naturally he fell in at the
first sight of the notice. Then the board
was removed lest the hole should become
crowded with applicants.
It was. debated whether the lady should be
thrown in also, as her feet were getting
worn out after three years’ service, and her
haulage power with the cart of plunder had
diminished. But it was decided that she
was good for a while longer. Points in her
favor were that she was well broken in,
obedient, uncommunicative, pious and filled
with a spirit of dignified resignation, which
saved something in the way of victuals. She
would stand untied, and showed no signs of
bolting so long as her moral principles
weren’t interfered with. Also, she was tidy,
and answered to almost any name. The con-
clusion arrived at was that when she was
quite used up she could be boiled down to
prevent the secret leaking out, in which case
her master and mistress 'might be run in.
By this time the neighborhood was black
with lurking detectives, who suspected that
something had been up for several years,
but thought it was influenza. The discovery
of sundry bodies, and a wireless apparatus,
with a German accent, and large quantities
of bogus notes, confirmed them in this belief.
Many of them settled down in the locality,
and married, and brought up families of
young detectives, who practised lurking after
school.
The hero fell right through the hole, and
came out at the other end, where he was
arrested for trespass. Ultimately he worked
his passage home as assistant cabin-boy. His
idea was that he had passed the lucrative
billet in the hole through falling too fast,
and he proposed to have another try at it,
using a rope this time and taking testi-
monials with him.
Just then the whole conspiracy blew up
through somebody, who was never in the
story before dropping a hot cigarette among
the explosive machinations. The roar was
heard feet away. Nothing was found of the
uncle and the aquiline woman except a torn
coi’set. The atmosphere gradually cleared
of debris, leaving the landscape ready for
future use. And a new book began as soon
as the unresisting victim was selected and
enslaved out of numerous applicants.
JAMES EDMOND.
The Doc. in Egypt
“In the following pages,” the introduction
runs, “we have set out the problems which
faced the A.A.M.C. in Egypt....the necessi-
ties which forced one 520-bed hospital to
expand to a capacity of approximately
10,500 beds, and the manner in which the
work was done”; and the writers, Dr. J. W.
Barrett and Lieut. P. E. Deane, add that
“the officer commanding, Lieut.-Col. Ramsay
Smith, was responsible for a development
probably unequalled in the history of medi-
cine.” Dr. Ramsay Smith will not require
introduction to South Aus., at any rate.
When, in Kingston’s day in State politics,
there was a medical insurrection at the
Adelaide Hospital, two senior men were
brought from Britain to take charge. Ram-
say Smith was one of them. Kingston
picked his men well.
• During the first 10 days of the crisis
approximately 16,000 wounded men were
poured into Egypt—poured in without warn-
ing. Prodigies were done in those crowded
hours. It was no time to call up Melbourne
for authority to buy a bed or so. Things
were done and authority was sought after-
wards. Building after building was taken
°ver and hospitals extemporised. Over
10,000 beds were managed for a time by a
staff originally intended to handle 520.
Yet the organisation of the Australian
Defence Department on the medical side
was wretchedly poor. It could hardly
be otherwise. Few front-rank men had
had anything to do with the military
forces. A few zealous patriots had
attached, themselves; but for the most part
the medical officers of the Defence Depart-
ment were either old Army surgeons or
shallow men who loved to strut at social
functions in a uniform. When Dr Barrett
joined No. 1 General Hospital he “was in-
formed that specialists were not required.”
The first patient admitted was an eye case
there were from 60 to 100 in-patients
calling for the services of eye-specialists, and
1 0 out-patients. Yet “specialists were not

were Used on these Buildings.
The illustration is from an unfinished photograph of the
huge Wool Storage Sheds, erected recently in Wentworth
Park, Sydney, by Messrs. Stuart Brothers, for the Central
Wool Storage Committee.
These buildings were erected in record time, and the roofing
used on them constituted the largest order ever placed in
Australia for roofing to be used on one contract.
It speaks well for the superiority of
“ADAMAX”
AspHaltum Roofmg
for such orders to come our way. Certainly exhaustive tests
were first made, and it was only after “ADAMAX” had been
subjected to rigorous tests that it was proved to be a fire-
retardant roofing that resists all the ravages of the elements—
wind, heat, rain, cold, gases, acids, chemical fumes, etc.—
none of which have any damaging effect on “ADAMAX”
ROOFING.

with a corresponding saving in freight. Easy to lay, and

MERIT
In Fountain Pens, as in everything
else, it is merit that tells, and that is
why the “ Swan ” has for a quarter
of a century gained more users every
year. When you buy a “Swan”
you know you have a guarantee of

VARIUSE VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Beauty’s End.
Just like a beautiful pansy
Fluttering to and fro,
A butterfly came to my window
All in the noontide glow—
Oh, the delicate Fancy
That shaped and colored it so!
Flowers stood still in the garden;
Here was a winghd flower,
A sister-tking to the pansies
That die dewy hour.
Craving nor pity nor pardon
From Death in his moment of power.
Stern is dark Death in his duty ;
Grim, impassive and cold,
He takes as his own grey tresses
And tresses alight with gold.
Thus, in the end, comes Beauty
To sleep in the moist grave-mould.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
An Old Tasmanian Road.
It comes from days of agony and shame,
This old Tasmanian road,
When men the souls of other men might
tame
With stinging lash and goad.
I see them at their task, those convicts grey;
I hear a clanking chain
That fetters man to man along the way
Of toil and biting pain.
On hills where yellow-lichened she-oaks grow
The road winds up and down
To cross a stream, whose waters flash and
flow
Beside a ruined town.
Grey fences by the roadway fall and rot,
Gates, wind-swung, creak and groan;
By unmown lawns bleak homesteads, long
forgot,
Show fronts of crumbling stone.
And in the town are moss-grown tumbling
walls
That lapse round mouldered cells,
Where, memory-born, a brooding shadow
falls.
And of dark horror tells.
But lanes of alien oak and burgeoning elm
With summer’s kiss are green ;
Fair from forsaken gardens flowers o’er-
whelm
The shame that once has been.
And all the town is held by quietness;
No more the lash and goad
Drive broken men with aching feet to press
This old Tasmanian road.
So Time, the craftsman, weaves a mingled
tale
Along the changing years;
The old dark ways of shame in beauty fail,
Flowers bloom o’er olden tears.
TASMANIA.
O’PHIMERTY.
Evening.
The weary Day shakes down her burnished
hair,
Slips out each sunlight pin, each band of
pearl
Vapor unwinds; then flings aside her robes
Of gold airs girt with sapphire. Anon, wrapt
In neglige of amethystine haze.
Reclined upon a cushion of bright cloud,
With breezes light she combs her streaming
locks,
Bending her head to let the purple dusk
That in its fragrance hides, gloom all the
world;
Till, tired at length, she twists each dewy
tress
Into the white curl-papers of the stars.
N.S.W.
HELEN BURKE.
Out with Sally.
Sally’s hands are cool and quiet,
On the wheel that guides our going
Down the roads where roses riot
And the winds of song are blowing.
Road-songs rowdy, tough and hoary,
Love-songs with a tender rally—
Life is like a splendid story
Driving overland with Sally!
Thoughts that link themselves with sorrow,
Dreams wherein the sunlight sullies,
Haste away and swiftly borrow
Laughter from the singing gullies,
Then pursue, by zest made agile,
While, to make the pace the greater,
Sally’s shoes, like snowflakes fragile,
Touch the light accelerator.
We have flung aside earth’s bondage.
Stirring winds kiss waters dreaming.
On the sighing, swaying frondage
Golden-hearted sunlight’s beaming.
Sally’s thoughts are truly sunbeams.
Scorning deeps where doubts might dally,
And a laughing gleam of fun beams
Often in the eyes of Sally.
Sally’s eyes are like grey roses,
Warm and soft, with light, clear shadows,
As when Day her window closes
Over twilit shining meadows,
While the songs of bird and locust
Die away to fitful slumber—
On the road her eyes are focussed,
Lest a “trap” might take her number!
Sally’s lips—the scent of flowers
Floats across from some rich garden,
Where the slow bees court the hours.
Taking sweets nor seeking pardon!
Sally’s lips—l hear them saying
That this day is “not too bally”;
Through Elysian scenes I’m swaying,
Driven mad for love of Sally! ‘
MAOBILAND.
WILL LAWSON.

THURSDAY, MARCH 20, 1919.
THE MENACE OF A “DRY”
AUSTRALIA.
A recent message from New York stated
that the American Anti-saloon League was
“opening offices in- Melbourne, as well as Lon-
don and other European capitals, in order to
conduct a world fight against the drink traf-
fic.” About the same time a Miss Stafford
Miller arrived in Westralia from the
’U.S.A., and, after three days’ investi-
gation, stated that “Perth was the
most drunken city in the world, and
reeking with sin and infamy.” An
example of cause and effect. The anti-
liquor leagues exist to support people like
Miss Miller, and it is Miss Miller’s job
to supply them with scandalous discoveries.
If there were no case against the Drink trade
in Australia it could afford to smile. But
there notoriously is a case. The street
drunk is a feature of every big city and town
at certain hours. Crimes committed while
mnder “the influence” are frequent; and the
drink habit is responsible for a good deal
of economic loss. The anti-liquor reviv-
alists are helped by the fact that the evil
which the trade does is necessarily w T ell
advertised. Taking one thing with another
it looks as though the drink manufacturer
and drink-retailer may very soon have to
answer the question : “Will you get to work
and help make the trade decent, or do you
prefer to have it reformed out of existence?”
* * * *
America is in a favorable position to send
out missionaries of abolition. There is only
one wine-growing State in the Union —Cali-
fornia —and it produces a low-grade, article
at the best. American whisky is so poor
that most Yankee connoisseurs prefer to
pay a high price for Scotch. And there is
little sale outside the U.S. for American
beer In Britain the brewing and distilling
industries are of great importance. _ France,
in normal times, produces 1,100,000,000
gallons of wine, and without the wine-
indnstrv it would drop to the status
0 f a second- or even third-class Power.
Italy and Portugal are relatively in a
similar position. Australia is in its
infancy as a wine-producing country.
But its sun and its soil make it an ideal
place for growing the wine grape. Hocks
and clarets are already being manufactured
of a high class even when judged by Euro-
pean standards; and there is a big demand,
both at home and abroad, for Australian
champagnes. There seems nothing to stop
Australia being, in a comparatively short
time, the greatest wine-producer the world
has known —nothing but combined mulish-
ness and selfishness on the part of a number
of liquor retailers. America is listed at
present to go dry on January 16 next.. It
may or it may not. The statutory majority
of three-fourths of the States was obtained
not by referendum, but in the State Legis-
latures. That is to say, there was no dif-
ference between the voting strength of little
Maine, which has been dry for 30 years; of
Nevada, which has only 80,000 inhabitants:
and of New York, with its overwhelmingly
“wet” population of 9,000.000. California,
where a referendum was taken, voted “wet”;
its Legislature voted “dry.” These facts
may explain a vague cable message last week
stating that “the American Judiciary (Com-
mittee has decided that abolition will not
come into operation on the date originally
fixed.” But in any case, and notwithstand-
ing the difference between the two countries
indicated above, one of the main argu-
ments of the prohibitionists here will evi-
dently be a grand gesture in the direction of
theoretically bone-dry Amei’ica, coupled with
an insistence on the drink-sodden plight of
the Commonwealth. What is to be done
to avert the stampede?
* * * *
One of the most unedifying features of
Australian social life is the mean pub. It
provides the wowser with 80 per cent, of his
arguments. It is one of perhaps half a
dozen in a limited radius, and so competition
is fierce. The customer, however inebri-
ated, continues to be served so long as ne
has the necessary cash. The drink is as
bad as the retailer cares to make it. This
is because there is only one State —West-
ralia, where Miss Stafford Miller made
her discoveries —in which a system of official
inspection of alcoholic liquors for quality
exists. In N. S. Wales, for example, the
deadliest firewater may be vended. The
only occasion on which the seller falls in is
when he sells drink that is not alcoholic
enough. It is for this reason that some of
the wildest drunks are accomplished in appa-
rently respectable bars. It rests with
individual publicans whether they sell stuff
that is good, or poisonous; whether the
whisky in the bottle is as alleged on the
label, or crude rubbish. The old-fashioned
and insanitary boozing-den, the dive where
relays of men spit and drink six days a
week, the resort which 50 per cent, of them
are ashamed to be seen entering or leaving,
is a calamity not only to the public but to
the trade. And so are many of the more
ornate establishments, which in an elabo-
rately magnificent -manner serve spirits as
bad as any that are to be found in the
slums.
Wherever local option is there also
must be the mean and dangerous pub
in large numbers. There is only one in-
strument that will wipe out these places in
a way at once drastic and equitable—an
impartial body of experts sitting with a
fixed ideal in view. Any State in which
the sale of over-proof alcohol is legal, or in
which there is no check on the class of
drink, is bound to have a large tally of
drunks, even though everv liquor-shou be a
palace. Nothing will stop drunkenness
altogether, neither a drastic limitation of
pubs nor a rigid examination of all liquor
sold. The best that can be done, human
nature being w'hat it is, is to minimise the
trouble. There is no evidence that prohibi-
tion will do it. The Australian tempera-
ment is particularly unsuited to restrictive
control of this sort. Already, as an out-
come of such restrictions as exist in the dif-
ferent States, there are sly-grog shops in
plenty. They mostly sell a more or less
innocuous type of beer. With prohibition
there would be many more of them, and beer
would be barred owing to its bulk. Spirits
would be the line. There are 20 nips in
a bottle of whisky and six bottles go to the
gallon, which means that in a quarter-cask
(35 gallons) there would be 4200 drinks.
A quarter-cask is not a difficult article to
smuggle, and when filled with raw spirits
it wrnuld be capable of a wonderful amount
of damage. What is the alternative to pro-
hibition and its disastrous effects on the legi-
timate enjoyment of the people, on an indus-
try with vast possibilities, and on the crea-
tion of a new set of crimes? A measure on
the lines of a bill which was introduced in
the House of Lords last week should show
good results here. Its aim is “the trans-
formation of public houses into real refresh-
ment houses; the licenses for improved
mibh'c houses to include permission for
dancing, musical and other entertainments
on the premises.” There is no reason in
the w T orld why any hotel should not be as
reputable as any concert-hall or theatre.
As things are there are so many hotels, pubs
and bodegas which are infamous that the
public is beginning to take notice. The
electors, skilfully handled, might well decide
to sweep them, and with them the legal
manufacture and sale of all alcohol, out of
existence —just as a man might decide,
illogically, to burn down his house because
the drains were out of order. The trade
will only have itself to blame if the thing
is done.
COAL AND CHAOS.
The coal-mining fraternity in the State
which controls the situation shows signs of
arming for a general war — a hold-up of the
entire continent —to last till certain demands
not yet fully explained are granted. By
way of preparation the output is being
reduced in various mines under the “darg”
system, which prohibits any man doing more
than so much work and earning so much
money in a day. The object is to prevent
the accumulation of reserves of coal —unless
they are imported—to keep things going
when the output ceases. If there are no
stocks of coal whatsoever, industry will stop
in a day, and calamity will arrive in two
days, and civilisation will begin to totter m
a week. If revolution is aimed at there
will be as the nucleus of a force 20,000 coal-
miners with nothing to do but revolushe,
and all the other unemployed (almost every-
body being unemployed) and the miscel-
laneous elements of humanity to recruit
from. Barring imports of coal to keep the
wheels turning it should prove the same
easy matter as besieging a city which
omitted to lay in one square meal in ad-
vance.
* * * *
The situation is wholly different from that
which existed during the great silver-mining
and shearing and maritime and miscellaneous
strikes of 1890, or the shearers’ strike of
1894, or the famous Victorian railway strike.
When they happened Labor had never been
in power to put things right. But in N. 8.
Wales, the State which controls the coal
business and which (barring imports from
Japan or elsewhere) can hold up the con-
tinent, Labor was in power for six years
and would be there yet if it hadn’t thrown
overboard its old leaders for not being suffi-
ciently pro-Hun. The great, obvious, over-
whelming industrial job, over w'hieh Labor
had been cogitating during 19 years of Oppo-
sition, was to put things right in the coal-
mining industry. In ordinary years coal
is responsible for half or more than half the
industrial troubles, so it was obviously the
business to be attended to. Apparently the
party, after all the 19 years’ rumination,
didn’t know what its wrongs were, if it had
any, or didn’t know how to put them right.
Anyhow, it lived for six years in the old
coaly chaos and left the old chaos behind it.
A great deal has been said, and something is
still being said, about the nationalisation of
the coal-mines —the same thing that was
done in N. S. Wales and Victoria with the
original private-enterprise railways. The
scheme has much to recommend it, but
Labor, when in full authority, jibbed at it.
Now there is a party which repudiates the
idea of national ownership—ownership by
producers and consumers alike. It demands
ownership by a section of the producers only.
The men who opened the mines, and made
the machinery, and supplied the railways and
roads and ships for carrying purposes are
to be excluded, and everything is to belong
to the men who dig out the mineral from
holes already in operation. Therefore,
unless Australia took to importing coal, the
entire community would be controlled by a
much smaller body of men than the nobility
which once controlled old France. They can
answer for themselves whether they con-
sider that democratic.
No attempt has ever been made to create
co-operative coal-getting, either by the
miners’ union opening up new mines with
its own funds or by the same body buying
up the often-depreciated shares of existing
mines and acquiring control in that way. It
should pay better to spend money on indus-
trial production if the leaders know any-
thing about the management of industry,
than on industrial war and on keeping the
conscripted army of unionism always on a
war footing. But though the leaders talk
greatly about “taking over” industries they
haven’t built up a single one —barring a few
newspapers which mostly live by conscript-
ing subscribers among the unions—in order
to gain valuable’ experience and show how
the work should be done. Though they give
any amount of warning to the “capitalist”
not to create new industries because the day
of confiscation is at hand, they create none
themselves. They are as disappointing as
the Parliamentary Labor party was when,
having full authority to nationalise the
mines, they made no attempt to do it. If
Labor, during its long term of office in the
coal State and its terms of Federal office,
had put the most troubled industry on a
sound basis, according to its own ideas, Aus-
tralia would have acquiesced. It could do
nothing else, whether the scheme was re-
organisation, State purchase of the mines, or
tidy and orderly confiscation'. If the Parlia-
mentary Labor party explained clearly and
definitely even now how it is going
to put things right when it is next in
power, Australia would still have to acqui-
esce., But the obvious preparations for
a big strike suggest that the men who are
running the show don’t exactly know what
they want, and' propose to make a hash
of civilisation in order to find out their
own minds. The possibility that that part
of civilisation wffiich isn’t engaged in coal-
digging may object to be hashed under such
conditions explains why this country may
have a spell of living on foreign coal hewn
probably by brown men and brought prob-
ably in brown ships.
THE ALMOST EXHAUSTED MINE.
Nearly 60 years ago the gold yield of N.
S. Wales was four times larger than it is
now. And it w T as shallow gold easily and
cheaply got, and there was only one-sixth
the population to divide it among, and it
wasn’t swallowed up in paying interest
on foreign debts, as it is now, and 9Gi per
cent, of the area of the State was still to be
sold, and the world was young and wide, and
it was spring everywhere. Then the Gov-
ernment took the heroic measure of offering
practically the whole surface of the State
in comfortable blocks at £1 per acre. There
were details and exceptions, of course, but
the £1 per acre was a leading feature to
commence with, and it remained when many
other things changed. Land almost in
sight of Sydney came under this offer, and
as late as 20 or 25 years ago remnants were
still available within a few days’ drive —
horse-drive, not motor—from the big city.
People early recognised that there was
money in the business, and later they
realised that there was more money
in it than they had dreamed of. The
small price—for good convenient land
it was very small, and there was plenty of
such land at first—hadn’t e®:n to be paid in
one sum. The payment was spread over 30
years or more, and the interest charged on
unpaid instalments was 2) per cent., which
was hardly worth rnent oning. The one
drawback was the imprisonment clause.
Residence on the properly was compulsory
for a number of years lest population should
escape, and there were painfully elaborate
methods to prevent hasty sale to the accu-
mulator of big estates. The simple device
of striking the accumulator with a graduated
tax and then letting him do his worst was
an idea of later date. Mere boys were
allowed to select, and they married almost
any kind of girl in haste to break the loneli-
ness, so the marriage rate was high.
* * * *
For a comparatively short time things
boomed. The value of the land sold at fl
per acre—really about 10s. or 12s. per acre
if allowance is made for the fact that the
State practically lent the money to buy it
with fob a long term of years at half the
usual rate of interest —was pushed up by the
construction of railways and other works out
of loans. The interest on these was charged
to posterity, ana until land and income taxes
and local government rates were invented
very little of the charge fell on the settler’s
posterity. The State felt that it was on a
good wicket, for it was getting fl an acre
for what cost it nothing, though it had to
wait a long time for the money. The
original selector wasn’t on a very bad_wicket.
There are places as much as 200 or 250 miles
from Sydney where land apart from Jill im-
provements is worth £6 per acre, yet it was
taken up so recently that the term of 30
years or so over which the instalments have
to run hasn’t nearly expired, though the
period of compulsory imprisonment on the
land has run out. That is to say, the State
or seller has got fl per acre or part of it,
while the buyer has got £5 in addition to
his earnings. A multitude of purely agri-
cultural towns sprang up in the early days,
before the settlers reached the point at which
they were able to sell their selections and
clear out with the unearned increment to the
picture-shows of Sydney; and the money
spent on building the loan railways that
helped to create the increment was of ma-
terial assistance in the soringing up. Many
of these agricultural towns were really pros-
perous for a while, though they haven’t done
very much of late. A distinct change set
in when the best land was all sold and the
settlement boom was exhausted and the
settler, who had been tied to his gum-tree in
the terms of his contract, escaped by effluxion
of time. Last census showed that 53
counties in N. S. Wales were losing popula-
tion, and 15 of them were in the small east-
ern. or coastal or well-watered district where
settlement started first and the selector
escaped first. At the same time 56 munici-
palities of the 152 outside Sydney and
its suburbs were on the perishing list, a few
because local mines had given out, but most
because it is a poor busineess to be a purely
agricultural town when agriculture is in a
bad way.
So after half-a-century’s run the land-
mine is practically exhausted. There is
very little State land left, outside of the
drought country, to sell to the selector at
fl per acre, and that little is hardly worth
having. The land which was bought at fl
and sold in not very many years at £6 or
fS apart from improvements isn’t likely to
sell again in a comparatively short space of
time at £l6 or £32. The process by which
the buyer multiplied his capital by simply
existing has brought up short against the
fact that the price is now about all that the
earning capacity of the soil will stand. The
State has spent most of the £1 per acre that
it received when it sold cheaply the land that
cost it nothing, and is scratching around for
revenue to replace the easy land-sale money.
The original buyer can’t be tied down to the
soil a second time under pain of forfeiture,
so that chapter of the book of settlement and
development is nearly finished. In a
general way the change is from a time in
which the land bore two crops, one of food
and the other of increased selling value, to
a time when it bears a crop of food only, and
the food has to pay taxes which the early
selector with the unearned increment coming
in never dreamt of; and the market, through
the decay of inland towns, has got much
further away. In fact, the Ma State begins
to get down to plain agricultural business,
the safe and pleasant gamble which used to
back up the business having s*en its best
days. If Australia was a country where
industrial towns had a habit of springing up
here and there, so that the lonesome wheat-
patch might have a chance to become a
suburb, and a promising market might rise
up any day at the most despondent farmer’s
door, the unearned increment would have
another run for its money. But the few
big cities allow nothing to spring up except
themselves.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Repatriation Collapse.
Senator Millen. Minister in charge of
Repatriation, tried lately to put a good face
oh a very gloomy situation by saying that
70,000 soldiers have already come back, and
of these only 3 per cent. (2100) are out of
employment. This means that he knows of
that number —-in other words, that number
have reported ‘themselves as being in want
of employment. But a great many of the
returned soldiers are cripples, and are not
wanting employment because they aren’t fit
for it. That was why they were returned.
Now for the first time a vast number of
healthy soldiers who will simply howl for
employment are coming. The small and
partially disabled lot dribbled in during
several years. The big healthy lot is due
during several months. Then the early re-
turns often got johs by taking the places of
new soldiers who were leaving. There are
no new soldiers leaving now to make vacan-
cies. Again, a considerable number of the
men Millen talks about aren’t working in
the ordinary sense of the term. They are
living on sustenance allowances and learning
trades, and they will be wanting real work
later. Further, these early men were pro-
vided for in a sort of way while the floating
of stupendous paper-money war-loans for
local consumption was creatine- a temporary
boom, which makes another difference. And
—what is as important as anything —they


were provided for in a sort of way befoie
the amazing increase of imports came along,
under the miserably inefficient tariff, to make
local industry more superfluous and more
impossible. It was a common gag of the
Freetrade and importing party that the re-
duction of imports through the purely tem-
porary ship-famine in war time was I rotec-
tion in itself. That was true ; yet though the
bulk of goods imported was less, the cost
didn’t diminish much, for this helpless im-
porting country is at the mercy of the
profiteers of all nations. And even this
nominal Protection practically vanished long
before the armistice. As soon as the Hun
power was on the down-grade, the seas
became a little safer and goods began to
pour in. Here are the official figures:
Imports op Merchandise Before the War.
Year to Dec., 1912.. £76,483,360
„ „ 1913.. 78,196,109
During the Heat of War.
Year to June, 1915.. £63,563,781
„ 1916.. 76,740,899
„ 1917.. 75,354,890
„ ~ 1918.. 60,363,144
During tub Declinh op War.
Quarter to Sept., 1918.. £23,636,998
Or at the rate of £94,547,992 per year.
The Australian manufacturer was facing the
constant rise in wages and in the pr&6 of
materials, and the War-time Profits Tax,
which has been extended far ahead into
peace-time. The amendment of the tariff
against foreign cheap labor, which tariff is
so largely just free list, is postponed till the
general resurrection of the dead, as usual.
But the importer got in early and flooded
the country with more than double the quan-
tity of goods which he sent during the first
quarter of the previous year. The signs
are that there will be £34,000,000 worth less
work to do than last year, and probably
100,000 more able-bodied men wanting work,
with crowds to follow. A sufficient amount
of this sort of thing leads to trouble the
sort of wild, unreasoning trouble made by
men with no prospects and a bitter sense of
injury. And if the dawdlers in office don t
get a move on with the tariff the New Year s
Day of 1920 may see some big Australian
cities in trouble, and law-and-order running
for its life. There are plenty of leaders to
preach trouble, and Watt and Millen are
providing the followers.
A Lost Chance at Adelaide.
Forty-four carpenters were fined £5 each
the other dav at Adelaide for an illegal
strike. In two other cases there was a post-
ponement. They didn’t so much strike, they
alleged, as leave simultaneously without
notice in search of better jobs. Said the
hired man of law :
The men had considered the establishment of a
co-operative carpentry and joinery shop, but the
financial problem had deterred them from going on
with the scheme.
Yet there is hardly a doubt that they pos-
sessed the capital. In South Australia the
average savings-bank deposits are £29 7s. 3d.
per inhabitant, or £ll7 9s. for every family
of four —father, mother and two children,
which is about the smallest number possible
to prevent the population decreasing. These
46 adult males, who being skilled tradesmen
were probably no poorer than the average
clients of the banks of the comparatively
poor, might be assumed to command or be
able to influence £5403 in savings-bank cash
alone, in addition to whatever they possessed
in the way of houses, land, furniture, jewel-
lery, loose change, life-insurance policies,
building-society investments and sundries.
To start a complete, full-blown factory, in
which the bosses own (not rent) the land,
buildings and machinery, and have no debt
or mortgage on them whatsoever, costs £268
per worker. These figures are arrived at by
taking the value of lands, buildings, machin-
ery and plant of every sort employed in the
15,010 factories registered at the latest re-
turns, and dividing it by the number of
employees, boys and girls included. (This
list doesn’t include the vast number of tiny
“factories,” of which two or three can be
found in a street in any working-man’s dis-
trict, in which only liand-power is employed.
If they were taken in, the average amount
required per man would be less, but they
have no direct bearing on the case.) Land
and buildings stand for half the cost, so if
a firm of beginners is satisfied to rent them
or buy them on long terms the amount re-
quired is about £134 per man; and if the
new firm gets part credit for its plant, as is
the rule rather than the exception, the need
for capital is still less. Therefore it is im-
possible to believe that the 44 artisans who
were fined and two more who weren’t fined
were unable to produce the money required
so that they might have eliminated the capi-
talist and secured the full product of their
labor. Probably they felt that the business
would be a mess-up and a certain loss
because no one would obey the elected boss,
and too much time would be wasted in com-
mittee of the whole House. So they clung
to their savings, and went out to look for
more chances to be “wage-slaves.” Not once
has the One Big Union crowd, with all its
demands that the workshops should belong
to the workers only, attempted the establish-
ment of sample workshops to show how the
thing is done and to paralyse the world with
their success. The railways, owned by the
people on communistic principles, keep
. going because the Commissioners have a
definite standing instead of being chosen
from day to day by “stop work” meetings of
all hands and the cook. Their management,
like that of the Commonwealth Bank, is
denounced by the One Big Unionists on the
plea that the lack of “stop work” meetings,
and election of officers by soap-box oratory,
and the constant friendly consultations in
which the smallest employee is suffocated or
trodden to death in the crowd, makes them
“undemocratic.” They will probably con-
tinue that way.
Hours of Work.
The National Industrial Conference Board,
an American institution with headquarters
at Boston, is engaged in a scientific analysis
of the facts bearing upon the hours-of-
work problem “The investigation is not
made,” it is declared, “with the intention of
attacking or defending a workday of any
specific length, but, instead, to assemble the
available results of actual experience.” The
latest report to hand, dealing with the manu-
facture of woollens, is, to say the least of it,
disconcerting. Practically all previous
investigation had gone to prove that an
increased output followed a reduction of
hours. The American investigation suggests
that, in this industry in the U.S.A. at any
rate, the conclusion must be qualified. For
the most part the factories work 54 hours
per week, and 68 establishments now on
that schedule, but which previously worked
longer hours, have furnished comparisons of
the result of the change so far as output is
concerned. Only six of them show an in-
creased output; seven others show that the
output was maintained; the rest show de-
creased output. Factories employing over
half (50.6 p.c.) of the workpeople reported
a decrease about proportional to the de-
crease in hours worked; others employing
7 p.c. reported a decrease greater than pro-
portional. The most complete data came from
a large establishment which reduced the
working time by 9.3 p.c. It showed an
astonishing decrease of 13.8 p.c. in total
output. The industry is carried on partly
by time- and partly by piece-hands. Almost
invariably no increase in the per-hour out-
put of time-hands followed the reduction;
but in most instances there was an increase,
much or little, in the per-hour output of niece-
workers. The results of course are not con-
clusive. Data relating to one industry
cannot be taken as reflecting the facts of
all industries. At the same time they must
be taken into account in arriving at general
conclusions. The figures, moreover, are the.
most modern of all on the subject. There
certainly was a time, unless all previous
investigations resulted in a wrong verdict,
when a reduction of hours was followed by
an increased output. That obviously meant
speeding-up. And the persistent attacks
on speeding-up may have had the effect of
preventing workers from responding with
increased energy to a reduction of hours. In
some cases, unless the statistics are all
wrong, it appears to have led them in the
other direction. If so, it is to be deeply
regretted.
A Season of Bolshevism.
“J.N.P.” from Tokyo, Japan:—
Truly ghastly reports are reaching us from trust-
worthy observers now in Siberia of the terrible
social conditions in the western provinces, the result
of Bolshevik military successes and the fleeing of
whole communities eastwards before the advancing
terror.
It is of little profit to dwell on the accumulating
horrors suggested by reports constantly arriving, or
on the painful divisions among the Allies that result
in huge quantities of stores of all descriptions, so
badly needed, being held up at Vladivostoclc and
other centres, because authorities cannot agree.
Appeals now being made may set the railways run-
ning and oil the clogging machinery of international
“control.” But we may, with some profit, look over
the Bolshevik record, and try to discover if, amid the
carnage, social disruption and misery brought upon
the Russian people, there is anything that is good
in the Bolshevik idea, which so captivated the public
mind in two hemispheres a year and a half ago. .
It was on November 6, 1917, that the Bolsheviki
seized the supreme power in Petrograd, and Kerensky
was deposed. The historian of the future will have a
hard task in any attempt to describe the life of
Russia in those first days of national anarchy, but
he will tell us that the country immediately broke
up into many separate States, each proclaiming in-
dependence of its neighbor, and each announcing to
all the world that it was “free.” A period of bloody
internecine strife ensued. Russians fought Russians
on many fronts. From October, 19D> to April
following there were no less than 34 “fronts” on
Russian territory. In this period 37 so-called Soviet
republics were established.
This local warfare was conducted with a much
more fierce and barbarous zeal than w r as that with
the Central Powers; and especially vindictive was
the destruction of property. The financial corres-
pondent of the Russkoe Slovo estimated Aat the
economic loss caused by this civil .war far exceeded
Russia’s losses in the war with Germany.
It was shortly after this period of conflict that
many foreign travellers, mostly American, arrived in
Japan .with stories of the ruin of factories and mines
and other property in which they were interested, of
a character hardly credible, enormities that were
supposed at that early day to have been much orna-
mented by a rich imagination. But the facts as
since gathered suggest that anything was possible
during the delirium that then had possession of the
Slav mentality.
The people, in their fury, saw a particular enemy
in the railway. The Bolshevik economist, Juri
Larin, author of most of Lenin’s decrees, affirms that
during demobilisation 4000 waggons and 700 loco-
motives were destroyed; in the four months ending
February, 1918, a hundred and nine large estates
were pillaged—and estates in Russia are frequently
as large as an English county. Industry was para-
lysed—hut what do we mean by that common
phrase? The output of coal in the Donetz district
fell from two thousand million poods in 1916 to
half a million in 1917. Consider what sort of a
paralysis that would cause in a country where
artificial heat is essential to life, not to speak of
coal’s uses for other purposes. The output of iron
fell to one quarter; the production of naphtha
ceased. In two months, in the Moscow industrial
district, centre of the textile industry, 80 factories
closed down and a hundred thousand workmen joined
the mobs.
In places, during the civil fighting, the Bolsheviki
sometimes got the worst of it; but they never failed
in abandoning a town to take with them all avail-
able cash. Retreating from Kazan, they carried with
them gold to the value of 650 million roubles; in
Chita 300 poods of gold were carried away. The
Bolsheviki are never at a loss when running short of
the needful —plants for turning out millions of notes
were at work in Petrograd, Moscow, Toula, Orel and
Mogilev. In addition to this every Soviet repub-
lic issued its own currency. In Siberia alone there
are four different kinds of currency, and people in
happier countries have not the faintest idea of the
difficulties to which this fact gives rise. A few
examples: in the Amur district there are Muchin
notes; in Harbarovsk, Ivrasnostchekov notes; in the
Trans-Baikal, Mlatoboytzy notes. In two months
the Bolsheviki issued more paper money than the
Kerensky Government during its whole existence
of 18 months. Further, the Bolsheviki took a leaf
out of Germany’s book and levied contributions on
towns. In two months —March and April, 1918—115
towns were .made to pay an aggregate contribution
of 690,325,000 roubles, say £70,000,000.
The destruction of public monuments was early a
favorite form of diversion among the newly-free
men; another kind of monument, the newspaper
press, has been ruined, and only Bolshevik organs
remain. Railways are still disorganised; and rolling
stock is in the last stage of decrepitude. Industry
is paralysed. Nor is there any light showing ahead.
The country is not “settling down.” As might be
expected, in view of the activity of the note-print-
ing press, nominal prices are appallingly high. The
only cheap thing in the country is human life.
Noah’s Old Line of Business.
The building of wooden ships in Australia
for the Commonwealth Government has been
abandoned at a loss. The arks should have
been thrown together, and, having earned
400 per cent, profit while the war-freights
lasted, been about ready for the knacker by
now. But Hughes wrangled and wrangled,
so the Government had hardly got the first
one begun when the chance passed. The
first of the Government steel ships isn’t
launched yet, and they grow obsolete while
they are missing the ’bus. Now it is seri-
ously urged that the Commonwealth should
sell its bob-tailed fleet of purchased ships
and go right out of the business. The
vessels are a poor lot, though they paid
splendidly while the war lasted ; in fact, they
far more than paid their cost, which shows
what chances were missed. They aren’t
likely to do very well when affairs _ settle
down, and Australia must either decide to
build first-class vessels and do things on a
big scale, or fall back into the old depen-
dence on foreign private enterprise. Or, as
a third alternative, it might put such casr-
iron restrictions on the use of outside ships
and on the sale of Australian shipping
interests outside the country as to create a
big native private enterprise which would
spend its earnings here. Otherwise Aus-
tralia will in time own nothing that floats
except a few coasters and ferry-boats.
There are fine vessels on the coast which
are supposed to be Australian and some that
are registered here, but a larger and larger
proportion are owned in England. Regis-
tration means very little—a vessel may be
owned in Brazil and registered in Sweden if
it suits somebody’s convenience. This
country wants to really own ships, and this
isn’t a time for any Government to sell out.
unless it is the kind that hates to be found
working.

It Is All So Simple.
Interesting letter from Adelaide: —
Dear Bulletin, —The various scribes who have dealt
with the One Big Union scheme through your columns
should not be allowed to wander at large, as they
are doing at present; and in case your readers may
be led astray by their insane scribblings about the
methods and objective of the One Big Union, allow
me to say a few words. The One Big Union is cer-
tainly out to abolish the National Debt, but not in
the insane manner imagined. It will be done this
way. When the workers are organised into One Big
Union, the next act will be to reduce the hours of
toil, so that there will be a limited production,
leaving no profit at all for the boss. When the boss
gets no profit from his factory, or whatever the con-
cern may be, he won’t want to own it; consequently
it will fall into the hands of the workers, who will
use it to p#oduce sufficient only for themselves —no
more, no less. No interest can then be paid on
war bonds and' other loans, because there will be no
surplus. The workers will get all they produce, and
all who don’t work will get nothing. All the inter-
est on the War Debt and other debts is paid out
of Surplus Value. The workers in the One Big
'Union will simply refuse to produce any Surplus
Value, and this ends the argument. All the trash
about robbing the banks, insurance offices and other
capitalist concerns is not needed at all. There is
nothing at all wrong or illegal with the workers
refusing to produce more than they themselves need.
When this is done all who live wholly on or partially
on Surplus Value, in the form of interest, rent or
profit, will find their incomes from these sources gone,
and if they want to live they will have to work and
produce.—Yours truly, P.J.
And when the machinery of the factory
wears out, what then? And when “P.J.’s”
little Tom and Jack want jobs, which jobs
will require more machinery, what then?
If the world was going to stop with the
death of the present generation, the policy
of the One Big Union might be understand-
able. But if there is no surplus value —if
the workers produce only enough for them-
selves, no more and no less —how are fac-
tories and railways to be provided to
meet the labor requirements of the
largely-increased next generation? If the
miner produces only enough for his
own requirements, who is. going to do
the dead work of opening up the new
mines. And when little Tom, having
grown up, marries, and wants a house of his
own, where is it to come from? Little Tom
might think over these things. So might
Lizzie. And Lizzie’s mother. The scheme
of reducing the hours of toil, and limiting
production, and cutting out Surplus Value
sounds very beautiful; but it will leave
somebody very cold and hungry. And the
somebody isn’t going to be only the boss. It
is going to be rather the worker’s baby when
the worker’s baby grows up, and the worker’s
•widow when the worker dies and his former
fellow-workers produce only enough for their
own needs.

The Uses of People Like Barnes.
Minister Barnes, of Victoria, is of no im-
portance, save as an evidence that candi-
dates for Parliament should be required to
show a qualification—not the old property
kind, but a diploma certifying that they
have passed a stiff examination in history,
general knowledge of Australia, and, above
all, in political economy. Also they should
be liable to expulsion if their statements on
matters of fact suggest that they got the
diploma by hiring a similar-looking person
to pass for them. Plad that rule pre-
vailed a number of public nuisances
might have been warned off for. tell-
ing the community that the originally-
selected Federal-capital site was nearer to
Melbourne than to Sydney ; and a certain
blatant Freetrader, still in politics, might
have been put out of politics for persistently
declaring to the people that some old Protec-
tionist emperor built a Great Wall all round
China. Also Mines Minister Barnes of
Victoria would go out on his ear. Barnes’s
gag is that the gold-mining industry should
be subsidised and boomed “because for every
ounce sold to the Mint the Commonwealth
may issue twelve fl notes,” wherefore it is
the key to untold wealth. As a matter of
law the Commonwealth may issue within a
fraction of £l7, not for every ounce of gold
that reaches the Mint, but for every ounce
the Treasury contrives to retain after pay-
ing those foreign liabilities for, which our
notes are merely waste paper. But a§ a
matter of fact it can issue only as much
paper as the people want and have a use
for, unless it makes the issue compulsory and
irredeemable, and in that case it doesn’t want
any gold at all, and the notes are liable
to be Worth Id. in the £. On the evidence
of the circulation of the kind of notes that
people use —not the £IOOO sort which are
given as security for loans —the community
at present has a steady demand for perhaps
£25,000,000 or £30,000,000 worth, and
against these a gold reserve is only required
to prevent panic. It is the kind of reserve
that isn’t wanted when it is there, but would
be badly wanted if people knew or imagined
it wasn’t there. But if the Barnes kind
of man shoved out another £50,000,000 worth
of notes they would come back so fast that
in all probability a £25,000,000 gold reserve
wouldn’t cover them, nor even a £40,000,000
reserve. Australia has more than the
average number of politicians, and they are
of rather low average capacity, because, the
supply of capable men who care for politics
is limited. This is partly because schools
don’t teach politics. Some of the numerous
politicians are costly, inasmuch as their
misinformation destroys a lot of knowledge
that the community has paid for. Also, when
they get a chance they cost a lot in experi-
ments, like the doctor who doesn’t know that
prussic acid is fatal till he kills a patient
with it. For instance, no man of education
wants to try Barnes’s finance at this coun-
try’s expense. He can sit down and watch
at least half a dozen countries trying it at
their own expense.

The Australian Army Reserve.
From one who ought to know: —
“Returned Citizen,” who writes (B. 6/3/’l9)
about the Australian Army Reserve, has that little
learning which is a dangerous thing. Advisedly the
scheme interferes as little as possible with a man’s
civil life, and conditions are alike voluntary and as
elastic as possible; for this Reserve will consist of
men who have been trained in the only school that
really makes a man a soldier, and, consequently, a
four-days’ camp once a year will serve to bring
them in touch with their units, and be sufficient to
keep them from forgetting what they learned on actual
service. So it will “preserve for Australia the skill
and experience gained during the war.” In the case
of technical units I admit the keen soldier will ask
for more training, and lam equally certain that the
Defence Department will be glad to give it; but
everything has to have a beginning, and at this
stage the first essential is to ask for as little of the
man’s civil life as possible.
It is true, as “Returned Citizen” states, that all
citizens, including returned soldiers, are liable to be
called up for home defence; but he is wrong in say-
ing that “the scheme does not give the returned man
any further training or experience.” It does; and,
furthermore, it ensures to the man who joins it that,
if he is called up, it will be to a properly-constituted
unit—practically his old A.I.F. one —and that if he
has to fight it will be side by side with his old
mates. On the other hand, if he does not belong to
the Reserve he will, in the event of mobilisation, be
aliotted to the nearest Trainee Battalion, and will
probably have to fight with, and be led by, strangers
alike to himself and to 'active service.
The scheme, with all its faults, is going to give
Australia the finest Reserve the world has yet seen ;

returned men to he sure that the fact that it is
voluntary and its conditions easy will in no way
impair its efficiency, but rather increase it. “Re-

official literature” when referring to the camp: that

ing years.

against the scheme were absolutely in accordance
with fact, I still hold the scheme amply justified if
it do no more than bring the men who have helped
to save Australia and the world together once a year,
with no expense to themselves, to renew old friend-
ships of bivouac and battlefield. It is the least those
who owe them so much can do, and I am sure every
decent man in Australia is prepared to pay his share
in the doing of it.
The Sawbones Trade.
From a city consulting-room:—
The remarks issuing from a country surgery (B.
G/3/’l9) call for some comment. The writer evi-
dently is of opinion that the B.M.A. is the proper
body to attempt to put the medical house in order.
He couldn’t be wider of the mark. What we require
in these times of medical unrest is not a company of
snipers to work for us, but a regiment of storm-
troops. There would be very little need to put our
house in order if all practitioners could be persuaded
to make a pilgrimage, every five years or so, to
British and foreign clinics, with a view to brushing
the accumulated cobwebs from their grey matter and
absorbing new ideas. The amount of professional
ignorance in the registered ranks is appalling, and
it is by no means confined to the riff-raff.
As far as the writer can make out by close obser-
vation for over 20 years, the general practitioner,
who is the backbone of the profession, has been con-
tent to go on plodding and pottering about doing
medical-hack work, year after year, piling pound
upon pound and filling his surgery-safe with mort-
gages at 8 p.c. Instead of being a kind of Iket Mo,
each and all of them should be waging war against
disease, imbued with the true scientific spirit of
inquiry, and should let the pursuit of boodle go
hang! Doing the same old soul-depressing, intellect-
cramping, body-exhausting, medical hack-work for an
indefinite period • prevents the practitioner from
becoming up-to-date; consequently a loophole is
afforded for aspersions on the profession of Medicine
in the lay press. The watchword, “We are all out
to make money,” should be altered to “We are all
out to become good doctors.”
Your country doctor talks of being “fed up with
the panic-provoking activities of the politico-medical
authorities,” and in the same breath requests the
B.M.A. to put the medical house in order. He must
know as well as I that 10 of the 12 members of the
N. S. Wales Medical Consultative Council are also
members of the B.M.A. Council. They can hardly
be expected to take action against themselves for
’flu blunders.
The B.M.A. has no political influence or power
whatever, and generally comes off second-best in any
attempted reforms. The Victorian lodges’ dispute is
as acute as ever; the N. S. Wales Education Depart-
ment has hail its own sweet way with regard to the
medical treatment of school-children; and ophthal-
mic and aural cases are sent up to the building near
the Railway Station, to the Department’s own special-
ists, notwithstanding all the fuss that was made not
very long ago about the nationalisation of the pro-
fession by the Association’s perfervid orators. The
Queensland Government has passed a Bill for the
registration of opticians in defiance of B.M.A.
opinions, and, at present, spectacle-makers can
prescribe glasses for any of the serious diseases of
the eye with perfect safety under the ®gis of the
Ryan Government. The Labor party’s troubles
about the blinding of Brisbane! Nothing but a
powerful medico-political union, or, better still, a
trade union with a carapace of sounding brass, would
have had the slightest chance of success against the
Queensland pachyderms. The B.M.A. listens to
papers being read on medical subjects, and prepares
various kinds of rules and regulations in camera,
which it springs upon members when they are not
looking, and which often cause a storm in a teacup
by their manifest bias and unfairness. At best, its
meetings serve as a hunting-ground for office-seekers,
and it is spineless and nearing its last kick.
A few words about the stagnant established prac-
titioner. If the general practitioner doesn’t soon
rouse himself from his professional lethargy, he will
find, like Othello, his occupation gone. If he would
only take the trouble to keep himself up-to-date
there would be no need for the host of new special-
ties which are springing up in town like mushrooms.
But “we are not breeding intelligence nowadays,”
says a London physician ; and he is about right.
India and the War.
Despite much official politeness, the fact is
plain enough that India was a disappoint-
ment in the war. Its greatest achievement
was the maintenance of 360,000 men, which,
in point of population, was about 11 per
cent, of Australia’s effort. Nobody ex-
pected the country to send 28,000,000 sol-
diers to the war, but what it did was less
than might have been hoped for. The war
contribution in cash was about 2s. 7d. (31
pence) per inhabitant. Nobody expected
our brown brother to lash out at the rate
of £SO or £IOO or more per head, as some
of the white Allies did, for he is not an
affluent brother; but even making all
sorts of allowances his effort was humble.
In Flanders his arrival was hailed with pre-
meditated enthusiasm, but little was said
about his numbers and less about his doings.
Presently he was allowed, to all intents and
purposes, to fade out of the story. The one
lamentable surrender which a British force
made in the war—at Kut-el-Amara —was
largely his surrender. In the Palestine cam-
paign he was let loose with his flying steed
and his picturesque accoutrements when the
Turk’s heart was already more than half-
broken. But he was tried very little at
the work which required illimitable patience
and profound endurance —the uphill fight
when the hill was perpendicular, or the
dogged retreat when there was no place
worth mentioning to retreat to and little
hope of getting there. The bulk of the
Indian peoples don’t even profess to be the
fighting sort; the Bengali, for instance,
would hardly blow a fly off a sheep unless
he was sure the sheep was unarmed, though
for miscellaneous tumult he can always hold
his own with the mob of stage hands in
“Julius Caesar,” And even the fighting
races are below a very ordinary European
standard, to say nothing of the standard
that was set at Mons and Verdun and Ypres
and Gallipoli and Mont St. Quentin, though
it wouldn’t be polite to let the fact leak out.

The usual Parliamentary recess in Tasmania
lasts for six unbroken months, from Decem-
ber to June, and anybody who dared to sug-
gest disturbance of that tradition has been
looked upon as a dangerous iconoclast and a
person of no account. But the Lee Govern-
ment, threatened with internal combustion,
recently disregarded the ancient custom and
called a special session to select a Senator
vice Long, resigned. One result of this re-
volutionary proceeding is that the holding of
a business session in the middle of recess is
no longer held to be sacrilege deserving of
destruction by the lightnings of Jove ; where-
fore Labor Opposition-leader Lyons has
written to the Premier asking for an imme-
diate assembling of the House to deal with
matters of urgency. Among these items of
alleged importance are :
Provision for the payment to railway men of the
increased wages recommended by a Reclassification
Board since Parliament put up the shutters.
Legislation for improving the sanitary conditions
of workers employed in the hopfields in view of the
threatened influenza plague.
Consideration of the King River electric power-
scheme in the light of the recent dropping of nego-
tiations between the Government and the Read-
Rosebery Co. for the sale and purchase of 20,000-h.p.
of current for treatment of West Coast ores.
Mention is also made of the desirability of
Tasmania’s own legislature doing something
definite for Tasmania’s own returned soldiers
instead of tacitly endorsing Millen’s heart-
breaking go-slow policy. Looked at from a
distance none of these things seem to be of
the kind that ought to demand legislative
action. They are all matters of administra-
tion. But Parliaments, instead of laying
down principles, have got into the habit of
insisting upon going into details; with the
result that when Parliament is not sitting
Governments are either incapable of dealing
with emergencies or unwilling to do it.
“Keystone” : A recent case in a Mel-
bourne suburban court hits the point raised
by “F.C.” (B. 28/11/YS) about Victorian
manufacturers making goods for the local
market and branding them ui to the
standard of other States. Years ago _a
splendid Pure Foods Act was built up jn
Victoria, mainly by Professor Osborne and
Dr. P. Wilkinson. The standard set was
very high. A ' movement was presently
begun to have a uniform Commonwealth
standard. Victoria and N. S. Wales both
had fairly complete lists, but the N.S.W.
standard was much lower generally. Vic-
toria agreed to climb down on one or two
points, provided N.S.W. came up on some
others considered vital. N. S. Wales agreed
at first, but suddenly backed out. the sup-
posed reason being pressure by N.S.W.
manufacturers. So the conference fell
through, and now certain Vic. manufacturers
turn out stuff guaranteed under Bananaland,
Tasmanian or N. S. IVales standards. Why
shouldn’t a State insist that all goods sold
in it comply with its own regulations?
“Cargo Bill” : Since August, 1914, a score
or more mercantile-marine officers have
answered the call and joined up with the
Australian navy. Some left positions with
honorable employers, and will get them back.
The majority are not so fortunate, and now
that the war is over and demobilisation is
imminent they are wondering what to do
for a crust. During the war great oppor-
tunities occurred in the merchant service,
and promotion has been very rapid. For
instance, the Commonwealth collected 15
modern Hun steamers in various ports. Then
the Commonwealth became a shipowner in a
large way. Promotion in these vessels has
been very rapid. But the Reserve officer,
after being thanked very much for his ser-
vices and told not to trip over the mat, finds
himself standing in the street, wondering
where he will look for a berth. I make this
suggestion to the Naval Board: that, as the
Commonwealth (Government) line is as
much a national institution as the Aus-
tralian navy and is practically controlled by
the same authority, the Board should
arrange that Reserve officers, on being de-
mobilised from the R.A.N., be given positions
in the Commonwealth ships, their service in
the R.A.N. counting for promotion. This is
the least reward that can fairly be offered
them for their war services.
New York is chuckling. It wants
£2,000,000 of revenue. Though distinctly
wet itself, it has been ordered by the Union
to go dry with the rest. So, in view of the
loss of revenue from real liquor, some bright
mind has conceived the idea of taxing soda
fountains, soft drinks and mineral waters—
which take the place of tea in Australia.

assailed.
“Wings”: Over 500 pilots have been
trained for the A.F.C., and most of them
have had service in France or Palestine.
Those who are left are now coming back —
a good many have landed—and the Defence
Department doesn’t know whether it is
going to have a Flying Service or not. So
the expensively-trained specialists are taking
their discharges and scattering over the
country. Some day somebody in the dead
Department will have a brain-wave; and a
branch will be established to chase up the
addresses, of the discharged men and invite
them to chuck up the jobs which another
Department has employed a lot of men to
find for them. Or, more likely, having spent
many tens of thousands teaching us the fly-
ing business, the Department will establish
us a s bee-farmers, and Ihen send to England
for an expert to teach a fresh crowd at
further great expense.

What looks like a new dodge in spieling
was introduced to a Melbourne man in
Bourke-street when a casual stranger com-
mented on his lusty appearance. He
thought he would go “at least 15 stone. ’
The other reckoned he weighed nearly 16.
To test the matter the stranger slipped his
arms round the other’s waist and lifted him.
Then he confessed that he thought he had
miscalculated, wished the other good evening
and departed cheerfully. When he put
his hand in his pocket the stout person found
he was 3 stone 81b. lighter. The affable
stranger had got away with £5O.

Joseph Leask, ship-constructor to the
Navy Department, has given the Australian
toiler a testimonial which goes to show that
there are still plenty of hard-doers in this
country. According to Leask, in the fitting
and re-fitting of transports, horse-carriers
and cargo-vessels, over a four-years’ war-
period “there was not one stoppage of work
on any pretext, nor a strike of any descrip-
tion, nor was there any evidence of the go-
slow policy.”

Twenty shillings or a month.
The Defence Department lately sold ofi a
supply of military tents at Broadmeadows.
The method of sale, at fixed rates ranging
from ss. to 12s. 6d. per tent, was character-
istic. Samples were exhibited at the camp
site. Buyers had to visit melancholy Broad-
meadows and examine them, return to the
Victoria Barracks and pay for the required
number of a given sample and then make a
second trip to Broadmeadows to take delivery.
For complete disregard of the public con-
venience aiyl the ordinary rules of business
this instance is fully up to previous records.
Mouldy old Dr. Watts or somebody said
“Satan finds some mischief still for idle
hands to do.” When a mob of perhaps
400,000 idle hands, which means 200,000 idle
mouths, gets together it will do horrors that
no individual member would dream of, for a
mob has a conscience of its own. These
400,000 hands, carrying 400,000 bricks, may
rush Willie Watt before another year is over
unless he gets to work with the tariff and
finds some remedy for idleness. A French
mob made a most unpleasant mess of a man
who told the unemployed to eat grass. Watt
doesn’t sap that sort of thing, but he acts it.
Something of a problem arises about re-
cruits for the Australian navy. When the
Hun is disarmed —it is proposed to limit his
army to 100,000 men without reserves and
to reduce his navy to a skeleton —then other
countries will be able to cut down their
forces to. correspond, provided the United
States gives up its project of an overwhelm-
ing war-fleet. The soldiers and sailors who
remain should be the best-paid class in the
community, for when a very few men are
responsible for the public safety they should
be the best men the public can buy. Inciden-
tally, there can be shocking waste of sailors
outside of a navy. Australia, for instance,
employs a multitude of men to hump half or
more of everything it grows to the ends of
the earth, and hump back nearly half of all
it uses. If it were a self-reliant country it
could do with one-third of these seamen.
Superfluous sailors are a drag on the com-
munity just as much as superfluous dukes.
“Edo”: The Bulletin lately credited
Westralian returned soldiers with proposing
to repudiate the national debt. It isn’t so,
but Perth papers printed the rumor and
then would not rectify matters. The facts
are these. A few men belonging to the
R.S.A. convened a Sunday-afternoon gather-
ing and called it an R.S.A. meeting. The
R.S.A. executive at once objected, but to no
purpose. In fact, the advertisement appeared
too late for objections to do much good.
The meeting was attended hy every
variety of extremist, a few soldiers standing
around as interested spectators. Only three
returned men spoke, and when the repudia-
tion resolution was put it was voted for by
all and sundry down to small boys and flap-
pers. Since then, nearly every branch of the
Westralian R.S.A. has indignantly disclaimed
any connection with the meeting or any sug-
gestion of repudiating the debt.
From a man in the business:—
There are tons of experience coming to some of
those returned Billjims whom the repatriators are
putting “on the land.” Right and left of me are two
Diggers. One has eight acres of grass land, on which
he proposes to grow flowers and lemons. He ad-
mittedly knows little of either—and lemon-growing
is a profession which I don’t suppose more than 20
men in the Commonwealth have mastered. The other
returnee has taken a place that has vainly sought a
buyer for three years, and has been empty most of
that time. On it are an acre of pears and three
acres of peaches, badly neglected. This place has
already been thrown up by an ex-soldier who leased
it on trial. Probably the repatriators think the
present occupant, having been a carpenter, may make
a better do of it. Why doesn’t the Department get
the advice of competent local growers? This man
has no chance under the conditions, but with that
block cleared and ready for the plough he could have
made a living by growing potatoes and certain other
vegetables, provided he knew anything about the job.

The change to “Lucia” at Melbourne
Princess’s on Saturday seemed more unwel-
come to the sophisticated Italian principals
than to the comparatively untrained Austra-
lians who took the chief feminine roles and
provided most of the chorus. Probably one
trouble with the Italians, conspicuous in the
cases of Filippini and Barontini, was a sud-
den consciousness of the national inability to
wear Scotland’s draughty petticoat like a
religious conviction. Filippini, who is never
obtrusively masculine on account of a small
tubby figure and an air of anxious futility,
merely expressed in Henry Ashton a silent
supplication for a pair of trousers. Baron-
tini, less embarrassed than his senior,
appeared to be oppressed by the feeling that
the exposure of his calves was rather risque,
so he tried to make the most of a difficult
position by dramatically folding his arms at
intervals and affecting an attitude of ridicu-
lous composure. Both, however, sang well,
Filippini’s tremolo quite pardonably becom-
ing agitato as his legs grew colder; whereas
Lois, who had the advantage of the lyric
raptures which Donizetti has congested in
the mouth of Edgar, lost much of the effect
of his impressive entrances and pretty
clothes by obviously waiting for the guiding
hand of conductor Slapoffslii. He was
plainly not at home, and moreover he had
apparently devoted the interval since his
last appearance in acquiring from Cacialli
the complete spaghettiquette of the Dago
school of acting. Lois showed some dra-
matic originality as Turiddu in “Cavalleria,”
but. his Edgar systematically semaphored the
varied feelings that disturb that demonstra-
tive person. Cacialli gave one of his char-
acteristic hand-painted pictures of the burly
Raymond, and the Australian tenor, Brown-
ing Mummery, failed to reach eminence in
the role of Arthur Bucklaw. The one out-
standing achievement was the performance
of Gertrude Johnston as Lucia. She might
have developed a fuller flood of melody, but
she always had the right note and she never
once stumbled in the Mad Scene, which is
the popular standard. Also, her acting
in most places was an acceptable equivalent
of the behavior of a female in Lucia’s state
of distress, whilst avoiding the florid Italian
“business” which usually serves to heap deri-
sion on the character. The chorus again
showed high efficiency, but in its mechanical
semi-circular grouping it suggested a crowd
of pommies watching a roundabout. To-
night “Lucia” will be repeated, to-morrow
“Faust” is expected again, and on Saturday
the. double bill of “Cavalleria” and “Pagli-
acci” will get another turn.
