At the Melbourne Royal Muriel Starr con-
tinues to let down her dark tresses and take
a violent farewell of her muvver’s photo
before confiding herself to the care of the
brutal person who has broken into her home
in Arizona. Tosca, with her virtue tremb-
ling in the balance, never showed a higher
consciousness of the impending sacrifice than
Muriel Starr, though Muriel’s captor is of
finer moral calibre than the dirty Scarpia
and eventually proves so dashed respectable
that he is allowed to marry the lady and
turn “The Great Divide” into a multiplica-
tion table. The arrival of the baby under
orthodox circumstances must be an un-
pleasant jar to the people who were con-
fidently expecting the Worst to happen, but
the infant’s appearance is so belated that
there are nearly two acts for spicy specula-
tion. In a sense the nipper constitutes a
“spoof” on the audience, but you only realise
the absurdity of tin 1 surprise when you come
to painfully think it over.
When The Bulletin last saw “Goody
Two Shoes” at Melbourne Majesty’s Dame
Stigant was endeavoring to brighten her
part in the story by doubling her allowance
of red flannelette; and there are now stiil
more blinding gleams of vermilion when the
stout lady collapses in the ecstasy of listen-
ing to her own rich jests. But Stigant’s
lurid underwear is not bringing in the
population in sufficient quantities to fill
the cash-box.
Cavities are visible also at Melbourne
King’s. Intending supporters of “Jack and
Jill” were violently dispersed by General
’Flu, and now all the King’s jokes and all
the King’s men can hardly bring the people
together again. So “Jack and Jill” will
probably be sent over the Murray to see if
Sydney has lost the panto habit.

William S. Hart
has been sighted at
Hoyt’s Melbourne,
making his own
trouble in “Selfish
Yates.” It is a
characteristic item
with more Hart
than art, and follows a route that a
short-sighted Melbourne detective could
find in the dark. Elsewhere Vivian
Martin has to provide appropriate flapper
attitudes for the vicissitudes of “Vivi-
ette” as a pursuer of men. Vivian only
feels her assumed age now and again, but
the picture gets a special cheer because it
derives its inspiration from “The Loves of
Vinette,” by the popular W. J. Locke. At
the Britannia the amazing Tarzan is hurry-
ing through his jungle romance while ruling
his fantastic simian kingdom. The Melba is
more civilised with “The Mating,” but the
civilisation doesn’t go too far —Herbert Raw-
linson and Gladys Leslie furnish a glimpse
of primitive passion when the film threatens
to take the prize for politeness and breeding.
“Hello, Everybody!” is rapidly turning
into “Cheerio, Everybody!” The large and
glittering revue is skipping through its last
nights, and the curtain at Sydney Majesty’s
will fall on Friday, March 28.
“For a Limited Season Only” appears in
pencil on the label of “Bubbly,” now being
uncorked at the Palace (Sydney). This
seems to throw a thin shadow of closing-
time over the revelry ; but no guest has yet
become so gloomy as to mistake the leaping
Kurylo gentleman for the President of the
Methodist Conference.

Vera Pearce, in “My Lady Frayle,” con-
tinues her heroic and generally artistic
efforts to bribe the Devil with somebody else’s
soul to exchange her grey hair and false
teeth and give her the year 1875 or there-
abouts. Meanwhile the' devil of Claude
Flemming grows more and more like a draw-
ing-room conjurer out to amuse the children.
How long will this quaint mixture hold the
Sydney Tivoli audiences? Odds are that
regular patrons would plump for a little
less Marie Corelli drama and a little more
songandance.
“Maytime” is giving a good imitation of a
winner at Sydney Cri. Hmvever, it is com-
ing off in ten days’ time to make way for
“Nothing but the Truth,” a farce framed on
the nonsensical idea of a world which sud-
denly goes completely veracious.
Till Saturday Alien Doone will persist
in the “Wearing of the Green,” and on that
night he will change into the nicely-laun-
dered ruffles of “Tom Moore,” That’s one
thing about Allen Doone: his linen is
always as stainless as his plays.

It has been a slack, though not a close,
season for amusements in Adelaide. Royal.
Majestic and Tivoli all closed for lack of
visiting artists. The Scarlet Gaieties at
Austral Gardens had the best of things.
Open-air shows were naturally the most
popular, and for a time they had the help of
Sydney .Tames and Madeline Rossiter, who
were unable to get away to Perth. The
Royal has reopened with the gloomily in-
structive “Damaged Goods,” finely screened.
The public has been invited to consider whe-
ther the Nude is Rude at West’s. The play,
“Hypocrites,” was so successful that it had
a further run at the Pav. “Sherlock
Holmes” as acted by Gillette is the present
attraction at West’s. Wondergraph replies
with Dorothy Dalton, the breezy Fairbanks,
and an unending drama showing how the
Secret Service of Uncle Sam outwitted the
local Hun spy.
Some of the Radical Liberal members of
the Vic. Leg. Assembly are going to propose
that the form of the preamble of"bills should
be altered to read: “By and with the consent
of the Melbourne Club, Collins House, and
Cliveden Mansions.” *
“Gyp” : The Huns’ secret service in Egypt
was up to the minute. Four pottering old
tramps, capable of doing six knots, left Port
Said harbor for the Med. one day at noon.
Before the last of the Noah’s arks had cleared
the breakwater her engines broke down
and she put back. The other three steamed
on, and about 4 p.m. a Fritz tinfish sank
them all. Then the Hun skipper looked
round and asked us survivors in the life-
boats : “Where’s the fourth ship that left
with you?”
“Dial-Sight” : The Digger stood and stared
when he saw the sallow little Portugee
greet a Lisbon friend. I first saw the per-
formance when a new arrival alighted from
a motor-waggon to join a fatigue party.
Spotting a cobber amongst the aroup he
hauled him to his chest. After the break-
away the attacking Portugee handed the
recipient what looked like a gentle kidney-
punch. Another clean break and a hand-
shake were followed by a peck in the region
of the right ear. Then they felt calm enough
to start talking.

“Edo”: “Horace Cheese” (B. 0/2/’l9)
suggests political economy as a school sub-
ject. Over 25 years ago South Australia s
Education Department had it on its pro-
gramme of study. The text-book was a
well-written little volume by the late Cathe-
rine Helen Snence, “The Laws We Live
Under.” Unfortunately Miss Spence didn’t
suppress her personal views; so the Trades
Hall folk of the day had the book thrown
out. And I haven’t met a text-book since
that one party or another wouldn’t declare
to be heretical.
“Yarra Ben”: No wonder Melbourne
failed to detect the ’flu for days and days
after it had started killing people! In a
record just received by Chief-Secretary
Bowser, the cheerful tidings are published
that the crimes reported to the jxilice in
the State during February numbered 533,
of which 478 were undetected. The previ-
ous month beat that, for there were 656
offences reported and only 83 tracked down.
Gilbert notwithstanding, in Victoria a police-
man’s job is a nappy one.
The American female “reformer” threatens
to worry Uncle Sam worse than the Kaiser
ever did. Having killed liquor she is now
out after tobacco. The American tobacco
industry is bivger than the same industry in
anybody else’s back yard. But Mamie says
it’s sure got to go—both use and manufac-
ture. The Women’s Christian Temperance
Union, which did more than any other body
to bring about prohibition, has declared for
years that tobacco would follow drink into
the discard. The fight is now opening. So
Australia knows what to expect from the
American Anti-saloon Leaguers here on good
salaries and liberal expense accounts. The
Leaguers will never let themselves be run
out of a job. After liquor and tobacco
there will still remain racing, boxing, surf-
ing, the movies, dancing, draughts and eat-
ing.
“F.D.” : This is what two generations of
dependence upon the Government Cow has
done to Australians. A cattle-raiser from
the Gulf country gave a column interview
to a Melbourne pressman, and the burden
of his dirge was that the Government would
not replace the old punt which had sunk in
the river near Normanton (Q.). Said he:
“We are forced to make a detour of 34 miles
every time we have to cross the river,
because of the absence of a punt. It would
not cost more than £lOO to replace the
sunken one.” Let those self-reliant northern
cattlemen go on making their 34 miles run-
around ! Some day they may w T ake up and
help themselves to a new tub. Isn’t the
price of beef high enough for them?
From a man in the country:—
My brother and 1 carry on this business. At least
we did until he went off to the war. Since then the
Department for the Suppression of All Enterprise
has been taking a keen interest in our affairs.
First, I employed the leading accountant of the town
to prepare our War-profits Tax return, and he
assured me that it was done in accordance with
departmental instructions. Since then I have re-
ceived two letters from the Department, requiring
returns which were guaranteed to keep a clerk busy
for a week ; and now I am called upon to furnish a
statutory declaration that my brother was, amongst
other things, actually in a position of danger between
such and such dates. Beyond that he is a private of
the Battalion (and I have supplied them with
his regimental number) I know next to nothing, of
course. How could I know whether he was in a
position of danger on a certain date? If these
people have nothing for part of their staff to do
except harass people with idiotic questions, what
about thinning out their staff?

1918.. 28,247 10 p.c.= 9700 18,547 31,281
Established nine years ago this Australian
office, which deals in all classes of insurance
except life, has shown remarkable growth
of business. Gross premium income ivas
£146,000-odd in 1915 ; for the past year it
was three times greater. But it is necessary
only to glance at the list of the score or so
“marine settling agencies” created during the
past three years, and now proudly displayed
among the co.’s world-wide connections, to
learn the source of a big proportion of the
new business. Marine insurance during the
war provided fine opportunities for the ven-
turesome. Many big, conservative concerns
looked upon much of the business with a
cold eye and limited their risks, and smaller
and more aggressive offices have been able to
pick up more than their proportion of what
has been going. Some of the cos. that threw
their trust on the navy and plunged have
shown remarkable figures; for instance, there
is the case of the Western Australian Insur-
ance Co., whose premium income in 1913-14
was £3500, and in 1917-18 over a million.
Fortunately for the punters, the gamble
worked out all right, and the business has
proved highly profitable. For the year to
June 30, 1918, £411,948 of marine net pre-
miums were got in by cos. doing business in
N S. Wales at a commission cost of 6.81 p.e.
Losses called for only 37.36 p.e;, and all other
exes, chargeable to the business ran away
with a further 20.07 p.e. Thus no less than
35.76 p.e. of the net premiums was profit.
No doubt, because of the sudden disappear-
ance of Wilhelm’s tinfisli, the last months of
1918 showed a still wider margin.
The 1.0. A. has been showing steady ex-
pansion in other lines, but the effect of the
cheaply-got marine business and the low loss
rate has been to pull down the percentage of
outgoings in this way : —•
Net Loss Expense
Premiums. Losses. Expenses. Rate. Rate.
1911. . £25,951 £6,748 £17,537 26.0 p.e. 67.5 p.e.
1912.. 47,241 22,443 27,845 47.5 „ 58.9 „
1913.. 54,618 22,911 35,275 41.9 „ 64.6 „
1914.. 67,510 39,292 35,222 58.2 „ 52.1 ~
1915.. 93,516 52,002 37,019 55.6 „ 39.5 „
1916.. 115,885 59,731 38,742 51.5 „ 33.5 „
1917.. 144,917 79,419 47,161 54.8 „ 32.5 „
1918.. 229,554 110,887 63,046 48.3 „ 27.5 „
For a co. which has been in business for so
few years that expense rate is wonderfully
low ; but, as explained above, it is mainly due
to abnormal conditions. It may be, as the
directors believe, “the lowest expense rate
shown by any Australian co.” But it may
bo different when, instead of some of the
business being practically left on the door-
step, the co. has again to scrap for every
new risk that is worth having.
As shown above, the underwriting profit
last year was £55,621. Interest and rents
brought in £5671. To arrive at the net pro-
fit of £28,214 shown in the first table, deduct
£2444 to cover rates, taxes, etc., on the co.’s
properties, £14,601 set aside for bad debts
and contingencies, and a further £16,000 pro-
vided for taxes. The reserve for contingen-
cies is apparently in connection with the
co.’s investments, and the appropriation is so
substantial for a co. which has had its plate
out for only a few years that it suggests that
the white-ants have wasted no time.
The least satisfactory feature of the
1.0.A.’s balance-sheet is the absence of any
reserve for unexpired risks. The Bulletin,
recollecting the shaky beginnings of some
old-established concerns, hates to throw
bricks at young shows which are gradually
finding their feet against fierce competition.
But a co. like the 1.0. A., which has already
handed out .three 10 p.e. divs. to its share-
holders and is preparing to hit out all over
the globe (J. Charteris Black left for Cousin
Jonathan’s country the other day as “foreign
superintendent” to “open up branches
throughout the world”), is looking for criti-
cism if its affairs are not entirely in order,
judged by accepted insurance standards. Last
year the co. showed a net premium income of
£229,554, The whole of this has been taken
credit for in estimating the year’s profits,
though obviously some of the risks on which
these premiums were collected will not expire
for some months yet. According to the
generally accepted practice 40 p.c. of the
year’s premiums should be held to cover un-
expired risks. On that basis, at December
31, instead of the reserves of £31,281, to
fully protect both policy-holders and share-
holders there should have been nearly three
times that amount in hand.
Capital is £96,497; and total funds repre-
sent only 56 p.c. of the year’s premiums.
Something nearer 156 p.c. is generally
considered a safer proportion to fully
protect both clients and shareholders. For
1917 the funds were equal to about 75
p.c. of that year’s premiums, so to
chase the doubtful profit from marine
business the co. overreached itself last
year. Such a position might easily have
put the concern in trouble; but luck was
with the management. No doubt with the
disappearance of war risks the premium
figures will shrink considerably, and a more
reasonable proportion will exist ’ between
funds and risks. Liabilities at December 31
amounted to £33,713; and the funds were
invested in this way: Deposits, cash, War
Loan and sundries, £138,905; premises,
etc., £41,948; shares in other cos.,
£16,420; and mortgages, £6443. Deducting
all liabilities and allowing for the contingen-
cies provided by the management, the co.
could at December 31 meet claims totalling
over £137,000. And when considering
current risks the incoming premiums have,
of course, also to be taken into account.

The year’s profits were considerably better
than the figures shown in the last line. The
management did not know until last year
what it would have to part with in War-
time Profits Tax for the previous couple of
years; but instead of setting by some of the
profits of each of those terms in a taxation
reserve, P. and L. was left to take the full
shock when the tax-collector called. Dur-
ing the past 12 months £4552 has been paid
in taxes, as much, within a few pounds, as
was called for altogether in the previous
three years. Since each year’s contribution
cannot be accurately gauged, the whole of
this £4552 has been deducted from last
year’s profits, besides a further £6500 which
the directors have since transferred to a
newly-created reserve for taxation. It will
thus be seen that, despite the scarcity of
shipping, which might reasonably have been
expected to seriously affect the business of
the co., better results than ewer have shown
up. As a matter of fact, the war has given
it a big lift: for the four years to 1914
gross profits totalled £173,775; for the four
years since, £290,645. Of course, a lot of
this has been due to the new money put into
the business during the former period, and
which no doubt only began to earn some-
thing more than its board-and-lodging when
the war started.
This shows how capital and other balance-
sheet figures expanded over the two four-
year periods: —•
1910. 1914. 1918.
Capital .. ..£74,682 £120,000 £150,000
Sundry creditors.. 47,911 45,230 40,577
Premises and plant 21,310 28,875 52,655
Stock-in-trade . . 84,675 121,293 125,407
Book debts . . 26,375 38,808 34,163
Cash and sundries 710 1,317 17,095
Between 1910 and 1914 the liquid surplus
grew by £52,359 and fixed assets by £7565.
During the same period £45,318 of new capi-
tal was got in, and £1452 in premiums. So
after handing out 10 p.c. aivs. the co.’s posi-
tion was improved by about £13,000. Since
then the div. rate has been lifted to 15 p.c.—
£76,500 being distributed to shareholders
against £36,354 for the previous period.
Meantime the liquid surplus has expanded
by £19,900 and £23,780 more has been added
to the book value of premises and plant.
No new capital has been got in since 1914,
the increase of £30,000 shown in the table
being transferred reserves. Thus in the
past four years, after deducting the current
div. and omitting also the £6500 set aside for
taxes, there has been an improvement of
£22,150 in the co.’s position. The policy of
expansion which was decided upon after
some argument 10 years ago has thus been
justified. Whether during the rapid growth
of the concern shareholders have not been
a little too greedy in the matter of divs.—
particularly in view of the 15 p.c. now being
paid on the increased capital—is a matter
of opinion. It is doubtful whether it would
not have paid better in the long run to have
kept more of the profits in the business to
provide for further expansion in the better
years ahead. Still, the co. is now very
snug.

Last year was full of difficulties for the
Malayan rubber cos. Owing to lack of
shipping space and the restriction of im-
ports into the U.S.A., the Singapore price
slumped from 25.3 d. per lb. for the previous
year to 18.2 d. Since at that price there
was very little margin over the cost of pro-
duction, this co. decided to curtail its out-
put until prices looked up. So instead of
harvesting the estimated 85,0001 b., only
70,6011 b. were got, and at the close of the
financial year 27,9221 b. remained unsold.
Of course, maintenance charges went on as
usual; so, after writing off £832 for depre-
ciation, the year’s operations showed a loss
of £677. Since the armistice shipping diffi-
culties have eased considerably and prices
are improving. For the current year the
crop is estimated at 100,1621 b. If-the co.
can show the same margin of profit as in the
year before last (about Bd. per lb.), there
would be a profit of about £3340 on this out-
put, equal to over 13 p.c. on the modest
capital of £25,000.
The co.’s estate consists of 808 acres near
Seremban (F.M.S.).and 708 acres are under
rubber. So far 279 are in bearing, so the
estimated current crop of 100,000-odd lb.
is only a fraction of what should be got
later on. So far £27,129 has been spent on
development, and £3326 on buildings, plant,
etc. Owing to last year’s adverse condi-
tions, and because of the further £5169 laid
out on development, finance is not so easy as
it was: —•
1917. 1918.

Liabilities . . . . 1311 7586
Surplus assets .. £2394
Excess liabilities .. £4765
If everything works out all right during the
current year there should be a big recovery
in the co.’s position. And as the market im-
proves, which it certainly should do as the
world gets back to normal, shareholders
should soon see some fine returns for their
long wait. So far they have had only two
5 p.c. divs.

What a money spinner it has been! But
it was not always so. The story is told of
how at one time early in the co.’s career—
it is now in its 40th year—it was doing so
poorly that the bank had grave doubts about
getting back much of the money it had
advanced to the concern. In desperation
the bank demanded a change of manage-
ment, and at the instigation of one of the
bank directors F. W. Browne, of the McCul-
loih Carrying Co. of Vic., was put in charge.
Browne is said to have promptly requested a
further overdraft, which a nervous bank
directorate granted in the hope of seeing its
other advances made good. Very soon the
fortunes of the co. looked up; and when a
little later Browne again tapped the bank,
still further assistance is said to have been
almost cheerfully given. Not many years
later the bank had got every penny of its
money back and the co. began to prosper.
Go back to only the beginning of the table.
Capital was then £BO,OOO. Since, £14,000
of new money has been got in (£4OOO of it
as premiums on shares) ; but meantime,
after transferring £35,000 of reserves to
capital and paying a regular 10 p.c. on the
increased capital, £19,701 has been tacked
on to reserves apart from the £35,195 of
internal reserves which have been revealed.
And there is, no doubt, still a substantial
bit up the sleeve which the directors could
shake out if they cared to do so. Now, on
top of all the sweet picking's shareholders
have had, comes a cash bonus equal to one-
fifth of their present paid capital. There
are very few cos. which can show such hand-
some results from the recent difficult years.
This one, however, continues to progress
rapidly. Gross earnings for the Jan. half,
1912, were £31,812: exes., apart from depre-
ciation, £23,242. For the past six months
the earnings were £47,522, and exes. £32,619.
Meantime the balance-sheet figures have
moved in this way:—

has been put into the co.’s Victorian busi-

Deduct the £14,000 got in by the new share

years.

high-grade steel industry in the Common-

installed at works at Alexandria (near Syd-
ney). Since the war, because Sheffield could
no longer draw pure iron from Sweden for
the old crucible methods, the electric fur-

The latest copper-smelting plant to be erected in
Australia is that of the Mount Cuthbert Company
in North Queensland. It consists of buildings, power-
plant with boilers, generating sets and blowers,
blast furnace, copper converters and converter re-
fining plant, blister gathering and cleaning shed,
also steel bins for ore and coke. Wherever possible
the plant is electrically driven. The whole of the
plant was supplied by the Austral Otis Engineering
Co. of Melbourne, who have made numerous smelting
plants working in Australia at present.— Advt. [2

steelmasters throughout the world have
adopted the process for their high-grade re-
quirements. The first six months of the Aus-
tralian Electric Steel enterprise were spent
in experimenting and tooth-combing the cony
tinent for supplies. Then Descliamps got to
work in earnest, and the results of the past
IS months are probably unparalleled in Aus-
tralian industry. The co. has been supply-
ing the Broken Hill Prop., Mount Morgan
and other mining cos. and numerous indus-
trial concerns with machinery parts of a
quality that previously could not be obtained
outside Sheffield. That is a big claim to
make for the little concern whose staff has
been developed from Australian workmen, of
whom there are now 130 employed; but
official tests have shown that the quality of
some of its products are actually superior to
the highest grade previously imported. Plant
has, of- course, been enlarged considerably;
but the co.’s success has been so phenomenal
that to cope with the business which threat-
ens to come its way capital is being in-
creased fourfold, and, besides enlarging the
Sydney works, a foundry is to be established
in Westralia. All the new capital is being
put up by present shareholders. It is, how-
ever, admittedly impossible to manufacture in
Australia at the same cost as in cheaper-
labor countries. The awakening of labor in
Britain may level up costs to some extent,
but the only sure way to retain this and
other Australian industries is by a scientifi-
cally-adjusted tariff. Except in one or two
isolated instances, all high-grade steel pro-
ducts are at present admitted to the Com-
monwealth duty free.
» * * *
Minister for Mines Fitzpatrick called all
the N. S. Wales copper cos. into conference
last week. It was a brave thing to do,
because it was apparent the Minister would
not get rid of them without facing a demand
for assistance. Replying to the conference,
Acting-Prime Minister Watt made a state-
ment which bears out what this paper said
last week. According to Mr. Watt the
“situation in relation to the production,
treatment and sale of copper seemed to be
generally misunderstood.” Further, it is
apparent that there is no desire on the part
of those advising him that it should be other-
wise than “misunderstood.” Until the War
Precautions Act is abolished the truth in
connection with this metals business will not
come to the top, and when it does Mr. Watt
may possibly be a much-amazed man. He
added:—■
Encouraged by the Government, va-Sb development
has occurred during the war, and several branches of
the industry have been preserved and established.
Copper works have been extended and new plants
installed.
All camouflage! Certainly the Port Kembla
refinery has been extended, but the old, reli-
able and cheap-working E. and A. copper
refinery at Waratah (N.S.W.) has been
forced out of business through the methods
of the Government’s advisers. Despite
the war prices of copper, the small pro-
ducer has been knocked out entirely, and
although Kembla now has the whole of the
refining business in its hands it has been
compelled to pay off some of its employees.
Our present production is at the rate of
about 16.000 tons copper per annum, where-
as the normal pre-war production was 43,000
tons. From the day war was declared, in
face of the high prices ruling, the copper
production of Australia has been seriously
declining, and the causes are chiefly political.
Not only is the primary industry of mining
the ore being knocked out, but such of the
secondary copper industries as have been
started are taking the count because of
American, Japanese and Canadian competi-
tion. If the Federal Government does not
see to the tariff without delay, Mr. Watt must
cease talking about the development of in-
dustries, because the unemployed alone pr.ove
how absurd is the claim. To further justify
the copper position, the Acting-Prime Min-
ister dragged in a lot of irrelevant matter
concerning silver-lead and zinc. In due
course the present Government will strike
trouble with the smaller producers of these
metals also. Most of them went out of
business some time back, but they have not
forgotten exactly why they fell. Apart from
putting up cash to tide the copper-mines
over the present trouble (which is a shuffle-
some way of doing things, anyhow) the
Federal Government can give a certain
amount of relief by at once clapping on a
Protective duty in respect e£ all copper
material coming into the country. The
relief would have an immediate cash value,
inasmuch as those interested would at once
know that at least our own market was
being reserved for our producers.
* * * *
The Electrolytic R. and S. Co., Port
Kembla (N.S.W.), has struck industrial
stagnation that threatens to make that big
concern very sick. In fact, unless arrange-
ments are made to lift some of the enormous
stacks of refined copper at the works (which
doesn't seem probable), it looks like a shut-
down. A few years ago a co. known as Metal
Manufactures, jutd., secured a site adjoining
the Port Kembla works, with the object of
manufacturing all kinds of copper goods,
from rivets to electrical apparatus. This
concern is buttressed by the Electrolytic Co.,
but has experienced bad luck. One consign-
ment of machinery was sent to the bottom
by a torpedo, and owing to war circum-
stances it has been impossible to secure other
machinery required to get properly going.
So far an excellent quality of copper wire
has been turned out, but further develop-
ment is held up for want of other machinery.
But even with these works in full swing it
is estimated that not more than 5000 tons
copper per year would be required. As the
capacity of the Electrolytic works is about
80,000 tons per year, and the average Com-
monwealth production about 40,000 tons, it
follows than either the manufacture of fin-
ished articles here must be greatly extended
or an export trade fostered to keep the in-
dustry going.
* * * *
So that N. S. Wales Mines Minister Fitz-
patrick might better understand the copper
position, it was decided at the conference of
N. S. Wales cos. last week that the manage-
ments should provide him with “confidential
estimates” of costs. But why "confidential” ?
Unless a management is guilty of extrava-
gance, there should be nothing to hide in the
matter of costs. Though copper cos.’ annual
reports are generally loaded with figures,
Wallaroo and Moonta (S.A.) is about the
only concern which gives a straightforward
statement of all costs. These drawn-blind
methods are only playing into the hands of
the Bolshie. On the other hand, a complete
statement of costs would show Labor what
a big proportion of the value of the product
it takes even under normal conditions.

With a little two-head battery and an
otherwise inefficient plant, for several years
Tungsten Mines, Ltd., has been working a
wolfram-sclieelite proposition near Frogmore
(N.S.W.). From 6584 tons ore assaying
2J2 p.c. 101 tons concentrates worth £15,868
were got. But no less than £lB,OOO worth
of the metal meantime found its way into
the tails. With a better plant it is reckoned
that at least half of this leakage could have
been saved. So a new co. is being formed
to take over the show. Nominal capital,
£30,000 in £1 shares ; 12,000 fully-paids and
£5OOO in cash to the vendor; 18,000 con-
tribs. offered to the public, to be called up
to Bs. to start with. An outlay of £l5OO-
- in plant is reckoned to put the show on
a 150-ton per week basis, which with a 75 p.c.
recovery from 2.66 p.c. ore should, with the
product at 525. 6d. per unit, give a net profit
of £4BO per week. If this all works out as
well as it looks on paper, there might be
dividends equal to 166 p.c. p.a. on the con-
tribs. To go on with, Mines Dept, geologist
Harper thinks well of the show r .

Brok-en Hill South is going into the Tas-
manian electrolytic zinc business. A meet-
ing of shareholders will be asked to sanction
the investment, which the directors will
recommend. It will take about a million to
complete the works at Bisdon.

The sky is again cloudy at Daylesford
(Vic.), for the Ajax Central shoot at 1500 ft.
south looks like being of the squib type.
The stone came up into the drive, and car-
ried good gold for a few feet. Then sand-
stone split it in two and now has wedged
the reef in twain, though one run of quartz
carries gold. The intrusion was not alto-
gether a surprise, for deep mining on the
field throughout has been a thing of shreds
and patches. But everything on the Ajax
line hangs on the Central turning up
trumps. Ajax, working from 700 ft. to
400 ft. on the run of gold from which it has
been forking out sixpenny divs. monthly,
now has the battery playing to a sdwt. tune,
which of course isn’t good enough. So a
rise will go up north at No. 5 level to see
what is overhead, and there won’t be any
dilly-dallying over getting the No. 4 cross-
cut out to see if the shoot lives to that f x>mt.
These contretemps have upset 'he barrows
which the Nuggetty crowd were trundling
next door, for if big Ajax tumbles their cake
is poor dough.
* * * *
“Greta”: It took a lot of prodding to get
a move on at all with Peacock, Bowser or
.Lawson over Vic. brown coal, and now even
the specialists selected for the Board aren’t
of the material to make things hum. They
are Prof. Lyle, a Metropolitan Gas director;
George Swinburne, another old gas man;
and A. McKinstry, oerhaps the best qualified
of the lot, though he comes from the British
Westinghouse Co. The best of the State’s
electrical talent has been passed by to give
jobs to the crowd that were mixed up with
' Hughes and Higgins on the Federal Advisory
Science Board or munition metals stunts.
There isn’t a mining engineer on the Board,
though if electricity is to be got from brown
coal cheap mining is the factor of prime
importance.
* * * *
Perth Mining Association is overwhelming
the West with proposals for reviving the
industry. Its officials have been worrying
the Mines Department for months with a
scheme for cutting up the £50,000 voted by
Parliament to assist production. A lot of
peonle are anxious to get their teeth into
this grant, and as usual the least deserving
are reaching out with both hands. When
the Government was in* funds battlers apply-
ing for small amounts were often turned
away practically empty-handed, yet cos.
which had become hard-up through paying
dividends (often before they earned them)
managed to get what they wanted. This
sort of thing has happened over and over
again, and is likely to happen in the future
unless someone steps in and prevents it.
The Association has also butted in with the
object of having prospecting and mining in-
cluded in the Commonwealth scheme for the
repatriation of returned soldiers. About
13.000 men volunteered from Westralian
goldfields, yet no provision has been made in
the Act for assisting them on their return.
The only jobs offered returned miners and
prospectors are poultry-farming, bee-keeping,
selling sandwiches, or assisting poverty-
stricken cookies on the dismal wheat areas.
* * * #
Poor ore and water have been the troubles
of Lyell Blocks (Tas.) for so long that the
time has come to try another string.
So Frank Duff’s cupriferous clays—how
he used to roll the phrase out in the
’nineties! —are to be given a trial.
From the old 550 ft. level bores proved
that the clays lived down 40ft. under-
foot, and a hunt is to be started at 630 ft.
for them. Manager Carroll works out that
from the 440 ft. level of the old shaft down
179 ft. he ought to get 100,000 tons of clays.
As the old bores showed 3.5 p.c. copper and
as the material mined before was worth 2i
p.c., he accepts the latter value. If his
figures tot up all right he will have 1750 tons
copper to send to market. That at £BO per
ton would bring in, say, £140,000. If he be
right, there can be no question that it is
well to take a cut at the clays. But then
come in the factors of expense and the price
of the metal. To work the clays will not
be costly so long as the ground will stand,
and treatment charges should be easy. But
even so, it is all a forlorn hope. Carroll at
present is cleaning up at the I.looft. and
1200 ft. levels. He was driven out at the
1300 ft. by the water that burst through
when the No. 25 bore went into con-
glomerate at the 1200 ft. north drive. He
did 2000 ft. of boring during the past six
months only to disclose promising prospects,
so the most determined sticker to the Blocks
is pretty well satisfied that so far as deep
mining goes the property is only a stringer.
Last halt-year 872 tons were broken, assay-
ing 8.8 p.c. copper and 5.30 z. silver per ton.
The only pity, is that there was not ten
times as much, as it cost £7322 in calls to
win it and do development work. This is
what Lyell Blocks has cost the faithful: —
Mine account, £233,631 ; plant, etc., £27,010 ;
development, £77,788. And not a taste in
return. Last half’s loss of £493 has swol-
len the P. and L. debit to £11,859. At
Dec. 31 there was £6482 in liquid assets, and
liabilities amounted to onlv £695. The
300.000 255. shares have now been called up
to 235. 9d. *
* » * *
Etheridge (N.Q.), run by a Melbourne
crowd, is in a bad way. The belated figures
to June last were laid before shareholders
this week, but work has been suspended
since the middle of December. The co. has
been battling with exceptionally hard ore in
a 24in. lode, and sliming is necessary to
release the metal. Costs have been soaring
(wages have increased more than 25 p.c. in
three years), and labor has become scarce.
Consequently snly one shift was worked
during the year and costs ran away with
675. 6d per ton crushed. The mill gave
36S cons concentrates worth £10,665 from
5408 tons ore assaying from 18 to 23 dwts.
This showed a working loss of £424.
Including administration exes., the co. went'
£7OB to the bad. All the ore got out came
from the old Queenslander, in which potter-
ing around disclosed an unknown quantity
of pay ore in the collapsed workings. At
June 30 there were 2125 tons of 20dwt. stuff
in sight in this mine ; 3700 17dwt. stone in
the Havelock ; 3000 tons worth 15dwt. in the
Big Reef; and 500 tons worth 21dwt. in the
Nil Desperandum. Finances look sick;
at June 30 there were liquid assets of
£3210, and £4638 was owing to sundry
creditors. The 120,000 10s. shares are
fully paid.

There has been a boom in saltpans all
through northern Victoria during the last
year or two, and salt-shovellers were busy
this summer—till the February rains flooded
them out —on the beds of salt-lakes of all
sorts and sizes, from Lake Tyrrell down to
stray lagoons of a few acres in the Mallee
About 2000 tons were scraped off one pan
near Swan Hill, and as it was of extra
quality the holder was looking for £3 a ton.
Cloncurry claims to have the largest iron
deposit in Queensland. Mt. Leviathan,
opposite the town, is reckoned to hold 10
million tons of ore above plain level.

HOW TO RELIEVE STOMACH PAINS
IN A FEW MINUTES.
Nine times out of ten when your stomach
hurts, when you are doubled up with the
pains of dyspepsia and indigestion, it is
simply because of acid which burns and
inflames the delicate stomach lining, and
fermenting food which forms gas that dis-
tends the stomach just like blowing up a toy
balloon. In cases like this—and most all
stomach trouble is directly or indirectly due
to acid and fermentation —almost instant
relief may be obtained by taking in a little
hot water, immediately after eating or
whenever pain is felt, a half-teaspoonful of
Bisurated Magnesia, obtainable of any
chemist at small cost. This simple remedy
may seem old-fashioned, but those who have
used it say that besides being inexpensive,
it beats the expensive remedies they have
tried for quick and positive relief. Try it
and see.—Advt.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Hardy old ex-Governor Macgregor has been
seriously ill, but is making good again at his
home in Scotland. The old man has been
hard hit by the war; he has lost, among
others, a daughter, who worked in the hos-
pitals in France; a son-in-law, Sir Alfred
Paget; and the only child of his favorite
niece, killed in an engagement with the
German raider Moewe.
The British “Governor of Germany.”
General Sir Charles Ferguson, who is to
administer the occupied Rhine provinces
from headquarters at Cologne (and stands
about as good a chance of getting assassi-
nated over his job as any other living man
who could be named), was closely associated
with Australia in his earliest days. Prob-
ably the first recollection he has is of a wild
night ride on a trooper’s saddle, through
suburbs of Adelaide, to the bedside of his
dying mother, wife of the State Governor.
The widower soon married an Adelaide girl,
although she was engaged to another (one of
S.A.’s most prominent citizens of to-day),
and made a dramatic finish long after in a
West Indian earthquake. The Fergusons
have a way of getting there. The Geteral’s
younger brother James, who was born in
Adelaide, commanded H.M.S. Thunderer at
the Jutland battle, and is now a rear-
admiral. A cousin is Administrator of
Llong Kong. Another, who chose a different
path, is Anglican parson Severn, well known
up and down the Murray in S. Aus.
The case of Justice Street, who has just
stepped down from the N.S.W. Supreme
Court bench for six months’ spell after 12
years’ hard work, makes some people wonder
whether success at the Bar ought to count
in the choice of judges. Street has been a
great success on the bench ; yet his practice
at the Bar' Was small—if it hadn’t been he
would no dotibt have turned down the offer,
as others, did, of a six months’ judicial job.
He had the temperament; but it was sheer
luck that made a vacancy on the bench before
his six months had expired. If it had
occurred before he had had the ehfince to
show himself in the temporary job whicli
others wouldn’t take, at least half \a dozen
men would have been better fancied.'
No Australian who wasn’t born in Vic.
has yet become an Anglican bishop in his
own country; and two of the three Vic-
torians have come from Geelong.
Big James Gibb, of Glenroy (Vic.), gone
out of the sheep business forever, started his
climb to fortune at Berwick, where he bred
Ayrshires at what is now Melville Park,
owned by Andrew Chirnside. He owed his
success to good luck as well as good man-
agement. The Gippsland railway, in a
deviation which cost the State many un-
necessary thousands, was taken through his
property. The estate was thereupon sold
for a large sum, and a big deposit paid.
And almost at once the boom broke and left
the buyer on the rocks. Afterwards the
property was sold at £2O an acre to Sam
Fandango Mackay, who in turn passed on
the land to Chirnside at £39. Gibb sat in
the Assembly for some years ap member for
Mornington.
Died in Sydney, after an operation,
WiLiam Henry Carver, who was the busi-
ness man behind Wolseley, the inventor of
the modern sheep-shearing machine. For
40 years he had been one of the managing
directors of Dangar, Gedye a,nd Co., which
still markets the Wolseley and Lister
machines. Mr. Carver was to have left for
Europe by the Osterley; and his sudden ill-
ness and death robs the State of one of its
soundest yet most genial business men.

Major Shillington — Chaplain-Captain Wilson.
“At Landing”: The woman who landed
on Gallipoli (B. 27/2/Y9) had a sad mis-
sion. She was Mrs. Doughty-Wylie, widow
of Colonel Doughty-Wylie, V.C., of the 29th
Division, who was killed about a week after
the Landing. He was buried at Cape
Helles, and his widow went ashore there to
place a wreath on the grave.
A third auction sale of the late Dean
O’Hea’s immense landed property at Coburg
(Pentridge Stockade region) has just been
held. In this mere remnant there were no
less than 166 homestead lots, with still
more to follow. Mother Church was the
rich old Dane’s legatee, as with Backhaus,
of Bendigo.

A wounded Billjim from London :—•
I was waiting for the lift in Australia House the
other day with half a dozen other soldiers and twice
as many civvies. Presently down comes the lift, and
in steps the most insignificant-looking civvy. I
tried to follow’, but was pushed back by a horrified
attendant. I had almost intruded on the Prime
Minister of Australia! P.S. —I was in hospital blues
and on crutches.
Another Smith has come to the top in
South Aus. This one is G.J., lately Goods
Superintendent of State railways, now made
chairman of the Wheat Board, to replace G.
11. Prosser, resigned. He is 57, Northum-
brian by birth, but has been in S.A. since
childhood, and his whole’ life has been given
to railway work. His brother is Mayor of
Pori Adelaide.
“T.8.L.”: “Smith’s” question about the
only woman to land at Gallipoli (B.
27/2/Y9) reminds me of an Anzac yarn. A
Turkish sniper was giving our boys par-
ticular hell. After a few days’ observation
two Billjims located the “possie.” They
wont over the top that night, and a few
hours later returned with a very surly
Jacko. A little later two heroes were ask-
ing a very perplexed O.C. what they were to
do with her.
The long arm has been at work again.
During a thunderstorm in the Northam
(W.A.) district George Wilson, 25, while
driving a five-horse team, was struck by
lightning and killed. The two leading
horses in the team were also killed.
Another young man of 23, named Adlum,
likewise carting wheat with a team of five
horses, reached his end at the same time in
the same way, together with the two leading
horses of his team.
“8.0. N.”; Billy Hughes, in his most
waspish mood, rang up the chief clerk (he
is head now of a Commonwealth Depart-
ment) to deliver an official instruction.
After vainly trying to shriek down the
noise of the “buzzer,” Billy broke into gib-
bering rage, and threatened to visit the
G.P.O. and tear down the lines of com-
munication. When peace reigned again, he
said : “Did you take a note of what I said ?
Where were we?” The unperturbed official
repeated a string of blistering words.
“That was the last I heard definitely, sir,”
he replied.
“O. Blin”: Mrs. Woodrow Wilson is the
widow of one Galt, a Washington jeweller,
and she caught. Woodrow by her bouncing
physical attractions at a time when he was
looking for some distraction from the tyranny
of his restless intellect. Mrs. Wilson is still
a handsome woman, whose husband could
never be expected to notice an ominous chin-
line owing to the lady’s fondness for alluring
pink roses under large shady hats. She is
reckoned the best-dresser in Washington, and
has ndded prestige to the position of Presi-
dent s lady. Wilson’s enemies spitefully
whisper that she drafted the famous Notes—
whicn inspired the wicked witticism that
counterfeit Notes had become a State mono-
poly. It is certain that she has a large in-
fluence over him.
Dr. Donaldson, Anglican Archgaiters of
Brisbane, on being released from quarantine
intoned sweetly:
It has been an experience. I cannot pretend that
we are not glad to be out of it; and we have all
returned home with a definitely enhanced appreciation
of a comfortable bed, a clean dinner-table and a long
bath. We had, it is true, some luxuries which the
boys lacked.
But, my dear brethren, we must bear our
luxuries bravely. They are sent, so to
speak, to try us.
Among the expatriated Australians taken
oft' by the ’flu outbreak at ’Frisco was a
much-travelled Victorian —Harry Madden,
whose dad, the late Henry, Madden, is re-
membered by another generation of pre-
Federal politicians as housekeeper at State
Parliament House. The younger Madden
started his world-whacking career as a
Broome pearler. Afterwards he took in the
’Frisco earthquake, had various experiences
in the China seas and remoter parts, and
finally settled in California, where lie mar-
ried an American girl.
“T. 0.1.” : Up to and including Sir George
Gipps, all Governors of N. S. Wales were
soldiers or sailors, and depended chiefly on
their Imperial pay. Fitzroy belonged to
a ducal house and married into one, so we
will assume that lie had a private income.
Denison, an engineer officer, was well in.
Sir John Young had extensive estates in
Ireland. So had Belmore, though he took
the Governorship while the estates were
being “nursed.” With the help of cabbages
grown at Moss Vale he cleared off the en-
cumbrance. Hercules Itobinson was neither
poor nor wealthy, though he did maintain a
fine racing stable. Loftiis was poor, always
in debt, and badly treated by the British
Government after long service as ambassa-
dor at several Courts. Carrington, like
Belmore, was nursing liis estates. Jersey
was rich, Duff poor, Hampden comfortable,
Beauchamp wealthy. Bluff Harry Rawson
had an Imperial income to back up his local
screw. Chelmsford married a wealthy wife.
Gerald Strickland had an old estate and had
married into the peerage. He was fairly
well-to-do, but no spendthrift.
“Physiognomist” discusses the most-
talked-of man:—
Lips usually open —credulity; love of the marvel-
lous. When closed, the corners droop—grumbling,
discontent. Projecting lower lip—habit of tafu.
Large projecting teeth—self-esteem (yet. not quite
so large as in the case of the late President Roose-
velt). Flat cheeks with hollows —poor digestion.
Retired JUDGE DEA T NISTON, Chairman
of 'Maoriland's Epidemic Commission.
Full eyes-—an abundant vocabulary. Eyebrows raised
—little concentration of the perceptive powers.
Large ears —obstinacy. Good forehead —gobd mental
powers. Rather projecting and pointed nose—curi-
osity. Many straight lines in face —lack of humor.
With these factors, we can interpret the personality.
Having little sense of humor, the subject takes
himself veiy seriously; with small perceptions and
large credulity, he will depend on the report of
others. His long head and correlated horse-like
face make him slow in coming to conclusions; yet
the projecting lower lip and full eyes keep him
talking all the time. He wants to do right, and
his self-esteem and obstinacy will make him sure
that what he thinks —for the time being—is right.
Given time, and handled by an expert, such a mind
could wander all round the compass of opposing ideas
with positive assurance. He can be too proud to
fight; eager to fight; and then an apostle of non-
resistance. He may preach disarmament and then
order the biggest navy ever constructed. An enemy
to secret diplomacy, he will join in the most
important debate for the world’s future with closed
doors. Having no deep, self-thought-out convic-
tions, he sways with weathercock facility to crowd-
suggestion. Beside Lloyd George, Balfour and Clem-
enceau, he is an intellectual infant, and given time
his susceptibility to suggestion should mould him
to their will.
THE WOMAN ACROSS THE WAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
*fle woman across the way is glad; she has decked
her window-blind
And trellised her garden porch with flags and a
banner of welcome designed—
But the ship that is bringing her son to her has left
my son behind!
Long hours since, when the day was young, she set,
with her face aglow,
The finishing touch to the lettered strip and the
flaunting portico,
And frets with her gloves and her bonnet-veil, and
waits for the time to go.
Somebody paused by her gate just now, gossiping
cheerily;
Out of the gossip there flung these words, “His ship
comes in at three.”
She smiled; and the joy within her smile struck
through the heart of me.
How should she know that I who sit, feeling the
dreadful dart
Of envy deep in my mother-soul, here in a room
apart.
Record in the lines on my furrowed face the lines
within my heart?
Armies and kings, and the strange, mad world that
kings and armies sway,
Strive, and we care not why they strive —such is the
woman’s way !
Only we know that the lives we love stand as the
pawns they' play.
God, who is wise, should know; and yet a sad and
a soulless thing,
Tangled and reasonless, it seems, that a woman’s
pang should bring
Babes to the world to grow and go stark to the
slaughtering.
Why do I seek (as a woman will), perverse and
stubborn-necked,
Vainly to probe to the heart of thijigs men’s selves
can scarce dissect,
Daring to match with their ordered; minds my patch-
work intellect?
Spartan mothers, perchance, would smile; and where-
fore then should I
Mope in the shade of a curtained pane, with leaden
lids and dry,
Feeling this awful envy cling, that gnaws and will
not die!
Yet how shall I reason, while through my brain the
blind thoughts reel and blur?
What can your rusted phrases give, your sophistries
aver
To woman who bears the pain, and find pain’s
guerdon snatched from her?
Soon he will be with her, bright of eye, stalwart
and service-browned ;
Rumor of laughter and song will flow over and
through and round,
From night till dawn, these poor thin W’alls that
cannot shut out sound !
And I, because of her happiness on whom God looked
and smiled,
Will cower and nurse in my froward heart my way-
ward thoughts and wild,
And gather the sheets about my ears, and wince like
a beaten child!
The woman across the street is glad: her house with
flags is gay!
If only the tears would blind my eyes, they would
blot the sight away !
I almost find in my heart to hate the woman across
the way!
VICTORIA.
J. ALEX. ALLAN.

“Dale”: Bill was sick of it and wanted
to be sent back to Australia after con-
valescence. But the examining M.O. was
a hard nut, with a strict sense of man’s
responsibilities. All the blokes before Bill
had been marked for light duties or return
to the Front. When Bill’s turn arrived he
besought a private word with the doctor.
It was declined. But he urged his case
with such persistence that he secured the
removal of the other men from the room.
Then he explained his matrimonial complica-
tions to the M.O. “They’ll pinch me for
bigamy if you send me back to Australia,”
he urged. That settled it. “Return to
Australia,” was the prompt verdict. “Of
course, I only got one wife,” Bill told us;
“but I knew the, old ——
* * * •
“Iris”: Clear mental vision during periods
of public danger and disaster is given to
very few people on this old planet. In the
pathological experiment to which we have
been subjected recently in Sydney, there is
no possible doubt whatever that there was a
distinct tendency for medical thought to be
distorted and its judgment warped—a sort
of mental metamorphopsia
had occurred, if I may be
permitted to borrow an opti-
cal term. Metamorphopsia
in its visual sense means
that objects looked at be-
come distorted and bent out
of shape, so that telegraph-
wires appear crooked. In
addition there is sometimes
magnification of objects
viewed. So with the medical
mind during the recent epi-
demic. It couldn’t think
straight. It was biased by
what had occurred in other
lands under different social
and climatic conditions. A
disease that obviously in its
onset and terminations was
of the mildest possible
nature was judged by the
warped professional mind to
be a visitation of the most
disastrous and pestilential
kind; and the public has
suffered greatly in pocket and in body from
the blunder.
«-.***
“Moana” : The ancient Maori practice of
depositing on Henare’s grave all his house-
hold furniture and effects was revived at
New Plymouth when natives lately went
west via the ’flu. The cemetery soon
began to wear a gala look through being
decorated with Maori mats, nearly-sealskin
coats, sewing-machines, pink chiffon frocks,
gramophones, green silk handkerchiefs,
musical-boxes and gay things of that kind.
The accumulation was causing those respon-
sible for the good behavior of the burial-
ground a lot of sleepless nights, for it is
not easy to be rude to people about their
dead relatives; but when a funeral proces-
sion hove in sight lugging an ancient piano
and an iron bedstead, which it intended to
plant on the grave of the deceased, authority
rose up and asserted itself. It told Henare,
in language quite unpoetic, that a cemetery
was a place for planting the dead and not a
second-hand dump. The decision inci-
dentally solved a problem that was causing
a riot in a Maori family. A relative own-
ing numerous golden sovereigns had died,
and the family was debating whether the
cash should accompany the deceased in his
coffin or whether they should cut it up.
When they heard the decree that only the
dead were to be planted they suddenly
stopped fighting and said “Py korry, Kapai 1”
“Copabella”: A mixed squad of Aussies
and Tommies was on guard duty in London.
A carriage rolled by, and the guard saluted
lavishly. “Who’s the bloke?” asked a
Digger of a Tommy mate. “Oh, that’s
King George,” replied the awed English-
man. “Oh, King George !” retorted
the Digger. Tommy thought awhile in
amazed silence. Then came to him in a
flash the retort crushing: “And Ned
Kelly, too!” Then he carried on calm in
the conviction that accounts had been
squared;.

“Silas”: A steam road-roller bogged in
Canterbury-road, Middle Park (Melbf), one
morning a week or so back, the front half-
section sinking so far in a soft patch as to
give the roller a cant that strained its intes-
tines. There had been strenuous efforts to
get the mass to move, when a little man with
whiskers came along. He was pushing a
pram with small twins in it. He was a very
proud little- Whiskers, possibly by reason of
those twins, and he may have weighed Bst.,
twins and all. He paused to look the
trouble over; then gravely he set his shoul-
der to the inert iron. “Now,” he said, “all
together!”

“Henry Ilorsecollar”: A Northern Terri-
tory correspondent tells me that much of
the disaffection there is over small things—
the small things that count. For instance,
Darwin is a capital city without a water
supply, though there is abundance available
by gravitation only 16 miles away. There-
fore there is no gushing fountain in the
street, and the stranger who doesn’t want

visit to a Darwin Federal pub in summer.
The bar abutted on the street as if there was
a land famine. It left an impression of bare
boards and fried galvanised-iron. There was
a hard chair to sit on, a bare table to sit
at and a drink of dubious quality minus ice
to sit in front of, and the service was done
by a man who might have been the member
for the city of Indifference in the State of
Donkerdam. And I thought of a pub I had
met in a much worse town in a hot Spanish-
speaking land where ; space was equally
cheap. It had a big walled courtyard w’ith

“M.S.”: Arbitration Courts and the like
ought to protect unions which show some
regard for the public convenience; but they
don’t. The Merchant Service Guild pati-
ently waited years after its last award
ran out before the Arbitration Court could
find time to listen to it. Then it was told
that it would be impossible to make an
award retrospective, though the owners
expected it would be. And it had been done
for the more militant cooks. The Sailors and
Firemen’s Union made much clamor, conse-
quently it got a variation of award and a
big rise of screw before the last award had
half run its time. This after having held
up not only our coastal ships but transports
and hospital ships in a strike not 18 months
before! Now they are not striking, but
merely refusing to work. As I write half
a dozen ships are tied up in Brisbane while
the individual fireman tries to make profit
out of the ’flu scare. It’s not a strike; the
Union secretary merely wants to confer with
the Shipping Controller about something.
All the same, the man who is willing to work
is a scab. These wasps have come to the
conclusion that the meek don’t inherit the
earth or the sea. Whose fault is it?
Largely, I say, the Arbitration Courts’.
The bigger the nuisance you make of your-
self, the better your chance of getting a lift
from them.
# * * *
Victorians are showing a tendency to
cling to single-blessedness longer than they
used to. From 1881 to IS9O, of every 1000
women who said they would 642 were under
the age of 25, but for 1917-18 the number
per 1000 was only 496, which means a
corresponding decrease in potential births.
Of the 9506 brides- in 1917, 1008 were
between 30 and 35, 527 between 35 and 40,
and 246 between 40 and 45. There were
1579 bridegrooms between 30 and 35, 843
between 35 and 40, and 416 between 40 and
45. No male abandoned bachelorhood before
he was 17, but five undertook to keep a
missus apiece at that age. There were eight
brides aged 15, and 35 a year older. The
fancies of seven males over 75 lightly turned
to thoughts of matrimony, and 17 took the
plunge between 70 and 75. No bride could
be induced to admit to 75 springtimes, but
three confessed to over 70, and a dozen to
more than 65. Two damsels under 25
became the darlings of dashhounds of ,65 and
>7O, and four girls between 45 and 50 kid-
napped youths in their early twenties —a tri-
bute to their enterprise in a State which
possesses 68,240 more females than males.

The most belated young man in Australia
turned up in Melbourne the other after-
noon. He was found wandering in the
Town Hall and confessed to being of Gipps-
land origin. “Well, what, are you seeking
here?” he was asked. “I was thinkin’ of
enlistin’,” he replied, “and they tol’ me this
was the bookin’-office.” “But,” he was
assured, “the war is over ” The Gipps-
lander seemed a bit surprised. “Aw, well,”
he said, “y’ better take me name and address.
Y’ might have another.”

AT CLOSE OF DAY.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
She lies so sweetly at the close pf day
In her soft frame of whiteness and of lace,
When all day’s turbulence is put away,
And all the world has left us for a space,
That I’m content. The thought to me is wine:
“This is my wife, and all her heart is mine.”
Yet through the day my soul was sore with doubt,
And jealousies have bitten -at my heart.
Watching her lip 3 in little flirtings pout,
In little talks with Smith, or Brown, or Smart,
I’ve railed upon her as a flirt of flirts ;
But still she smiles, nor cares how much it hurts.
My jealous heart begrudges her the love 1
She gives the world. My soul is all aflame.
To some love-island with her would I move.
But when the world is gone, she calls my name,
And says.... Oh, jealousies are swept away—
She lies so sweetly at the close of day!
Francis.

Really good Whisky should
possess the virtues of
MATURITY & DESIRABILITY
Maturity. —Age ever did command respect. In
Ce war's to-day is to be found all the experience
of many generations in the arts of blending and
distillation, which has for its end the production
of a whisky—always ripely matured —worthy
of its universal designation as the Supreme
Spirit.
Desirability. —Charm and desirability always
go hand in hand, for without charm nothing
'is desirable. Dewar's is a fountain of fascin-
ation —-its triumphant quality persistently
pleases but never palls. The matured merits
of its magnificent mellowness may be enjoyed
year in and year out with never a trace of
tiredness of taste.

Hobart tradesmen were furious at the
idea of barring mainland visitors during the
‘Hu season, holding that the city’s prosperity
depends largely on the affluent excursionist
from over the strait. A motto for Hobart:
“All’s well that spends well.”
Australia has to pay for giving itself and
the world the poisonous idea that its people
are poor creatures only fit for rough coolie
jobs. Keith Murdoch, writing to the Sun
from London the other day, said what every
Digger knows to be the truth:—
The earliest Anzacs will remember how, during
the training days in Egypt, and the days of Gal-
lipoli, all crimes were put down to the Australians,
and they were even accused of crimes they never
heard of. It was the set policy of the British com-
manders: British prestige must be maintained in
these dependencies, and as Australians were birds of
passage, here to-day and home to-morrow, they must
take the discredit of anything that offended the
natives. That is notorious throughout the A.I.F.
But if British officers thought that the good
name of Australia didn’t matter, Australia
being a country of sundowners and bush-
rangers, it might anyhow have been ex-
pected that Australian newspapers wouldn’t
throw the same sort of mud. Yet a week
or so ago, when there was a row in London
among the Americans, Australian papers put
up these headlines ; “Soldiers’ Riots —Melee
in the Strand. —Australians take part.”
Gradually the Diggers faded out of the
subsequent accounts until only one was left
in, and he was alleged to have been taken to
hospital. And finally even that solitary
Digger was scratched, and the truth was
told that not a single Australian had been
either arrested or injured.
“lI.McK.”: Here and there you get a
travel-tainted Digger. There was all the
usual excitement of a mother and an assort-
ment of sisters awaiting the return of big
brother Bill. I hadn’t had the pleasure of
meeting Bill, but the family assured me he
was a “typical Australian.” They agreed
among themselves that Bill must have
shaken up old London and- Australianised it.
Then he arrived —a fine big fellow. Frantic
sisters made a rush at him, and he gathered
them all to him. “Ba'i Jove!” he drawled,
“it’s priceless seeing you girls again!”

* * * #
When recently heard of, the party which
is surveying routes and supply stations for
the London-Melbourne air service had been
doing four miles per hour per swift motor
over the rich, lonesome alluvial plains about
Blackall (Q.). It isn’t a bad pace for these
parts, where road metal is almost as scarce
as inhabitants—especially it isn’t bad after
rain. A little further on the Winton-Hugh-
enden express restricts itself to about 15
miles an hour even in dry times, for if the
earth is shaken violently it cracks, and if
the crack is too big the train might fall in,
and no one knows how far it might fall.
Next year the organisers of the new service
hope to travel aloft where the rich alluvial
habits of the country won’t trouble them and
the permanent way doesn’t crack or slide and
the speed is the same uphill and down. i

“H.R.L.”: The South Melbourne kid had
been riding free on the back of the tram for
10 minutes when the conductor grasped his
collar and hauled him on to the plat-
form, remarking, “Now you can come for a
ride right to the terminus! Perhaps the
walk back will teach you to knock off this
game.” On reaching me end he said, “Good-
bye, son. Hope you enjoy your walk
home.” The' kid screwed up his face,
rapped out: “Rats to you ! I’m home now,”
and was out of the danger zone before the
conductor could kick.
“Mayne Stay”: “J.D.” (B. G/3/T9),
speaking of the mercantile marine officer
and discipline, says the officer “is accus-
tomed to handle Lascars, Dagoes, Swedes
and a particularly miserable type of Brit-
isher.” Somewhat unpromising material,
yet even “J.D.” acknowledges the job is
fairly well done. But there is no “disci-
pline” about it. Discipline infers training
on both sides and, for the officer, power to
enforce obedience. It’s the knowledge that
the power is there and will be used to the
limit that makes the discipline in navy and
army. % In the mercantile marine there is
no discipline and officers are not trained to
.handle men. They painfully acquire the
knack—-if they have it in them. If not
they drop out. The flatfoot is usually
caught young. In sailing-ships the older
shellbacks exnlain forcibly at first (and his
own commonsense teaches him later) the
need to jump at an order for the common
safety. Further, lie sees it is the mate
who stands by the sheets when it’s “Clew up
the foresail” and the second mate who jumps
aloft first when it’s “Up and furl.” So he
acquires some respect for an officer and is
disciplined to a certain extent. But with
the firemen and many of the steamboat
sailors there is none of this. The man on
top is the boss who can give him the sack
JIMMY’S JOB.
“How about going up and getting dressed?”
“In a minute. Wait till Jimmy finds the key of my locker .”
at the end of the voyage. But as he doesn’t
intend to stay longer, in any case, what’s
the odds? True, if he gets drunk he can be
fined ss. If absent without leave he for-
feits a day’s pay. But it’s only when the rest
of his money is blewed up that that worries
him. One sometimes marvels that the mer-
cantile marine gets on at all. The politi-
cian has a kindly eye on the unionist vote.
The shipowner is not interested so long as
the ships keep moving. When he has deal-
ings with the union he only comes in con-
tact with clean, sober and plausible leaders,
who would give one the impression it is a
union of angels. Meanwhile the 1.W.W.-
One Big Union idea of going slow and
worrying the boss is growing all the time,
and the lives of officers and engineers are
made a misery.

“William Wardroom” : I put in nearly four
war years and know how valuable the
R.A.N. ships were to this (apparently) in-
different continent. For weeks Yon Spee’s
squadron was in Australian waters, and
nothing but our one big stick, the Australia,
with her 12-inch guns, saved Sydney from
bombardment. We at sea had the privilege
each night of hearing the Hun ships queru-
lously asking each other where that thrice-
damned battle-cruiser was. So our navy
was not born a second too soon. But the
service even then was seething with discon-
tent, and most of the lower deck swore that
for them at any rate there would be no
more sea when the war was over. In most
cases the cause was not so much the tardi-
ness of promotion as the shocking inade-
quacy of pay. Admiral Henderson was
brought out to suggest a scheme for a locally-
owned navy, and. after studying Australian
conditions, also formulated a scale of pay.
The small coterie of Saturday-afternoon
sailors known as the Navy Board afterwards
reduced this by 25 per cent. Promotion
did not seem to worry many, because the
increase of pay was infinitesimal and the
increase of responsibility considerable; in
fact, several of the senior lower-deck ratings
would have actually lost money by accepting
warrant rank or even commissions, bearing
in mind the increased cost of living in an
officers’ mess, the cost of uniforms and the
loss of marriage allowance. I knew an
officer, 28 years of age, who did most of the
de-coding and confidential work of the ship,
in addition to his ordinary job, to whom the
fatuous Navy Board awarded the princely
sum of 10s. a day. Out of this .he
had to live as a gentleman, buy uni-
form, feed himself, and provide for a
wife ashore! Most of the officers in
the ship in which I served got consider-
ably less than the average miner, even when
he voluntarily puts himself on the .darg, and
the fresh-caught subaltern in the A.I.F. was
a bloated capitalist beside these men, who
had begun their job as cadets at 13 years of
age.
“Sgt. S.”: Re The Bulletin’s remark
that if the League of Nations permits Ger-
many to still hold and fortify Kiel Canal, the
decision may some day be a precedent justi-
fying the U.S.A. in fortifying the Panama
Canal. I don’t think the U.S.A. will bother
about a precedent or any other old cobweb.
In December of last year they had in the
Canal Zone guns from 14in. down to one-
pounders, 15 submarines (some of them the
very latest), with accommodation for 40
more, a flight of Curtis flying-boats, several
airships' and captive balloons as well as
mines and shore torpedoes. The guns and
depots are well sited, and the general scheme
would win the admiration of the keenest
engineer. We were shown everything at
Colon and Cristobel, and fSted in Panama
City. They ran a special for us and placed
cars at our disposal, a full band played us
through the big locks, and, as the pilot was
kissing the skipper good-bye, four tugs loaded
us with oranges, bananas and magazines
sufficient to last us to Australia. In all
their excellent arrangements, through which
they were to sell millions of “dallars’ ” worth
of stores to passing vessels, the authority
made only one bad guess. John Bull quietly
countered by establishing his own stores,
docks, etc., in the Indies, supplying his own
and friendly boats, at cheaper rates. Another
case of J.B. getting there without ringing
the bells of Saint Paul’s.

“The Oldest Inhabitant” finds an old
pump:—■
Smart’s Hotel, at the corner of Pitt and Market
streets, in Sydney, has just been bought by Parkes’s
next-to-last P.M.G., C. J. Roberts, who has sold his
interest in the ancient Roberts’s Hotel at the corner
of Market and George. Smarts’s dates back to the first
’thirties, when H. S. Green, who was storekeeper at
Hyde Park Barracks, opened a pub on the corner
and named it The Cricketers’ Arms. The ex-store-
keeper kept it till the mid ’forties, when he died. His
widow worked it for a time as Green’s Hotel. John
Williams, Crown Solicitor, married the eldest Miss
Green. Following Mrs. Green came Edward Boston,
who had presided over many inns in old Sydney. He
was the patron of Queen Gooseberry, and this famous
old black sister died in-his kitchen at the Sydney
Arms in Castlereagh-street. Boston was in occupa-
tion of Smart’s until the early ’sixties, when Charles
Larny renamed it The Frankfort Hotel. He was
there until 1869, when Henry Robert Smart, a
jeweller, came along and installed himself in Smart’s
Family Hotel. Lately a limited co. has worked it.
In its 85 years the building has been thrice re-
modelled.

“Brizac”: Get your hooks into this. For
the past two years a bone-dry youth has been
employed by the Navy Department as a mes-
senger to deliver prodigious envelopes around
the city. Apart from this connection with
the Department he is a universal service
trainee in the It.A.N., and has “H.M.A.S.”
on his cap-band. Since the armistice was
signed this mariner lias been given authority
to attach three blue chevrons to his right
sleeve and has been presented with a badge
surmounted by a crown and bearing the
words “Imperial Service, 1914.” It appears
that he is rated as being “on active service,”
and has received a discharge which entitles
him to membership of the R.S.A., the badge
of which he wears. 1 have known the lad
for years past, and he has never in his life
been outside Port Phillip Heads on any sort
of vessel, let alone a ship of the navy.
* * * *
“O. Pip”: With the repatriation muddle
as a background the attitude of the Vic.
Education Department towards its men
stands out as a bright example of the way
the Aussie might be restored to citizenship.
The majority of the soldier-teachers who en-
listed were in the sixth class on the classifi-
cation roll. They have returned to find
themselves well forward in the fifth class;
and a 50 per cent, rise in salary—scant at
all times —is a common thing. Director
Tate’s generous policy will mean goodwill and
a struggle, anyway, for higher efficiency.
“0.L.” : A medical writer in The Bulletin
(6/3/T9), criticising my proposal for an
inquiry by tribunal, on national life-saving
grounds, into every death that happens, (1)
describes the suggested plan as a “Bolshe-
vik” one; (2) takes exception to my refer-
ence to the “criminall.v-neglectful riff-raff of
the profession” ; and (3) scoffs at the idea
of holding a nurse responsible before the
proposed tribunal for failure in her duty dur-
ing a critical period, “whatever that may
mean.” As to this :
(1) The sea-captain—to continue an analogy pre-
viously used by .me—accepts a system equally Bol-
shevik, doing so under conditions of pay and pros-
pects far inferior to those of many medical men.
(2) Two instances should suffice—(a) that of the
registered medico who; on occasion, wallows in alco-
hol or “dopes” to the danger of his patient’s life,
and (6) that of the practitioner who, against all
the teachings of his profession, is bestially careless
in matters of common cleanliness. Everybody in
the medical profession knows that there are such
rotters in Australian practice to-day, and that some
of them have been directly responsible for prevent-
able loss of life. “Criminally neglectful” hardly
seems too strong a term to apply in such in-
stances.
(3) Surely the writer knows that the actions of
the nurse at a time of crisis—-in such matters as
cool-sponging, for example, or the administration of
a stimulant—are of vital moment to the patient.
Regarding my critic’s prophecy that, under
the system proposed, “it would be unsafe to
practise medicine at all, and the patient
would die without medical or nursing atten-
tion,” the magnificent and self-sacrificing
records of these allied professions, under all
manner of conditions, make such pessimism
quite absurd.
