Davey Hall, who is always put up when
it is hoped to catch the birds with chaff, told
Petersham the other night that it would be
a dreadful thing to have a soldiers’ party.
“What,” he demanded, with his tongue in his
cheek, “is the difference between the rule of
militarism here and the rule of militarism
in Europe?” None at all. But the struggle
of the soldiers for representation in Parlia-
ment is simply a struggle to escape from
militarism and get settled in civil life.
Considering the chronic row about valua-
tions, it might not be amiss to enact that
every advertisement of property for sale
shall include a mention of the value put on
it for tax purposes and the value put on it
for probate purposes, if it has been probated
within the last three years. The advt. might
read like this :
SPLENDID BLOCK, an Unparalleled Sacrifice at
£lOOO (municipal valuation £2OO. Sworn for
probate' in 1918, £6O). Don’t Miss This Chance.
That would at least keep the situation before
the public eye and cause recrimination in
the local council, which is usually too peace-
ful, and it might make the Government look
up the valuer who swore to the £6O, especi-
ally if he happens to be also the man who
did the £lOOO valuation.
No wonder the shires are always on the
cadge to the State Government. Some land
in the Turon (N.S.W.) Shire was lately
looked at with a view to pur-
chase for a soldier’s settlement.
The value was declared to be
from £37 to £4O, an acre. Yet
the shire was rating it at £4.
One of the queer things in
connection with the League of
Nations is the renewed talk
about a Japanese base in Mex-
ico. Mexico has a large and
fairly warlike and intelligent
brown population, and having
no nope of ever being a really
white, country it doesn’t mind a
few more browns. It has no
allies, though it has a very big
candid friend on its border, and
it wouldn’t mind buying a
strong new friend. When the
deal was proposed years ago the
U.S. fell on it like Wackford
Squeers, and forbade it as con-
trary to the Monroe Doctrine.
Mexico is no party to the Doc-
trine, and, anyhow, the Doc-
trine only says, and has said
volubly, for 96 years, that the
U.S. will regard the acquisition
of further territory in America
by any European Power as an.
unfriendly. act —it says nothing
about Asiatics, who weren’t
thought of in Monroe’s day.
Wilson was recently questioned
on the matter. He was moved
to say, though not quite in these
words, that if the League of
Nations couldn’t be persuaded
into falling like an avalanche of
elephants on Mexico’s right of
self-determination in this matter, the U.S.
would see the League dodgasted first.
By 216 votes to 41 the U.S. House of
Reps, has over-ridden President Wilson's
comfortable halfway-house doctrine that Ire-
land alone, among white-man countries,
should be refused that self-determination
which inevitably means an Irish
Republic. Presumably the Sen-
ate will go the same way, for
the same party captured both
Houses last November. And
that presumably means that the
League of Nations won’t be
accepted with Ireland left out.
Edmund d-e Valera’s appoint-
ment as President of the Irish
Republic seems to be coming
true. '
The sudden closure of Aus-
tralian copper-mines, which em-
ployed 8512 workers in 1916,
begins to be a calamity. In the
last year before the Avar re-
duced imports, this country paid
±4-19,970 to foreigners for cop-
per wire and pipes and copper
plates alone, and the local man-
ufacture of these goods would
supply work at £4 a week
to 2115 men. Probably
allowing for miscellane-
ous . brass and copper
articles imported it
would be possible, under
a strong Protectionist
tanft, to employ the
whole 8512 men in
copper-mines and cop-
per manufactures, even
ir the outside market closed for good.
The simple fact with regard to copper is that
during the earlier part of the war, when the
had to get the metal at any cost,
American producers, who absolutely con-
ro led the situation, put up the price sky-
£i 9 (^ e av erage for 1914) it
an to £loo 10s. There is no metal in the
world whose output can be so quickly in-
creased, partly because there are thousands
ol low-grade copper shows which are worked
only when the metal is high-priced. The
output in this case bounded; and for vari-
ous reasons---some of them discreditable—
tile .British Government went on buying
until to-day it has £30,000.000 worth in
hand Ihe war price was wholly fictitious:
and there cannot be even a normal market
until the accumulations are used up.
1 robably, as the biggest holder of stocks in
the world, the British Government doesn’t
want copper-mines to work, and won’t will-
ingly do anything to give them a free market;
and that doesn’t improve the outlook. If
any Australian Government gives any
assistance at all, it ought to be only on a
price-basis which would still leave opera-
tions at many mines unprofitable until costs
are greatly reduced.
In Wullie Ividston’s day, 12 or 15 years
ago, the Queensland Government ran to two
motor-cars. Denham increased the number
to three. Now there are 01. The taxpayer
still has the privilege of running behind.
The N. S. Wales Government last week
appointed J. S. Marks chairman of the new
Public Service Commission at a salary of
£2500 plus a pension of £6OO a year; and as
Mr. Marks is already 59 and must retire at
65 he seems to have made an excellent bar-
gain. There were numerous applicants, but
Mr. Marks wasn’t one of them. He was,
however, “a schoolmate of Mr. Fuller,” and
Mr. Fuller is Chief Secretary. Until a few
months ago Marks was manager of the City
Bank in Sydney; then he became general
manager, and, on the absorption of the City
Bank by the A.8.C., he became chief in-
spector. His whole working life, until quite
lately, was spent with the City Bank, which
was the least progressive bank in N. S.
Wales, if not in Australia —though that
probably was not Mr. Marks’s fault. The
new man may be a genius at organisation;
so far, however, he has had no opportunity
to handle any kind of a staff except a few
bank clerks, who are not the most trouble-
some people in the world.
John Storey, leader of the N. S. Wales
State Labor party, has been writing to the
press about the rotten finances of the State.
John Storey’s party was in power when there
was perpetrated that incredible imbecility or
swindle, the Norton Griffiths capitalistic
Private Enterprise loan and railway con-
struction contract, and John Storey’s crowd
voted solidly for it after secret debate (it
would be interesting to know what was said
at that debate) in John Storey’s caucus.
Though the party had split when the burst
and the stench came, John Storey, as leader
of the Opposition, couldn’t say what he
should have said because lie was in
the responsibility from his boots up toHiis
hair. So he denounces the State’s rotten
finance in general terms without allusion in
detail to the Norton Griffiths affair. Truly
the front of John Storey is high. He is
John the Skyscraper.
“Fighting and wars are wholly contrary
to the Christian faith,” says Archbishop
Clarke, of Melbourne, in a Lenten pastoral.
Then there is a misprint in the office Bible
which reports that the sons of lieuben
“made war with the Hagarites,” of whom
“there fell down many slain, for the war was
of God.”
E. E. Judd, who has told Australia
frankly what “direct action” means, scorns
AN EXPENSIVE FLIRTATION.
the Labor party’s objective of State owner-
ship. “State ownership,” he says, “strength-
ens the position of the capitalist class.”
Also: “Governments, acting as executives of
the capitalist-class State, invest the capital-
ists’ money in State-owned industries and
guarantee them a return on their money.”
It is a clod that the Labor party has well
earned. But there was a time when the
party didn’t propose anything of the kind.
It proposed then that the State should own
the industries and instrumentalities —that it
should pay for them, little by little. But
once in power it saw an easy way to buy
votes. It therefore adopted the old policy
of borrowing, and carried it further than
any bloated Government of capitalists had
ever dreamed of doing.
THE LEAGUE OF NATIONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Light on the towns and cities, and peace for ever-
more ! . .
The Big Five met in the world’s light as many had
met before,
And the future of man is settled and there shall be
no more war.
The lamb shall lie down with the lion, and trust
with- treachery;
The brave man go with the coward, and the chained
mind shackle the free,
And the truthful sit with the Har ever by land and
sea.
And there shall be no more passion, and no more
love nor hate;
No more contempt for the paltry, no more respect
for the great;
And the people shall breed like rabbits and mate as
animals mate.
For lo! the Big Five have said it, each with a fear-
some frown;
Each for his chosen country, State, and city and
town;
Each for his lawn and table and the bed where he
lies him down.
Cobbler and crank and chandler, magpie and ape
disguised;
Each bound to his grocery corner—these are the Five
we prized;
Bleating the teaching of others whom they* ever
despised.
But three shall meet in a cellar, companions of
mildew and rats;
’And three shall meet in a garret, pungent with
stench of the cats,'
And three in a cave in the forest where the torch-
light maddens the bats —
Bats as blind as the people, streaming into the
glare—
And the Nine shall turn the nations back to the
plain things there;
Tracing in chalk and charcoal treaties that none can
tear:
Truth that goes higher than airships and deeper than
submarines,
And a message swifter than wireless—and none shall
know what it means —-
Till an army is rushed together and ready behind
the scenes.
The Big Five sit together in the light of the World
and day,
Each tied to his grocery corner though he travel
the world for aye,
Each bleating the dreams of dreamers whom he has
despised alway.
And intellect shall be tortured, and art destroyed for
a span—
The brute shall defile the pictures as he did when
the age began ;
He shall hawk arid spit in the palace to prove that
he is a man.
EARTH-RISE FROM THE MOON.
Gobbler and crank and chandler,. magpie and ape dis-
guised ;
Each bound to his grocery corner —these are the
Five we prized ;
Bleating the teaching of others whom they ever
despised.
Let the nations scatter their armies and level their
arsenals well,
Let them blow their airships to Heaven and sink
their warships to Hell,
Let them maim the feet of the runner and silence
the drum and the bell;
But shapes shall glide from the cellar who never had
dared to “strike,”
And shapes shall drop from the garret (ghastly and
so alike)
To drag from the cave in the forest powder and can-
non and pike.
As of old, we are sending a message to Garcia still —
Smoke from the peak by sunlight, beacon by night
from the hill; »
And the drum shall throb in the distance—the drum
that never was still.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

COUGHS ARE DANGEROUS.
USE TAR AND SUGAR TO GET QUICK
RELIEF.
Coughs and colds are dangerous because,
if neglected, they so frequently lead to more
serious complaints. Pneumonia, bronchitis
and lung trouble would be almost un-
known if coughs and colds were taken
in hand in time. To be sure of get-
ting prompt relief and to prevent
danger of your cough becoming deep seated
you should always keep on hand a supply of
bitrate of tar cough syrup, which can easily
be made at home for a nominal sum by dis-
solving- half a pound of sugar in half a pint
of hot water and then adding two ounces of
bitrate of tar. Stir thoroughly, pour into a
pint bottle, and when cool it is ready for
use. One or two teaspoonfuls of this simple,
inexpensive home-made cough syrup taken
every hour or two will quickly relieve coughs
and colds, loosen the phlegm, and clear out
the head in a most astonishing manner
Tickling throat coughs may usually be stop-
ped by slowly dissolving in the mouth a
lump of sugar on which you have first put
15 to 20 drops of bitrate of tar. Re sure to
ask your chemist for the bitrate of tar, as
this is a most suitable form for use in cases
of coughs or colds, and is also very pleasant
to the taste. — Advt.

“Glen Huntly”: Have just made the
acquaintance’of a photographer in the person of a
large Murray cod. On cutting it open, found
on a white slimy fluid in its innards an easily-
recognisable representation of the tree under which
it was caught. A fisherman friend tells me this
is no new discovery—that the cod makes a habit
of taking snaps of its dwelling-places and storing
the negatives in its interior. I’m told these photo-
graphs can be developed by being placed on a
piece of cardboard and left in the sun to dry. But
my informant is a fisherman.
*** * *
“March Fly”: Tell “Gouger” (B.
13/2/’l9) that his bootlace was not being tugged
by the bald-headed Calvinist who informed him that
snowed-urider sheep breathe easily through the
blanket. . It happens every year in the hills
around Methuen (M.L.) that jumbucks are snowed
in several feet deep, and often they reside in their
dugout for weeks without suffering any harm. The
only danger is that, if they are huddled together,
they are likely to breakfast on each other’s jackets.
In that case' the fluffy stuff curls into a ball in
their innards, blocking the gangway and bringing
about death. A sheep will hang- out on as little
as cousin goat. I knew of a ewe which, as the
result of a flood, was for 18 months stranded in
the Ashburton (M.L.) river-bed. For a year and a
half the only item on Mrs. Sheep’s menu was gorse,
and she was rearing a fine family on it when she
had to come home.
* • * * #
“Mookingah”: Peter Halfpenny, out in
the Delalah Downs open country, cutting scrub for
bis working oxen during the recent drought, caught
82 wild dogs in one month, worth £1 per scalp.
Peter ran out of tucker and put in the last two
'lays on pigweed and mulga. Smith, on Thirloo
Downs, caught 45 dogs in one night at Osaca bore ;
scalps £1 per head, bonus £2 per dog—a neat
£135 for one night’s work. But don’t rush it: the
stations have stopped the bonus.
“Tim Buektoo”: West of Winton (Q.) a
gargle costs a bob. A pound-note buys 20 drinks;
but if three thirsts breast the bar and place thereon
a half-crown, it buys the three drinks. This is
the “Barcoo shout.” Anybody know its origin?
“0.L.”: As long as one is alive there is a
chance of being carried off by flumonia or phthisis.
Either may be my own fate; but I have every
reason to back up “Chloe” (B. 27/2/’l9) in that
lady’s advocacy of the “sluicing” habit for prevent-
ing nose, throat or pulmonary disease. For the
past five years, following the advice of a bloke
from Outback who only caught cold when lie came
to town, 1 have got rid of possible germ-colonies,
after being'in doubtful company or in closed rooms,
dusty streets or among the wrong sort of smells, by
snuffing up and blowing out a few handfuls of plain
water —or, on special occasions, a weak solution of
soda bi-carbonate and boric acid. Salt, as recom-
mended by “Chloe,” doesn't suit everybody, because
in some subjects it causes irritation. In the old
days my “colds” lasted a fortnight or so; now-
adays l hardly ever get one—and never a real
“barker.”
“Death Trail”: “Poison” (B. 13/2/T9),
who oiled his chin to remark that although ho
had used phosphorus baits for the past 20 years
for rabbits he had never known native birds to be
poisoned thereby, must have operated where birds
don’t exist. I have been in different country, and
I can assure him that 1 have, by phis; horus, un-
willingly killed hundreds o; magpies, .kookaburras,
soldier-birds, butcher-birds and others. I’ve since
chucked the poison-cart, believing it to be a. sure
destroyer of the best friends that the man on the
land has.
THE BREACH.
“ Haven't seen you with Linda lately. Haven't parted, have you?"

“Chris” : Quandong is a question of atmo-
sphere. In town a squashy tomato would beat it
hollow. But come on it suddenly in the bush,
miles from the Dago store, and it becomes a peach.
Near Oodlawirra (S.A.) “wild peaching” furnishes
the week-end frivol. Masked with cream the stewed
“peach” is not bad. Eaten raw it is as interest-
ing as a Moreton Bay fig. Has anyone tried to
grow a quandong in a Melbourne or Sydney garden?
It would make a fine show on any front lawn.
* * * *
“Werrimee”: The duck-shooter meets with
some annoying surprises at times. I had a freezing
swim after a bird in a swamp. When I emerged
from the water I threw the game down on the
grass. I had carried it by a wing in my mouth,
and it didn’t stir a muscle on the journey; but the
instant it touched the ground it gave a flutter and
flew away blithely, laughing at the top of its voice.
“FY7”: “Gecko” in saying in a recent
Bulletin that some Australian snakes are cannibals
is only partly right. None of our snakes is known
to be an habitual swallower of his brother, like the
Indian hamadryad or the black snake of North
America. Both these find their chief food-supply in
the family, so to speak. But any snake will eat
another if occasion offers, especially in captivity. A
snake’s teeth do not allow him to let go once he
has taken hold, and the only thing for him to
do if he gets an accidental mouthful of his missus
or his mother-in-law is to go right on and hope for
the best. A python in Adelaide Zoo some years
ago got accidentally started on a woollen rug. He
carried on till the rug was stowed away, but he
didn’t long survive the meal.
* * * #
“Melos”: There are as many flavors in.
bush honey as there are colors in our flowers. The
eucalypts supply the greater part of the bee food.,
especially ironbark, grey-gum, stringv-bark and
flooded-gum. Spotted-gum yields heavily, but the
nectar turns into a somewhat inferior honey. Ti-tree
is another heavy cropper, but honey made from its
bloom is strongly flavored and rather unpleasant to the
taste. Bees sustained on this diet are notoriously
argumentative during “robbing” operations. Some
idea of the value of our native trees to the honey-
maker—-on the North Coast (N.S.W.), at any rate —
can be gauged from the fact that, during normal
times, an ordinary hive obtaining its supply from
bush blooms alone makes on an average 3lb. of honey
per day during the season.
“Gouger”: The Jap up north looks
around for a game with money in it. Then he goes
quietly to work to improve on the white man’s
method. Take beehe-de-mer fishing, for instance.
The old fashion was to chew cocoanut and squirt it
on the water; this, stilling the ripples, enabled the
diver to see the shallow bottom and the sea-slugs
lying there. The Jap soon made a pair of goggles
which, fitting tightly over the diver’s eyes so as to
exclude the water, enabled him, by swimming with
his face under water, to see the sea-slugs on the
bottom at a much greater depth. As the fleets
skinned out the sea-slugs at the shallower depths the
Jap made an oblong box, with a strong glass fitted
into the bottom. One man rows the dinghy, while
the other puts the glass end of the box under water.
As soon as a “patch” of fish is located the cutter is
signalled. With this box arrangement it is possible,
under bright sunlight, to see the ocean bed at a
depth of 20 fathoms—too deep for the boys to dive.
Then a 21b. lump of lead is tied to a long, strong
fishing-line. Pitted into the lead is a big fish-hook,
straightened out. On top of the lead is tied a
white rag, because at 10 fathoms the lead cannot be
seen. One man swims close by the boat, bolding the
lead and part of the-line in his hand. The rest of
the line is paid out by the boat-rower. When a sea-
slug is seen the line is dropped, and immediately the
white rag shows the harpoon to be -straight above
the sea-slug the line is slackened suddenly. Eight-
een fathoms, an extreme depth, has been worked
successfully by this method.
“G.C.”: After amusedly watching me for
about a week trying to catch Murray cod, the old
fisherman gave me some advice. “Mister, if yer
only here to pass the time away, vcr having a great
holiday; but if yer want to> catch some fish,, go
home and take off those pretty white clothes of
yours and put on some black ones.” I took his
advica, and next day, in the same hole, caught
over a hundredweight, including a 25-pounder. The
old fisherman gave me 2d. a pound for them, and
sold them straight away to some Chow market-
gardeners. He told me he had sold them nearly
two tons of cod this season. *They smoke them.
* * * *
“Frank R.”: The small boy in Rananaland
with the empty kerosene-tin and the hurricane-lamp
who used to make good pocket-money during Janu-
ary catching cane-bettles, which were purchased by
the farmers’ associations at Gd. per lb., lias bumped
against opposition from the ibis, magpie-lark,
yellow-belly, leatherhead, butcher-bird, minah, satin
bower-bird and the laugliing-jaek. The first two
are by far the most numerous, and have the advan-
tage that they follow their prey to the ground if
they fail in their attempt to secure it on the limbs
of trees. Most of the birds are too small to swallow
the beetles at one mouthful, so they beat them to
pieces on the branches before attempting to devour
them. Now that the birds are assisting to exter-
minate the" pest it’s up to the Queensland Govern-
ment to protect them from the youth with the pea-
rifle.
“Sieve Hart”: It has been pitiful to see the
birds, bees and fish, not to mention stock, dying in
thousands out in western N. S. Wales for want of
water. Met an emu within half a mile of a town t’other
day in a netted lane, and* tried to steer the dinkum
Australian to a hole of water I knew of; hut the
bird was too weak, and dropped exhausted. While
considering whether to put it out of its misery n;y
nomination for the mean-man motored up. “Got a
water-bag?” I asked. “I want to give this chicken
a drink.” “Yes, I’ve got a water-bag,” said the
road hog; “but not for those blanky things. Knock
him on the head and chuck him up here. He'll
make a feed for the dogs.” I was saying some
earnest words to him when he yelled, “Oh, go to
Hell!” and shot on.
* . * * *
“Steve Hart” : Will someone tell me why
lightning will strike a team of horses and pass a
bullock-tenm, though they may be close together ?
It cannot always be the harness or shoes, as I have
seen unshod horses struck in a paddock in which
sheep and cattle were also running.
* * * *
“Moana” : An eel with good claims to the
heavyweight championship has been bagged after
the manner of big game at Kaiuma (M.L.). A cocky
hearing a splashing in a small creek went to investi-
gate and found a couple of fair-sized eels on the
grass making hurriedly overland for foreign parts.
Their pursuer, who had got into shallow water and
was letting the whole world know it, was the gran’dad
of the eel family, and at first sight of him the
investigating cocky imagined that the sea-serpent had
arrived at last. The cocky reckoned a rifle was the
best weapon, and in consequence the eel was
accorded the honor of being shot. He measured
20in. round the body and tipped the scale at lcwt.
* * * *
“Leo Lear”: Have any Bananaland abos.
seen the dragon-bird, otherwise the noisy pitta,
lately? You may get a glimpse of them if you can
imitate the “whil-vchit-wheeeoo” and keep still; but
at most times they dart through the tropical tangle
like rabbits through bracken. Pittas wear a varie-
gated gown, but dark-green and black predominate;
there’s a blue patch on the shoulder and a scarlet
one underneath. If you find a large stone sur-
rounded by broken snail-shells in the scrub you
are fairly safe in betting it belongs to a dragon-
bird. Snails are their chief food, and each bird
seems to possess his own chopping-block.
* * * *
“G.C.”: Do sheep always travel faster
against the wind than with it? I know of a mob
that did 15 miles with almost a gale blowing
against them. When the wind was with them they
did only 10 miles in the same time. It was through
irrigated country; with plenty of feed and water on
both sides of the road. Incidentally the best sheep-
dog the drover of this particular mob had was a
greyhound.

“Ivonini”: W. Vella, of Maoriland, who
claimed the shearing championship of the world last
year after stripping 339 sheep in nine hours, has
gone one better. This time his tally ran up to
347 jumbucks in nine hours. Square and all,
according to the manager of Taihape station, who
did the checking.

BEYOND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Beyond the dismal sandscape
Lies the Land of God-Forgot,
Where the ice-cream does not flourish,
And the counter-lunch is not.
There is sand and there is sorrow—
Yea, there’s soreness of the eyes,
With a scarcity of oysters
And a plenitude of flies.
And every pain and every ill
That mortal ever got
Have nibbled at my vitals
On the track of God-Forgot.
Beyond the fringe of mulga
. Lies the Land of Dreadful Thirst;
Where the wild bull-camel rageth,
And the camel-man accurst
Smiles sleek behind his whiskers
As he contemplates the scene
Of a weary swaginan tramping
Down the track of Nevergreen.
But the language of the swag'gie
Is unfit for me to quote,
Though it seems to fit the climate
Of the Uttermost Remote.
Beyond the final gum-tree
Lies the Land of No One Knows;
Where , the spinifex and saltbush .
And the stunted samphire grows ;
Where tin* emugator creepeth
And the kangarooster flies,.
Where the jillawarra sleepeth
And the .fierce geramel cries ;
Where the whatsit and the whoyoo
And the whatyercallum grins
At the chap who humps Matilda
As a penance for his sins..
At the back of Jumbuck station
’Tis a pleasant thing to rest,
And review the situation
As the sun sinks in the west;
And it helps a man’s devotion
When he kneels at evening prayer
If the ants are in his whiskers
And a scorpion in his hair;
When a snake crawls up his trousers
You can het he’s proud and fond
Of living “close to Nature”
In the uttermost Beyond !
TOM BEASLEY.

“Firepot”: Here’s the approved method
for fixing- leaks in tanks which defy solder. (tot
a piece of calico or hessian, smear thickly on both
sides with fairly thin white-lead, paste over the leak.
If a large hole, plug with this material first, then
plaster over with another mixture of fairly thick
had. I’lie patch will adhere firmly and usually out-
lasts the tank. 1 need hardly remind you to use
Australian white-lead.

“Eloc": We were camped on the Moonie
(<t'.) with 5000 starving wethers. Handy to the
camp was a Chinese garden Where plenty of fowls
were kept. The fowls always roosted in small scrubby
trees. Somebody suggested that a quango of diet
wouldn’t hurt anyone. Black Harry, the cook,
said. “Me get, ’em!’’ That night he sneaked over
to the garden, sawed a shrub off at the butt, and
carefully inarched back to the camp with a bouquet
of five sleeping ehooks. Asked where he learnt
Hie trick he said, “Me bin traekem for Xew South
Wales police.’’
“Nymboida” : Some time ago on the
Clarence (N.S.W.) we watched some thieves at work
for better thieves. The river was full of mullet,
and about, oO divers or shags came after them,
feeding. But a crowd of pelicans were' hungry, too.
A shag would dive and come up with a fine big fish.
But as sure as lie did so an old pelican would go full
speed after him. The shag would flutter along as
fast as lie could, but in pretty nearly every instance
the pelican pouched the fish.
“D.F.”: Collins, Lieut.-Gov. of Victoria,
was, I think, the first official friend that the native
birds had in Australia. On November SO, being-
then camped at Sorrento, he observed in a general
order that many persons were daily bringing birds'
nests into the encampment containing either eggs
or young, unfledged birds. Wherefore:—•
It is necessary to prohibit a practice at once so
cruel and so destructive; any person found offend-
ing against this order will be punished.
Maybe it’s the weakness of old age that makes
bird protection in Vic SUch a farce now.
* * * *
“E.S.S.”: As a rule the so-called black
duck (which isn’t black) makes its nest in deep
rush-beds or in thick grass ; but occasionally it
leaves the marshes and reedy swamps, even when
these offer the most excellent facilities for nesting,
in order to lay and sit in hollow limbs of trees. I
saw one ih a dead gum, 60ft. from the ground, a
notable departure for the black duck, which other-
wise never alights in a tree except when wounded..
When the young are hatched the mother carries them
down one by one in her beak. The clutch is usually
about 10. Each little one hides away as soon as
it is put in the water, and when the last one has
been brought down a soft call-note from the
mother brings them all to her Side, and . she swims
proudly away to some feeding place. Even when
freshly hatched the ducklings show all the instinct
of an old bird, their sense of danger being remark-
able. One moment they may be seen clustered
about the parent and the next they have disappeared.
At the approach of an enemy the old bird whispers
a warning, which brings the progeny to attention;
it danger threatens, the next injunction is more
decisive, and the family vanishes without asking
questions. If the group is suddenly surprised, and
the mother is consequently in a desperate hurry, she
speaks in a quick, loud voice and smacks the water
with her wings before taking flight. The infants
dive smartly, and thereafter they remain invisible
until the mother returns to the spot. After alight-
ing on the water she twists about for a little while,
taking observations; then at a soft call the ducklings
appear instantaneously from half a dozen directions.
The only infant prodigy in birdland that can beat
the wild duckling for sense is the young scrub-
turkey, which has to battle for itself from the time
it leaves the egg.
“Half-moon”: While anchored inside the
mouth of the Small River (Cape York Peninsula)
recently, I saw an aboriginal crone bitten bv a
death-adder. The gall bladder of file slain worm
was promptly extracted by the bucks (the bite was
scarified meanwhile) and the contents rubbed into
the wounds. Immediately after treatment Mary
resumed her interest in my tobacco pouch. If, in
such cases, the attacking reptile escapes, which isn’t
often, Dr. Binghi has a reserve and effective herbal
remedy.
* * * *
“Gypo” : What is the lung disease that
kills horses on a sea voyage? Some time ago our
transport took over 800 army prads from Queens-
land arid N. S. Wales to Bombay (base for Meso-
potamia), and 17 of them died on the water, 16
snuffing through lung trouble.

“Nymboida” : Saw it years ago from the
balcony of a Casino (N.S.W.) pub. School had
just closed and the street was full of children,
when suddenly a big' red bull tore round the corner
(he had broken the fence of the bull-paddock), bel-
lowing and tossing his head. The youngsters broke
for shelter and all got clear but one poor little
thin boy of about eight. The bull aimed himself
at the boy, and we groaned as the victim was tossed
in the air. But it was a comedy after all. The
bull was very wide between the horns, and, as he
raced on, tossing his old head and looking for
more sport, we picked lip the yelling kid. There
wasn’t a scratch on him.

“Ida D.”: Biflghi’s words describing
water were very euphonious. “Coongboongboot.F is
water rippling among the stones. Can you not hear
it? “Kamballee” is “near a lake,” “minya-intha-eigi-
lona ’ is a waterfall on the Barwon. It means,
“What are you crying for?’’
* * * •
"Centurion” : I came across this yarn the
other day in an old book on Barb and Arab horses,'
by W. J. Gordon. After the Russc-Turkish war a
Jacko general presented to Sir Richard Airey a
horse which had carried the news of the Russian
repulse at Silistria to Turkish headquarters, a dis-
tance of 90 miles, without a stop. The rider died
from the effects of the journey. The prad was said
to be a pure-bred Arab. Airey sold it in England to
Earl Spencer, who showed him at Islington, under the
name of Omar Pasha. There he was promptly pro-
nounced a Waler by dozens of Indian cavalrymen.
They were correct, but how he found his way into
the Turkish cavalry is not explained.
* * * *
“Nenthorne”: If you, drive out from
Mcßae’s Flat (M.L.) you come in 20 miles or so
to the old Nenthorne goldfield, once the scene of an
Otago gld boom. To-day nothing remains of the
short-lived township of 3000 people except the
Croesus shaft-head and part of the mill, together
with a few stone foundations, overgrown heaps of
jam and beef tins, etc. At the Dunedin Exhibition
of 1890 one of the “star” attractions was a small
battery, at work crushing Nenthorne ore, with the
treatment complete to the recovery of the melted
bar of gold. Danny (now Professor D. B.) Waters
was in charge of the operation. This active
demonstration of how to do it stimulated the Scotch-
men’s imagination to the extent of many hundreds
of thousands, and a correspondingly deep regret
later on. The lodes were of the gash variety, small
in extent, without persistence, and earned the
description from those who knew them of being “poor
but upright. ’ Rich ore was often obtained ; 4oz. to
Boz. per ton was not unusual, but the quantity was
■very small. The poor old public was as usual
deluded by assays, the sample being in some eases
the whole of the mine. One of the assay tbs who had
a good connection earned the suspicion of genuine
mine-men by the rapidity with which he could
deliver an assay result, and also by the strange cir-
cumstance that he never obtained less than an ounce.
Some cynics once played him a dirty trick. The.'
faked a sample composed of broken bedroom-ware,
mixed with barren schist rock. It was said to
represent an entirely new lode of great promise, and
would he (the assayer) rush the result? An hour
after it was announced that sample so-and-so gave
1.250 z. and a rush set in for the Bedchamber lode.
Otago got such a lesson that it hasn’t had one real
flutter on lode mining since, though it has done
pretty well out of alluvial.
* * * •
“Nymboida”: About 1906 the people *of
Buenos Ayres were made jumpy by thousands of
snakes which swam to land from some large floating-
islands which had come down the Parana from the
equatorial forests in a big flood, and, when they met
the waters from the sea, became stranded. The town
was kept busy doing a sort of sword dance with
snakes until some large men-qf-war fastened cables
to the islands and slowly towed them to sea, when
the waves soon smashed them up and drowned all the
snakes that weren’t up town enjoying themselves.

“Gouger”: The old hatter’s bushy whis-
kers fairly bristled with rage. The camp-oven was
empty. Instead of the lump of fried meat left over
from tire morning’s breakfast there was only a
• eplv-scarred layer of dripping on the bottom of
the camp-oven. A big, fat gohanna, his claws still
greasy with dripping, licked his chops reflectively
and stared at the hatter from under the kitchen
table. “Come out o’ that,” yelled the old man
furiously, “yer dirty, crawlin’, thievin’ skunk!
Call yerself a ganna ; yer dirty, slimy snake! That’s
wot ye are. A slatherin’, sneakin’, wop-eyed son of
a carpet-snake. Come out o’ that while I skin yer
alive.” The hatter made a wild lunge under the
table, but the gohanna, scenting trouble, dodged,
scrambled out through the kitchen-door and swiftly
crawled up a corner-post and climbed out on to the
ridge-pole of the hut. The hatter dashed out after
him, but the gohanna, from his safe vantage-point,
merely cocked his head sideways and looked down
on the old man with a cold, insolent stare. “Yer a
cold-footed coward,” cried the hatter bitterly.
“Shake ther last bit uv fresh meat in ther camp, an’
too damn seared ter wait an’ get what’s cornin’ to
yer. Here I am slavin' ter keep ther home over us,
an’ you doin’ nothin’ but loaf about ther camp, an’
sleep an’ shake ther last bit of fresh beef in ther
camp. Yer dirty, fat, greasy, slop-eared son of a
death-adder; yer puttin’ on more frills lately than
these ’ere returned sojers. Wait till I catch yer.
111 flay yer alive.” Still furious, the hatter retired
growling into the kitchen, while the gohanna turned
to licking his claws with his iong forked tongue,
preparatory to beating a discreet retreat to his
hollow log.
BREAKING THE NEWS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sailor came up from the sea,
A solemn truth to bear to me—
A simple sailor with a tab;
Of how by sun, and salt, and gale
He’d won from some Pacific isle
Where God had cast his ship a while.
The mission-teacher's dear grandchild,
Blue-eyed and sunny, he beguiled.
It was of grandpapa lie told,
Tiie shepherd of a dusky fold
Upon that isle where grievous Fate
Had thrown the sailor desolate.-
“Your grandpapa was bold and true;
lie taught the Sheebmigs wliat he knew.
He won their hearts. At feast or play
He ever was as good as they.
When for a wager they might eat,
No man more resolute at meat.
“At that great final match he ato
A little sheep, and smiling yet
Devoured of taro and of rice
The full of such a basket twice.
His dusky friends the Sheebungs blent
Great honor with their wonderment,
“He only ceased when all was done.
Then Oozoo’s famed feast was begun.
He earned the prize!” By wonder swayed,
“What ate lie?” asked the little maid.
"A mighty eater he, for, ah,
My lass, lie ate your grandpapa !” •
VICTORIA,
EDDYSON.

The Comfort Car.
required”! Naturally Barrett lias some-
thing interesting to say about the eye-causes.
An eve punctured by a fragment of a projectile is
almost'invariably lost. The metal is non-magnetic.
It, is usually situated deep in the vitreous.... it a
projectile enters the head in the vicinity of the eye
in most cases the eye is destroyed. \\ hether from
the velocity or the rotation of the projectile the
bruising disorganises the coats of the eye and rendeis
it sightless.
The doctor did not believe in sending one-
eyed men to the Front. One battler, how-
ever, eluded him. He reached Gallipoli,
where he was hit in the blind eye by a pro-
jectile. Barrett subsequently removed the
Not only had the Australian Defence
Department no use for specialists: it had
none even for dentists. Its failure to pio-
vide them in the first instance is, these
writers mildly say, “difficult to understand.
Actually “it becaffie necessary at No. 1
Generai Hospital to borrow two dentists
from the Maoriland Government, to tit them
out with Bed Cross money and goods, and
in this \Vay to meet informally the difficulty.
Of the Bed Cross and its activities the
authors have naturally much to say —most
of it in high praise, though there is some
helpful criticism as well. And the services
of the Y.M'.C.A. are given the most generous
recognition.
The book (Australian Army Medical Corps
in Egypt, published by H. K. Lewis and
Co., London) is not a model of either concen-
tration or arrangement. But it will serve
very well as a record of a remarkable
achievement, more successful, happily, in its
results than the great enterprise to which
it was supplementary.
The Army that had to be De-
loused
Modern Crusaders, written by Major V\.
S Kent Hughes, M.C., and published by
Melville and Mullen (Melbourne), is a
book which follows the footsteps and the
dust-cloud of the Australian Light Horse
from Egypt to Jerusalem and Jericho.
That trek of some 200 miles in a straight
line occupied years. The remaining
proceedings, which wrecked two Turkish
armies and broke the heart of the Unspeak-
able so that he humbled his fez in the dust
and asked for peace, took less than half a
year; but the book doesn’t deal with them.
The preliminary struggle was through the
shifty desert. The troops—scarce at first
and ill-supplied with, many things—were
their own explorers in a land where travel-
ling meant merely going up a wall of sand
and over the roof and down the other side.
They were to a great extent their own road-
makers where roads blew away as they were
made. They developed the natural re-
sources of the sand as they went along by
sinking wells, which gave few results and
these often salt, so that much of the water
had to be carried on camels; and as the
camel’s load was limited and he had to go
back for a drink himself just as the force
was becoming acquainted with him, troop*
went ahead by small instalments m short
stages. Over and over again it was a case
of taking a strong position in a day, or else
giving up the attack and retiring -A) or oU
miles for a spell because the next supply oi
water was inside the strong position. In
other words, the attacking force had got
so far from the last supply that it was
approaching the point at which the camel
consumed most of his own load. Two cam-
paigns against Gaza failed because night
found the place untaken and it was neces-
sary to retreat towards the source ot
Nature’s brackish cocktail.
But the railway kept crawling up behind,
being built sometimes at the rate of a mile
and a half a day. Distances became shorter
and food and water more regular. A rare
harbinger of civilisation began to appear—
the lice train carrying elaborate appliances
for de-lousing and de-bugging the army, much
of which hadn’t slept under a roof, let alone
a roof with a bath-tub. for lialf-a-year. A
pipe line was laid, and water was pumped
out of Africa. So at last the army climbed
tlie northern bank of the river of sand and
rocks, after 3f years’ crossing; and when it
got where there was hard ground and some
roads and a fair amount of water, and had
been rested and really de-loused and de-
bugged, its progress was of the whirlwind
order. Some 3500 years ago Tliothmes
111., the Foch of his day, sent his army by
sea to Northern Palestine and cut out the
desert road, but there were no Hun sub-
marines about then.
The Major supplies a readable book and
the numerous photographs are honest, and
haven’t been faked by the artist who takes
20 years off his client’s age for a considera-
tion.
A Satchel of Books
The Twin Ideals, by Dr. James W. Barrett, of Mel-
bourne (H. K. Lewis and Go., London). The writer
describes these two solid volumes as a collection of
“essays, memoranda, articles and letters,’’ and they
skirmish with many subjects—criminality, tropical
kitchens, the inflow of capital, Alexander Peacock,
the German menace, consolidation of the empire,
Empire Day, deafness, T. Bent, etc. Some of them
date back 17 years and are a trifle stale. Birth,
marriage and death statistics, for instance, that end
with 1898 have outlived most of their usefulness.
Also some of them are untidy; thus “persons
married” in Sweden are 12 per 1000, and mat-
riages” in Australasia a shade over seven. As it
takes two “persons married” to make one “marriage”
it was only necessary to reverse the process to make
the Australasian figure 14, and the Swedish one six,
which would have looked much better. No less than
87 of the items were apparently letters to the Argus,
with which the masterful Melbourne eye-doctor once
communicated three times in five days. That comes
of living almost next door to a newspaper office.
The .lneae Pilgrim’s Progress, Ballads of the Aus-
tralian Army, bv Lance-Corporal Cobber, edited by
A. St. John Adcock (Simpkins, Marshall, London).
Rough verses, with something of the spirit of Gal-
lipoli in them, that should enjoy a passing popu-
larity with the elocutionist. The verse never soars
very' high nor .does it dive very deep, but it is often
catchy and vigorous.
A Critical Revision of the Genus Eucalyptus, by
J. H. Maiden. Part 6 Of volume 4 of this stupen-
dous work has just been issued. Some day the epic
of the gum-tree may be written with a pen dipped
in Australian sunshine; meantime the A'. S. Wales
Government botanist gives us a history and descrip-
tion which is of the highest value.
The Flying Teuton , by Alice Brown (Macmillan,
New York). The first yarn in this book of com-
paratively short stories is, in its way, one of the
most enthralling of modern times. It deals with the
occult; but it is • the occult handled with strange
conviction working out the doom and repentance of
Germany. The rest of the work in the book is very
uneven.’ At best it grips hard; at worst it is read-
able. Alice Brown is occasionally tangled in her,
own eloquence; but she has always the saving grace
of something to say.
What is Spiritualism? by Horace Leaf (Cecil
Palmer and Hayward, London). Here you have the
whole story from the point of view of the true
believer. Evidence is piled up which points to
something; but exactly what it points to is not so
clear. The big name of Lombroso stands among-
those of the well-known apostles of the cult. Later
on, something convincing may be discovered.
In Our Street, by Peggy Webling (Hutchinson,
London). Here you are right in the heart of mys-
tery. Everybody and everything tries to be mysteri-
ous’ ; but tile young man rescues the young woman at
the right time. A novel for table-rappers.
The Women Who Wait, by Mary Marlowe (Simp-
kin, Marshall, London). Another yarn by a lady
who used to stand behind the Sydney footlights. The
clairvoyant heroine takes to the better and more'
prosaic business of nursing the wounded in France,
and, later, to the higher task of bearing children for
her blind husband. Fairly written ; but with no
particular grip. v
World Organisation Alter the World War, by T. C.
Chamberlain. A pamphlet reprinted from the Ameri-
can Journal of Geology (why geology the Lord
knows) setting out a plan something like the League
of Nations. The author’s idea seems to be that in-
ternationalised trade highways are the guarantee of
peace. A map endeavors to prove it:.
The Inky Way
“A. 8.5.”: “Marqui” (B. 27/2/’l9) adds
Jovee Kilmer to the roll of poets who have
gone west in the war. Let me nominate
William Noel Hodgson as another. He was
hardly so precocious as Rupert Brooke ; but
from his literary remains it seems that his
full stature would have been considerable.
Before he passed out in July, 1916, he served
with distinction, annexing a lieutenancy and.
a Military Cross. He seemed to share with
Brooke, and Alan Seeger a premonition of
his death. ' Observe this stanza, written two
days before the end, for its resemblance in
prophetic properties to Seeger’s “I have a
rendezvous with death,” or to Brooke’s son-
net “If I should die”:
1, that on my familiar hill
Saw with uncomprehending' eyes
A hundred of Thy sunsets spill
Their fresh arid sanguine sacrifice,
Ere the sun swings his noonday sword
Must say good-bye to all of this;
By all delights that I shall miss,
Help me to die, 0 Lord.
“Chasuble”: Archbishop Mannix’s put -
chase of the Advocate has precedent—the
Archbishop of London bought the Tablet
and the Archbishop of Boston the Pilot.
Mannix could be easy while the Winter
interest held the Advocate, but the family
had decided to sell. Both with Tablet and
Pilot the Church had felt the inconvenience
of private ownership of the leading religious
papers. For instance, the Tablet, before
its purchase, had been led by the forceful
influence of the Duke of Norfolk to wound
the Irish Jby opposing Home Rule.
“Oamelero” : There is a printer in Jeru-
salem who is himself that he
got a fine assortment of English type gratis
from the Hun. When Abdul and Co. were
feverishly fortifying Caza, Bosclie staff-offi-
cers heard that the Australians were growl-
ing that they had to bear the brunt of the
fighting. The Huns decided to shower
icirculars from aeroplanes upon the Austra-
lian bivouacs, advising them that J. Bull was
sacrificing them and sparing his own troops,
and that they would be welcomed with open
arms by their dear brother Fritz. However,
there wasn't a font of English type in the
Holy Land, so a supply was ordered from
Berlin and found its way to Jerusalem just
before Allenby started on his wonderful dash
to the Holy City. It was a coincidence that
Australians were the first troops to walk
into the office, and they were in time to read
proofs of a Ilun circular. One of them
pushed his foot through the forme, and that
was the end of the job. The Jew didn’t
mind, as two days later he was working over-
time turning out printing for British H.Q.
with the Iluns’ type.
“S.T.S.” : Why does Bernard Cronin, writ-
ing about Tasmania of to-day in “The Coast-
landers,” and seeming to know the country
well, make everyone talk an American
dialect?
That was surelv a great think of yours... .Ma’s
wise to the plot.'.. .Now you think over it a spell,
and maybe you won’t feel sore... .They’s no cure
for political graft but only a shotgun Its a
sure thing they’re right up against it.... 1 must
have looked pretty near -as mean as. a scalded hog,
standing there with my mouth open like T was a lad.
The whole thing is like that. A man “hits”
a town when he arrives, and “beats it” when
he leaves —and so on. Why select Tasmania
as the locality for that kind of talk?-
THE EARLY EPITAPH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Here to-day, gone to-morrow.” —Advertisement o)
a neio newspaper.
All flesh is grass; all speech is chaff;
All paper is at best but tissue;
The journal’s solemn epitaph
Is written for its second issue.
Vain, vain are all the boost and boom,
The promise and the proud pretension;
Death has a little, silent room
For scores of papers I could mention.
What though the many sections float
Heavy with words of solid minting,
It is but wise that journals note
The dark, still hour the fates are hinting.
With us to-day, to-morrow gone!
Well, take the good the gods provide us.
A thousand summer suns that shone
May through the winter days deride us.
Death comes to all things soon or late—
The serpents of decay still hiss it.
If this for dissolution wait,
Well, let it hiss —and who shall miss it?
s.s.w.

GEElectric Irons
It is a conservative estimate that,
in ironing for an average family, a
woman walks seven miles a year
between ironing board and stove.
The G.E. Electric Iron will
save waste effort. It furnishes its
own heat, and STAYS HOT until
the ironing is finished. You don’t
have to leave the ironing board,
and you can work wherever there
is an electrical connection.
Write for particulars.

install a G. E. Fan and make
your office comfortable. Why
not put a Fan or two in your
home also ? You can enjoy the
same comfort during hours of
rest with equal economy. Your
family deserves it.

RENEW YOUR STRENGTH BY
TAKING PHOSPHATE;
RESTORATIVE OP VIGOR AND
VITALITY
The condition of the man or woman who
has lost his or her strength is pitiable, for,
above all, it is strength that makes life
worth living and renders possible the full
enjoyment of all that life offers. Illness,
worry or anxiety may have sapped your
vitality and robbed you of your strength, or
old age may be responsible for tissue wast-
age and consequent loss of power, but what-
ever the cause it is possible for you to renew
your strength, to regain the vigorous health
which is your birthright, and to defy the
advance of old age by simply furnishing
your system with bitro-phosphate. This
product is natural to the human body; it is
present in abundance in vigorous youth, but
in later life, or in consequence of illness or
excesses the quantity is reduced and its
reduction is marked by such symptoms as
weakness, depression, insomnia, debility,
languor, irritability, inability to withstand
mental or physical strain, etc., and, noting
one or more of these symptoms, you should
at once commence taking this restorative
bitro-phosphate treatment. No difficulty
should be experienced in obtaining the treat-
ment, for bitro-phosphate is now stocked by
practically all high-class chemists in the
form of small compressed tablets. - The de-
sired results are obtained in practically
every case, and you do not have to wait long
for proof that bitro-phosphate is doing you
good.—Ad\;i.

“C. Ockney” : The late Irene Browne just
missed being the original Lady Frayle in
London. Robert Courtneidge had engaged
her for the character, and she had rehearsed
it enthusiastically up to the eve of produc-
tion, being most anxious to make good in a
new line. But an attack of laryngitis pre-
vented her appearing for a fortnight after
the piece had been produced. Meanwhile
an understudy had had the most unusual ex-
perience of playing the principal’s part—
and the title role —at the opening perform-
ances.
“Bill Bile”: Moree, as “Treasurer” de-
clares (B. 13/3/’l9), may have looked upon
the Chautauqua show as a boon and a bless-
ing; but Moree isn’t N. S. Wales, and there
are several towns in the State that evidently
weren’t so well pleased to lose their good
money. This extract from the Orange
Leader, for instance, is fairly indicative of
the general feeling:—
Sad to relate, the guarantors did not sign on
again, so that Chautauqua will be seen no more in
Orange. They will have to put their hands in
their pockets, to the tune of about £3 each to make
up the deficiency. In this respect they are more
fortunate than other towns—Mudgee, for instance,
where it cost the original guarantors £6 apiece;
Bathurst, £5 ; and Lithgow a similar amount.
What would happen to a native-born slioav-
man Avho attempted to run his business on
Chautauqua lines? .
“Jean S.”: The mostly-Australian opera
co. at Melbourne Princess’s has now suffi-
ciently advanced in performance to justify
the management trying the experiment of an
all-Australian cast. The Italians are not
indispensable, and their dismissal for one
trial night could be justified on the ground
that many people would be glad to see the
unsophisticated locals playing as well as sing-
ing popular parts in a manner that derives
little inspiration from the Italian tradition.
The attractive Nellie Leach has shown us
what the home-grown product can do with
characters like Nedda and Siebel, and there
are other Australian songsters who have
been kept from giving a more vivacious and
natural readme of their roles by the deaden-
ing vicinity of some histrionic automaton
from Italy. The Italians are all capable
singers, but, with the immense exception of
Balboni, they act their parts like mechanical
toys.
A brisk little amateur comedian was lost
when Harold Allert died in N’Aork of ’flu.
In his young days in Adelaide he had a big
reputation in character parts. He went to
S. Africa seven years ago on business,
appeared on the London stage, got back to
Ins insurance work Avhen the war came, and
ended as partner in an American business.
He was in Adelaide a few months ago
representing his new firm, and had a great
reception. Only 34 at the finish.
“Private Boxer”: Recently dead in Eng-
land the broadly-humorous. Florence Baines,
seen in Australia 12 or 13 years ago
in a hotch-potch (said to be by Sydney
Grundy) called “Miss Lancashire, Ltd.” As
an exponent of the clog-and-shawl Manches-
ter mill-hand the late comedienne took a lot
of beating. But the play she appeared in
wanted more. •
Fanny Emily Mary Rose, Avliose name on
Poverty Point is Minnie Hooper, got into a
“leading case” in Sydney last week. J.C.W.
put her off for a fortnight, “plaintiff’s con-
tract for services being rendered im-
possible of performance by reason of
the Government proclamation closing the
theatres.” The firm paid her £10; she
asked for the other £3O of her fort-
night’s salary as ballet mistress. And she
gojc it, on two grounds: (1) that she wasn’t
engaged only for Sydney, where she hap-
pened to be when the proclamation was
issued; (2) that she was not a player, but
an inventor and teacher, and could work
whether the theatre was open or not-—in
fact, she did most of her work while it was
shut.
Arthur Maughan Humble-Crofts, who was
here a few years back, in his capacity of
husband of entertainer Margaret Cooper,
was one of England’s influenza victims. He
died in a military hospital at Dover.
Crofts is remembered in Australia as a
bored young man who spent most of his time
setting fire to cigarettes. Subsequently lie
told the press elsewhere what he thought of
the country, and what he thought didn’t
amount to much.
“Norbell”: Sara Allgood knows a lot
about ’flu and quarantine. After losing her
husband through the epidemic in Maoriland,
she had great difficulty in getting back to
Australia. She managed to escape in a
transport to Adelaide, where she was quar-
antined on Torrens Island for seven days.
Then she moved on to Sydney in the Wyan-
dra, and just as she had passed the Federal
doctor at Watson’s Bay, the State M.O.
discovered that the ship’s baker was ill, and
that meant another seven days’ dreary in-
ternment at -North 'Head. “Peg-o’-my-
Heart” is now back in her home at Mosman,
and is scared to go across to the city lest
someone should develop a temperature before
the ferry gets to Circular Quay.
“0.K.”: Died recently in London, Phil
Rae, a well-educated vaudeville comedian
Avith a fine baritone voice and a lively sense
of humor.. He Avas one of the brainiest
patterers in the business, and sometimes the
broadest. Three notable stage Phils I have
known, Day, May and Rae, avlio died before
their time. Phil May got his start in black-
and-white by drawing portraits of actors,
“BABES IN THE WOOD” PANTO.
Three acrobatic porters —the Paulastros.
and was himself so fond of the stage that he
even dreamed of taking to it in earnest.
Phil Day—well, there are no pilgrimages to
his Australian grave these days, but time
Avas when his Jaikes in “Silver King” and
his excruciatingly funny Bosco Blithers
made him the most popular comedian who
had ever come to this country. Phil Day,
like Phil May and Phil Rae, was Avell on the
right side of 50 Avhen he passed out. These
nippy names are strong incentives to
boliemianism, perhaps.
“Andrew Ralph”: “Galleryite” mentions

as the chief Australian success of the late
Irene Brown. Granted. But what about
including her passionate Berenice in “The

theatrical as Bella Donna, she had moments
of considerable power and looked voluptu-
ously exotic. To make the snake-Avoman —
THE SILENT DRAMA.
THE TALKATIVE ONE: “They say she is paid
four times as much for acting in the
pictures as she got on the stage.”
THE INTERESTED ONE : “It would take more
than that to induce some girls to give
up talking.”
a glorified melodramatic villainess- —con-
vincing would have been a pretty difficult
job. And, anyhow, for myself I could look
at a starette being merely voluptuously exotic
seven nights a week.
“Stargazer”: It is again claimed that
the problem of talking pictures is solved.
This time it is the Marconi Wireless
that does the trick. I hope not. Give
these good, familiar people of the movies
artificial voices, canned speech, and more
than half their charm will be gone.
Besides, knowing the language of the
dumb show as thoroughly as we do now,
talk will be a wasteful and ridiculous excess.
Much of the print flashed upon the screen is
that already. The pictures have a pull over
the legitimate in not demanding absolute
silence on the part of the audience, and the
Introduction of those continuous noises
which tlje politicians make will be a risky
experiment.
“Glen H.”: Australia’s old-time friend
May Yohe crops up again. Her very latest
husband is Capt. John Andy Smuts, who did
things in the S’African unpleasantness. To
quote from the chaste pages of the San
Francisco Examiner : “Dirt poor, wage-
earners in Singapore, Honolulu and Pacific
ports, this strange pair profess no regrets
for lost wealth and splendor.” May shooed
away the reporter who sought to interview
her, remarking simply (doubtless in clear,
ringing tones) : “I am happy now and I
want to stay happy.”
“A Little Bit of Fluff,” a feeble farce from
which Melbourne theatregoers stayed away
with great enthusiasm, has passed its 1000th
consecutive performance at London Cri.
The record has been achieved only three
times, by “Our Boys.” “A Chinese" Honey-
moon” and “Charley’s Aunt.”
“J.F.H.” (London) : The new English
Actors’ Trade Union has a membership of
2500. Its programme includes payment per
performance, minimum wage of £3 a week,
payment for rehearsals at not less than the
minimum wage, and a provision that no
manager shall dismiss an actor on account
of illness unless he has been absent from 14
consecutive performances. The Union ex-
pects that, with the strike problem to face,
managers will in future lend a more sym-
pathetic ear to the complaints of the profes-
sion. If not the ear will be severely bitten.
Arnold Bennett, who has collected cartloads
of royalties, has written supporting- the de-
mand for payment for rehearsals and declar-
ing the yarn that the cost of production will
not bear the expense to be “ridiculous non-
sense.”
“C.S.G.”: Somebody will yet collate the
songs of the A.1.F., and, duly edited, they
will be sold on the bookstalls with the pic-
ture of an Anzac on the cover and an
alliterative title. When we foregathered in
billets or estaminets Ave sometimes burst
into melody. A general
favorite was “Mademoiselle
from Armentieres hasn’t
been kissed for forty years.
Inky-inky-parley-voo.” The
verses were many and the
sentiment—oh, c h a rming!
Another sweet melody ran
like this: “Oh, I don’t give
a blank for the A.1.F.; they
can blank blank blank blank
blank.” And the air of
this was most seductive:
“Apree la guerre fine e,
mam’selle from Gay Paree
(seven miscellaneous blanks
here), no bianky bon for
me!” But after a couple
of rounds of vin blanc the
boys tired of these frivolities
and became sentimental sun-
downers in a real Answers
to Correspondents song.
Adelaide friends of Jean
Robertson, who made her
first stage appearances with
the Rep. Theatre there, be-
fore joining a Williamson
co., continue to get glowing

are James O’Neil, Nance

But Frederick Lewis as the
prodigal, and Jean Robert-
son as the beautiful Tisha,
almost run away Avith the
acting honors.” “Lithe as
a panther” the St. Louis
Republican called Ti er.
She must have developed a lot since her
Adelaide days, when she was too statuesque.
Miss Kyrle Bellew, a kinswoman of the
beauty actor who was once a Melbourne
Herald reporter, has become the second wife
of Arthur Bourcliier, the London actor-
manager. Violet Vanbrugh was the first,
and she had the tie snapped last year, by the
polite method of suing for restitution of con-
jugal rights which were never in danger of
being restored. The Bourchiers seem to
have acquired a winner in Cosmo Hamilton’s
“Scandal,” an artificial bedroom play in
which the heroine is not as naughty as she
seems or the hero as primitive as he looks.
“Old Villain”: Whilst we were playing
bellowdrama at the Cambridge Theatre
(now Hoyt’s Pictures) the electric light
failed, and we had to revert to gas. This
precluded the cutting-out of lights and shift-
ing scenery in the dark; consequently the
act-drop had to be lowered at each change of
scene. In simple language our stage-
manager explained matters: “Ladies and
gentlemen, the electrics having fused, we
can’t work a black-out whilst the back-cloth
is struck, so the drop will be lowered before
the box-in is set, and the props, raEing-
pieces and tormentors taken off stage. Of
course, ladies and gentlemen, this necessi-
tates alteration to the whole of the light
plot.” And the audience applauded. Audi-
ences will applaud anything —when the other
scoundrel is on.
Worlock, a handsome man who played the
Caliph in the “Kismet” that Oscar Asche
brought to Australia and was the first mem-
ber of the co. to enlist in Kitchener’s Army,
got badly Avounded whilst doing his bit. but
was* lucky in not being incapacitated for
hero parts. • Lately he took the place of
Pennington .Gush (what a name for a
mummer.!) in Marie Lohr’s “Nurse Ben-
son,” where his fine figure was duly com-
mented upon. It was another Benson —he
of the Shakspearean Co. —who
nursed Worlock’s histrionic talent when he
was a very young man.
Dorothy Brunton is noAV playing the
heroine in “Soldier Boy” at London Apollo.
According to one theatrical scribe who often
tells the truth Dorothy is now known as
“Queen of the Diggers.”
DROPPING THE MOTLEY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
“Ships in the harbor are having their dazzle-
painting removed and are being repainted with their
original house colors.”- —Shipping item.
Farewell, the fancy costumes that we’ve frolicked in
so long ;
The wardrobe mistress clads us as befits our sober
ways
On the well-tramped paths of ocean, where the
cyclone’s chorus-song
Goes roaring in the stays.
AVe have done some giddy .pirouettes while clad in
dazzling guise ;
We cut the straight and narrow way and danced
in maddening maze,
Whose courses showed like fever charts to our long-
tired eyes
• Through sleepless nights and days.
We are glad to drop the tinsel and once more appear
to view
In house-colors made for working-—the rough work
which is our share.
We are sick of turkey-trotting, doing tangoes on
the blue,
Dodging U-boats here and there.
We’re respectable old ladies and can leave our call-
ing cards
In raiment none can jeer at or refer to with a
grin.
We can feel at home full-speed ahead, or grousing in
the yards •
While taking cargo in.
The dance of death is ended. We have gladly flung
aside
The motley of the past few years that clad us,
shore to shove;
Its nightmare colors giddied us, but now on every
tide
We’re sane once more.
N.S.W.
E. J. HILL.

This CO. expended £102,272 in constructing
and equipping a line to link up the country
around Deniliquin (N.S.W.) with the Vic-
torian railways. Since the district is not
a wildly progressive one—between the 1901
and 1911 censuses Deniliquin added only a
couple of hundred to its population—the co.’s
state of health has depended entirely on the
local weather. The profits column is con-
sequently bumpy; but the fat years have
balanced the lean ones, and, after giving
shareholders a moderate return on their
capital, the directors have been able to build
up the co.’s strength. This comparison
gives another view of the position:—
1905. 1915. 1918.

p.c. p.a. on a capital of £lOO,OOO between
1905 and 1911, and since then a shade under
51 p.c. on £125,000 —the additional £25,000
having been a bonus issue—and also after
providing for all renewals and repairs out
of earnings, the co.’s position has been im-
proved by about £39,000 in 13 years.

There’s very little of the old trouble visible
now. The book talue of properties in
possession at the beginning of this year was
£3733, and £7OO-odd of that represented a
recent foreclosure. During the past half
another old property was unloaded, realising
£262 in excess of the figure to which it had
been written down. However, there won’t
be many more titbits to be got in this way.
In 1900 the co. swept away the old wreck-
age, wrote down capital, and started life
afresh. This shows what progress has been
made since

As in the case of most managements who
had learned their lesson in the ’nineties, this
one kept a tight rein on new business during
the uncertain war years. It is feeling a little
more confident now. and during the pasr,
year has built up its advances business to
within £2500 of the 1914 figures. Money is
dearer now, and the new loans are more
profitable to the co., while some of the risks
which helped to lift interest rates have dis-
appeared. On the other hand, the holding
of deposits has been reduced from £42.570
at July, 1914. to £33,142 in January last,
and when, later on, the co. may want to get
in more outside, money, it won’t get it at the
old rates. But even then there should be
a good margin: for the interest earned in
advances works out at about 8£ p.c. p.a.
The depositors’ security is 1 very strong:
’ there are assets with a book value of
£128,427 to meet £33,471 of liabilities.
From. J. L. Strevens :
As “Observe” (B. 6/3/’l9) puts it, there is appa-
rently good cause for accusing the Fisher-Hughes-Cook-
Watt Government of bungling on the Papuan oil
question. The monopolistic character of the oil
trade, as brought about by huge oil combines in all
parts of the world without Government interference
—often, in fact, through Government apathy—
emphasises the futility of a policy involving sole
control of operations by neophytic officials totally
unfamiliar with the methods that must be adopted to
ensure success. Unlike most other enterprises, oil is
usually a 50-50 venture, the commercial and financial
side being balanced by the scientific and technologi-
cal side, the whole being interdependent and requir-
ing the utmost co-operation in the several branches.
The most heart-breaking work is usually connected
with pioneering efforts in reputed new oil terri-
tories. Thus the late W. K. D’Arcy spent £300,000
on his exploration and testing work in Persia, and
the Burmah Oil Co. half that amount before the rich
fields now controlled by the Anglo-Persian Co. were
discovered, notwithstanding the fact that the whole
country from the Caucasus, in the north-east, to the
Mekran coast, in the south-east, was teeming with
oil indications. The same in Trinidad and Assam.
Many authorities are of the opinion that an un-
broken oil belt runs S.E. from European oilfields
through the Caucasus, Persia, Baluchistan, Hima-
layas, Assam, Burma, the Straits, Dutch Indies and
the Australasian Archipelago to New Caledonia, and
that in it will be found the largest sources of supply
in the world. Of these countries the only one that
can benefit Australia directly is New Guinea, and
perhaps, indirectly, New Caledonia. But if Australia
is in earnest about developing Papuan oil the sooner
the Government gracefully (or otherwise) gives a
free hand to those who are really able to do the
matter justice, the sooner will it take its place
among the few Empire oil-producing countries.

This is the business logic of the offer. If we can improve our trade
and gain more customers, will not we be better off with small
profits —-smaller profits than ever? We believe in a large volume
of business on small margins as the best for you and for us. This
offer will emphasise these fpcts.
But after all it is not so much the low price—as what you get for
the moneys—that is the main selling feature of London Tailoring.
We start by standardising the quality of the fabrics, linings and
trimmings. Then we have the best-skilled cutters and tailors to
do the making, in our own well-equipped workrooms. Finally the
suit has got to be just right —or there’s no sale. '
Satisfaction Guaranteed
or Money Refunded
is our bedrock principle of trading.

because it is impossible to replace them at anything like the price
we paid in early markets. Don’t be too late. Post the

It any illustration appearing in The
Bulletin takes your fancy, write to the
Manager, who will quote you price for it.
Every snapshot of President Wilson up
to date shows him with his mouth open.
“Baynett Frogg” : Everyone in the batta-
lion was sick of Coldfoot’s complainings, but
he shut up for a week after Bill Bidgee had
put his shot 'in: “Sparemefiamindays!
Yer’ll be wantin’ flowers on yer grave next
It is always something of a gamble when
a church decides to import a new minister.
Which is probably why such a decision on
the part of Stow Church, at Adelaide, is
printed in the Register under the beau line
"Late Sporting.”
“Coot” : He was a beginner in the estate-
agent line and was unacquainted with legal
terms, so when the intending purchaser in-
quired whether there were any easements
subsisting ■ on or over the property he
promptly assured him that “of course there
are the usual conveniences.”
The idea that anything dragged- from a
great distance must be better and cheaper
than the local article is getting a rude bump
in Maoriland, where retailers are cursing
twine made from brown paper in far-away
Japan. The joke is that the retailer is
being asked to pay 2s. Off. per ball for the
stuff while a penn’orth of good Maoriland flax
could be spun into a ball of far better twine
that would return a profit if sold at six-
pence. Probably the Jap stuff could be sold
at a profit at sixpence, too; but why should
the Jap take sixpence from tis when for lack
of competition he can extract 2s. Gd. ?
“Camelero” : The Australians and Maori-
landers who died in Egypt and Palestine are
to have a line monument at Port Said. It
was originally intended to erect it at the
entrance to the Canal, but a site could not
be got, and it will be placed instead in
gardens on the town side of the stream.
The cash required for the work has been
collected by the Diggers, and artists have
been asked to submit designs. So far the
design of Sergt. Otho Hewitt, of Adelaide,
is favored. It sliows a wounded Maori-
lander lying on the field with a Light Horse
trooper standing over him and defying the
enemy. The verses for the memorial
have been supplied by The Bulletin’s
“Gerardy.”
“Croson”; Before we quite recover from
the ’flu scare and forget all about the restric-
tions, I would like State Ministers to explain
why they took such Strenuous steps to com-
bat a disease that isn’t wholly preventable
while _ they merely play with—when they
don’t ignore—certain other disasters that are
wholly preventable. There have been in
Victoria alone in the last three years no
less than 1486 accidents and 24 deaths
because dangerous machinery in factories
was not adequately safeguarded. These
cost the State Insurance office £39,144, and
private offices probably more. And that is
only part of the bill. Why not quarantine
a factory where more than one “accident”
occurs in a year?
“Hamer”: The tipping curse worries
everybody in proud old England; but the
Aussies and, later, the Americans have
made things worse by their inability to think
in copper. The Australian, who mostly
hates the custom, hates still more to be
mean; and, when he got to know that in
England certain people live on tips instead
ot wages, ho went to the extreme. The
generosity has made life a misery in London.
You go into a. threepenny shaving saloon,
and, if a sixpence is tendered, the pirate tills
the full amount, while an attendant steps in
the way before you get to the door and does
unnecessary things to your hat until the odi-
ous blackmail is forthcoming. Even at
private entertainments the waiter will whis-
per into your ear: “I’m going after this
course, sir.”
Inquiries made by Bowser, Vic. Minister
for Health, “lead him to believe” that
there is a lot of overlapping in the dis-
tribution of milk, while the safeguards relat-
ing to the purity of the Melbourne supply
are inadequate; and the Cabinet has commis-
sioned Treasurer McPherson to make in-
quiries during his trip abroad into the
methods of distribution in England and the
U.S. In a particular case, the Minister
has learned that ten different milkmen
supply a certain suburban street containing
13 houses, making 20 visits daily between
them. Such a state of affairs hardly needs
time wasted on it in Europe or America by
a responsible Minister. Any Melbourne
dairyman of experience could suggest a
remedy in five minutes. But he would
probably hate to do it.
“The Oldest Inhabitant” re some others:
Apropos the claim of the Hentys to have been the
first settleis in Victoria. Captain John Hart became
master of the schooner Elizabeth, 'of Launceston,
owned by Mr. John Griffiths, in November, 1831.
Early in December, he wrote, he landed on the
Laurence Kocks, Portland Bay,. ’‘where we were
joined by a boat’s-crew left there the year before.
They had procured nearly 400 sealskins.” On
November 3, 1832, Hart left on a second sealing trip,
landing on almost every rock Jpetween Bass’s Straits
and Doubtful Island Bay. He wrote: “Whales so
plentiful that on my visiting the bay [Portland] in
June I found all the casks full and the men putting
oil into pits they had made in the clay.... I took
the first eargo of oil from Portland on this occasion.
Mr. E. Henty made his first visit to Portland with
me, returning to Launceston the same voyage.”
Writing to Governor La Trobe from Portland on
January 16, 1854, Stephen George Ilentv (sixth son
of Thomas Henty—there were eight boys in the
family) said: “1833.—The Henty family" being in-
terested, in a whaling company whose operations were
carried on along the coast from Portland Bay to
Port Fairy, Edward Henty [third son], accom-
panied by my father, visited Portland. Bay during
this year, and, finding the country well grassed and
apparently very well adapted for grazing purposes it
was determined by the family, on their return to
Launceston, to send'over stock and other necessaries
for an establishment at Portland Bay forthwith.
Edward Henty reached Portland Bay (1834), and
fixed his habitation on what is now block No ’ 4 in
the township of Portland, and now occupied by me.”
Ihe Hentys may not have been the pioneers of Port-
Land Bay ; but they certainly were the first settlers.
Inc whalers were birds of passage.
