The Governor has consented to open the
Show, so evidently the G.-G. will not spend
Easter in Sydney as expected. The plague
is, of course, responsible, for quarantine has
no respect for persons.
New, or rather renewed, Mayoress
Richards, who, by the way, is a sister to
Mrs. Sam Hordern, sen., is quite used to the
odor of civic rabbitskin. Like the panto-
mime hero, her husband seems ordained by
a special Providence to draw up by-laws.
And her father was in the Sydney Council
for many years.
There is hopeful talk in maidenly circles
about the Club balls being re-started as soon
as the Peace is signed. That is to say,
during the coming Autumn race-meeting.
But the Union Club is very doubtful. The
rent is enormous, the charge on the garden
being a particularly outrageous expense, and
very few new members have been coming
along lately. The members who are in town
much are more or less in a transition stage,
socially speaking. They are rather too old
to go to dances unless dragged there with
grappling-irons, and rather too young to
have offspring of a dancing age. The Aus-
tralian Club is prosperous. On the other
hand, many of its social members are away,
or just preparing to go. The others look
somewhat coldly on the dance-going habit,
and they would be well pleased to let it stay
in abeyance as it has, without complaints
from anv quarter, since the war began.
Miss Onslow, the youthful daughter of J.
Macarthur Onslow, M.L.A., of Camden
Park, has engaged herself to Capt. Stan-
ham, A.D.C. He was hit in the early part
of the war and has a stiff leg in consequence.
She will be an heiress in a very big way.
The Onslows own the best part of Elizabeth
Bay and a tremendous quantity of house
property elsewhere.
All Sydney and Mr. Holman went to the
Town Hall on Saturday evening for the first
concert given by the State-guaranteed
orchestra. The ever-popular overtures to
“Tannhauser” and “William Tell,” with the
five movements of the “Coppelia” ballet
suite, were the principal dish, Meyerbeer’s
“Coronation March” and Lacombe’s
“Aubade Printaniere” were the sweets, while
George Whitehead’s baritone flashed out the
wine in the serenade from “Don Juan” and
Massenet’s “Promesse demon Avenir.”
The applause which greeted the performance
had the sharp sound of a heavy hailstorm.
Major Oliver Hogue, “Trooper Bluegum,”
good pressman and good Australian, will
come no more to his land. Influenza
exacted ultimate toll from him in London on
March 3, just a year after the death of his
twin-sister from pneumonia. In the big
fight from 1914 Gallipoli and Egypt knew
him; and with conquering Billjim he rode
into Jerusalem and Damascus. Three books
stand as his monument and his testimony
of faith in the land he loved.
Professor David, who is nearly a General
these days, returns to Sydney next month.
He will be chaperoned by Mrs. David, of
course.
Another well-known Sydney man who is
returning within the next few weeks is Cap-
tain Humphrey Browning, M.C. He is one
of the two sons of R. J. Browning, the
Crown Prosecutor. The other was badly
gassed two years ago, and has been ill, off
and on, ever since. Captain Browning is in
the famous 13th Battalion, and is one of the
few men who, having been captured by the
Germans, managed to escape back to his own
lines from No Man’s Land. A Hun raiding
party poured into the dugout where he was
with his major, preceding their arrival by a
shower of stick-bombs. As the two Aus-
tralian officers were being dragged, pushed
and kicked back to the Boche lines, their
own machine-guns opened out on the strug-
gling mass. In the confusion Captain
Browning got loose with one bullet in his
arm and another in one of his kidneys.
The Jim Burnses are expected home very
shortly. He went back to the Front in July
last after being wounded, and his wife
arrived in London just in time to miss him.
Of the three sons of the big shipping man he
was the eldest. One of his brothers was
killed and the other put out of action at
Gallipoli. It is a useful family record all
round, and, to top it off, their father has
given about £50,000 one way and another to
funds connected with the war.
Captain Frank Hurley’s war photographs
taken in Egypt and France have been on
view at the Kodak Salon since last week,
when Lady Davidson bestowed her blessing
on the show. The Red Cross bags the
modest admission fee and the catalogue
money (nurses, soldiers "and sailors in uni-
form half price) ; so J. O. Fairfax added his
few words of’congratulation, and waved a
glad hand, in passing, to the V.A.D., who
is once more doing her bit by taking charge
of the Salon. . The dramatic intensity of
Captain Hurley’s work is stirring. He
knows all about the value of light and shade
and grouping, and in such works as “Just
As It Was” and “Laying a Duckboard
Track” gives poignant meaning to the ex-
pression, “four years’ active service.” No
glamor of quick movement here, no thrill of
onward rush —just the grimness of those
quagmires of sucking mud. “The Morning
of Passchendaele” is tragically sombre, while
in “Alone I Did It” the camera has caught
a group of Diggers, grave, gay and sceptical,
listening to the cheerful camp liar describing
his glorious adventure; and the Egyptian
photographs are marvels of rich coloring in
high suulight. Captain Hurley was with
Mawson and with Shackleton in their Polar
haunts; but at present he is satisfied with
the peace of Vaucluse, where he is living
with his English wife.
There’s a new young man sitting under
the Chief Justice’s desk. When the liter-
ary Evatt left to pursue briefs, R. V. Shield,
home from the war with a Military Cross,
took up the running as Associate to the
Chief. For a while he was with learned
brother Street, but learned-brother Street
won’t want an Associate for six months, as
he left this week for Europe, after doing 12
years’ hard labor on the bench. Mrs. Street,
an incorrigible Comfoi’ts Fund worker, went
with him.
Sydney’s best image of Pickwick had a
“golden wedding” at Randwick the other
day; and Mrs. F. C. Crouch shared con-
gratulations with him. “Freddy” Crouch
was in the N.S.W. Parliament over 30 years
ago; but he likes bowls much better than
politics.
Dr. J. F. Bartley and his wife —who was
a Melbourne Fitzgerald—are off to England.
There is a young Jack who waited only for
his eighteenth birthday to enlist at Broken
Hill. After over two years’ service he has
been entered among the young Australians
who are to continue their studies in Eng-
land ; and dad and mother are going to have
a few months with him before he puts his
nose to the medical grindstone.
William Silver,
S. Australia’s bril-
liant home-g ro w n
pianist, has been
added to the teach-
ing staff of the Adel-
ai d e Conserva-
torium. The S.
Australian U n iver-
sity was the first on
this continent to
have a chair of
Music; and the
school continues to
be one of the several
things the State is
proud of. And the
more home-g ro w n
teachers it gets the
prouder it is. Sil-
ver is a bachelor, too
devoted to his art to have thought of marry-
ing. And yet there are things'he can do
besides play the piano divinely. He is
alleged .to be almost as divine a cook. In-
cidentally the late Eugene Alderman was
his great friend; and Eugene’s specialty,
away from his violin, was millinery.
That great worker for returned Billjim,
Mary Makinson, is mourning a younger
sister, Miriam, a nurse of Prince Alfred’s,
who died of enteric. The other nurses have
asked to be allowed to join the mother in
providing a memorial to their popular chum.
Two old Australian, families were repre-
sented at a wedding in St. Andrew’s Cathe-
dral when Reginald Hugh Cox, of the 6th
Light Horse, was mated to Rhoda Beatrice,
youngest daughter of the D. C. Rennies of
Sydney. The Coxes and the Pipers first
joined hands and lands when William Cox,
one time of the 46th Regiment, married in
1813 Elizabeth, youngest daughter of John
Piper of the 6th Foot. The glory of the
Pipers has faded, but the Coxes still flourish
in N. S. Wales, Maoriland and Tasmania.
Another engagement is that of an elderly
member of the Bettington family to a very
young Milson girl. The Bettingtons have a
stud sheep-station near Cassilis, and they
also abide periodically in Parramatta. Mil-
son’s Point takes its name from the other
family.
The Diggers’ friend in Durban, Natal,
Miss Ethel M. Campbell, is to get an Aus-
tralian maplewood writing-desk from the
boys of the Boonah, which was held up in
Durban for ten days, though no one was
allowed on shore owing to the ’flu.
Dolly, of the singing Castles, has been
married in N’York to Major Charles Finn
of Perth.
Life-saving Ella Gormley is off to see what
the Columbia University can teach her in
physical culture. Since last year, Miss
Gormley has been instructing the girls in
our high schools.
Soldier-artist Dave
Barker is a Victorian
who has migrated to
Sydney. Some time
ago a report got
about that he had
been killed; but it
was a very substan-
tial ghost that faced
Judith Fletcher’s
camera in Sydney
last week. His war
service began on
Gallipoli in August,
1915, with the 2nd
Division. He was
on the Peninsula till
the evacuation, when
he went to Imbros
and worked with
Bean on “The Anzac
Book.” Back in Egypt for awhile; then to a
map-making job in Mesopotamia. The Kia-
oka Coo-ee got what drawings he could spare
time for. Unfortunately for him and for the
picture-lover, he hasn’t brought back enough
color-stuff to hold a show of his own. But
there are a lot of sketches from which he will
probably make a set of war-etchings. At
present he is working on “Australia in
Palestine,” the souvenir book of the cam-
paign.
Fritz Burnell, one of the most romantic
of Australian minor poets, leaves for Eng-
land by the Ulysses to seek inspiration from
the Peace.
The elder Holdship girl, Edith, is on her
way back to Australia. She went to Egypt
to marry her first husband a couple of years
ago. He got killed, and she has just been
re-married to Commander Darley, R.N.
The Holdship girls are heirs to good looks.
Their mother’s sister was Mrs. Willie
Mitchell, one of the most decorative Sydney
women of her day—which was about the
Hampden period.
The Pat Osbornes are settled in a London
flat. She' was Miss Ruby Madden, and most
of the family are now in England. The
Vallanges have been there for years, and
Mrs. Harry Osborne, another sister, has her
boys at school near her in the country. Pat
Osborne went away with the Remounts from
N. S. Wales, and, failing to get a commission
in a fighting unit (he was over the age),
plunged into war work in London. He spent
two strenuous years searching for details of
soldiers -who were missing or killed.
Captain Maddrell, who achieved the
almost impossible last week —that is, got
his word and that of his witnesses taken in
preference to the statement of a policeman
—belongs to the well-known Braidwood
family. It is conceivable that the fact had
some slight bearing on his acquittal. Percy
and Bob Maddrell are first cousins ; also that
very picturesque young woman, Mrs. Venour
Nathan.
The Society of Women Painters has
feathered an eyrie where the artistic soul
of Sydney—feminine gender—can alight for
spiritual nourishment, k or pause on the wing
to peck at a sandwich to sustain the earthy
flesh. You may be an associate member
of the club if you don’t dabble with brush
and pencil. The rooms are in the old
Queen Victoria Markets, and there is a
well-lighted studio, a tea-den and an enter-
tainment hall. Mrs. W. A. Holman is
president.
The Conservatorium lecture-concerts are
with Sydney once more, and on a warm,
moist night last week little Jenny Cullen
was almost blown away by the wind of en-
thusiasm that greeted the quartet to which
she adds her slimness. Beethoven and
Brahms were the composers of the evening,
and the Master, bowing dismissal to the
thunder of handclaps, dissertated on the
music of the forthcoming season, the Per-
manent Orchestra, and the composers in
hand.
Cavell-house—the rest-home for tired-out
nurses—is richer by £7O through sale of
Hilder’s “Parramatta Bridge” calendars.
Vaucluse Park on Saturday afternoon and
evening was a village green where feats of
strength went round. There were greasy
poles and long jumps, and behind canvas
a boxing contest in full swing. At the old
Wentworth home the Vaucluse Red Cross-
ers served tea. For 3d. you could enter a
gaily-hung booth, and see not exactly the
carnet from Bagdad but the flasr from the
leader of the flotilla of launches that cleared
the way to Bagdad for the British moni-
tors. “Vaucluse” is the name she boasts,
and she was skippered on that occasion by
Lieut. Milford of Vaucluse. What is more,
she was built in Sydney, took her trial runs
around our own coast, and was then shipped
to England. All we have of her is the flag
with her name and number ; the sturdy little
launch herself is stationed in Indian waters.
At night the Scarlet Follies frolicked under
the moon. Proceeds for afternoon and even-
ing go into the collection-box for the local
Repatriation Committee.
Dr. Emma Buckley, graduate of Sydney
Uni. and first woman to be on the staff of
King George’s Military Hospital, has in-
dicted our expensive oversea staff at Aus-
tralia House for its criminal carelessness in
failing to supply the Osterley with baby-
food after issuing a mandate that mother
was not to bring baby’s rusks aboard, as they
would be looKea on as superfluous luggage.
Fortunately many women took no notice of
the injunction, and these filled the breach
till the lied Cross took the affair in its large,
capable hands and produced sterilised foods
and fresh fruit. So the 210 kiddies under 18
months were delivered from a very grim fate,
and arrived in sound order and condition.
Henry Charles Osborne, who has just got
an M.C., joined up originally in the R.11.A.
In peace-time the R.11.A. was remarkable for
its magnificence; in fact, on parade it had
precedence over the Guards themselves.
But in the trench warfare of France and
Salonika there was no call for mobile guns,
and so the R.H.A. became almost indistin-
guishable from the humbler R.F.A. Captain
Osborne won his distinction by good work in
the field —it is necessary to mention that,
since about 80 per cent, of M.C.’s are given
for Staff work.
The Herbert Allens, who left on the
Osterley, have not been outside Sydney for
years. He belongs to the big firm of attor-
neys, and, like his brother Arthur, is a man
of work and hobbies. Walter, the only
leisurely member of the family, lives per-
manently in England. During the war he
plunged into the hazards and excitements of
a special constable’s life.
Mrs. Wheeler (once Annie Laurie, of
Springsure, in Queensland) had “gone
abroad’’ for a two-year stay to see daughter
Portia “finished,” when war slammed the
door. She took a flat somewhere in London,
decorated it with mementoes of Bananaland.
and lonely travellers developed the habit of
dropping in for a yarn with the little woman.
Soon “Mother” was entrusted with the little
jobs that Billjim scarcely got. the oppor-
tunity of doing. She cabled messages for
them, received and redirected parcels, and
searched out mislaid lads. She held their
money, lent them hers, did the rounds of the
hospitals, sent parcels to prisoners in Ger-
many, and regularly forwarded a two- or
three-column budget of news about hundreds
of boys who wrote her regularly from the
battlefields. When she returns (perhaps
this year) half the State will cheer. Inci-
dentally Portia has said “Yes” to Captain
Fox, whose father owns Carfax station.
Lieut. H. Bowden Fletcher, D.F.C., of
Stanthorpe, got back to Bananaland last
week after 44 years’ service. He went
through the Gallipoli, Sinai and Palestine
campaigns, and was wounded at the unlucky
second Battle of Gaza. He fought first with
the 12 th Light Horse, but after being
■wounded went flying, and spent 15 months
with No. 1 squadron in Palestine. The
D.F.C. was earned during the preparations
for Jacko’s final ejection from the Holy
Land.
One of. the charming and quaint figures
in .“Maytime” is Angelica (Beatrice McEl-
wain), for whom Madame Christian, her
teacher, has ventured on prophesying a suc-
cessful career. Kindly Mrs. Hugh Ward
takes an interest in the girleen, too, and
Melba’s approval has also been won. Her
lyric soprano is getting further exercise in
understudying one of the stars.
If you desire to watch your buds put forth
their first petals at the Peace Ball you may
do so in an atmosphere of quiet comfort,
even though you do belong to the era before
the two-step and if your feet find the glassy
surface, of the floor more a menace than an
invitation; for there are to be bridge tables
and comfortable chairs in backwaters away
from the swirling billows of tulle and ninon.
If you are a coming-out Miss and wish to
dance your first Lancers at the Peace Ball
in the Debutants’ set, send your own and (if
possible) your partner’s name to Mrs. T. 11.
Kelly before April 12.

The Newmarket opened the Flemington
autumn frenzy on Saturday. There were
flood waters ail round the paddocks, but the
course had dried up enough to let the chief
race be run. The crowd was huge. Yet
only a few smart dressers came out; they
were bright specks in a sea of dowdiness.
The women who haven’t seen their dress-
makers yet for their winter frocks are out.
of it, because there is a sudden reversal in
the female silhouette. The wide, bunchy
pleated flare is now old-fashioned. Skirts
must cling at the ankles.
Lady Helen was in sables and dark colors.
Lady Stanley wore a dark frock and coat
and tied her hat with a sweet bow under
the chin. The Genthelps were frockcoated
5n black, spatted and top-hatted. The stove-
pipe of Chief Justice Irvine rose high and
glittered, and his dame walked in deep black.
Lady Doris Blackwood wore a Dutch-blue
suede overcoat and chattered on the lawn
with the flapper daughter of the Everard
Brownes, who was smartly gay in a Vene-
tian-red coat-dress. Mrs. Russell Gabb was
in simple tawny tailorings of real Fifth
Avenue cut, and a smart little hat. Mrs.
Jack Winter-Irving was another pleasant
episode on the lawn. Her dress was grey
stockingette, weighted down with the new
“manufactured” fur in grey. “Manufac-
tured” fur is the fad of the moment. Dr.
Murray .Morton’s wife was smartly tailored.
Captain “Duke” Watt walked proudly with
his young wife in grey and seal. Mrs. Jack
Wallace wore a sporty, short-tailored suit.
Mrs. Carlyle 8 my the (out of quarantine
from Maoriland) hung rows of Maori beads
over her brown dress. Norman Fenner,
just home from the war, escorted his slender
fair wife, who was decorated in black-and-
white stripes. Fan Dango Mackay wore a
veil from hat-brim to waist. Over a smart
sown. Returned youivev folk of the Bail-
lieu and Fairbairn clans paraded in civvies,
and seemed glad to bo homo,'
Belitoppers on the men of the vice-regal
party at Flemington looked distinctly old-
fashioned and unimpressive. They were
almost the only tall hats on the course, and
one of tlieim—the grey cloth with a black
band that Gov. Stanley sported as usual —-
was uniquely out of date. The Stanley Ex-
cellency must be haunted by the wowserous
notion -that his old-time headgear is the
proper thing to wear on a wicked racecourse,
seeing that it figures freely in ancient pic-
tures of the English Derby Day. But these
times there is something depressing in the
sight of a belltopper hat, whether it be all
black or grey with a mourning band round
it. Nothing at Flemington last Saturday
was more lugubrious than the appearance of
the vice-regal party, unless it was the band's
performance of “The moon hath raised her
lamp above,” from “The Lily of Killarney.”
Heaven knows why Bertie iZelman allowed
this operatic air into the brass band’s reper-
toire. It is a charming thing when a cap-
able baritone and basso sing it in duet,
but because it is pitched in a low key fox-
basso and baritone it is the last melody a
brazen band should try.
Several gilt-edged parties were booked for
this week, most of them farewells to the
Stanley lady. The G.-G. and Lady Helen
were At Home one evening at nine o’clock,
and the Gaud Mayor and his missus had a
spread earlier in the week.
The impending departure of the Madden
dame has caused a succession of fare-
Avell teas and lunches —in . fact, it has
been blowing a gale of food. Mrs.
Bill Leonard started the series with
a mid-day peck at the Alexandra Club.
On Wednesday the widow was expen-
sively fed again at the same address,
with the Sam Staughton widow as hostess.
After an interval the guest and most of the
other lunehers proceeded to daughter Lesley’s
showroom for further refreshment. The
Madden dame hopes to find three daughters
waiting to welcome her in London. The
fourth, Ruby, with husband Fat Osborne,
will have started the voyage to Australia
before mother arrives.
Lesley Madden and Mrs. Willard gave a
party last Wednesday to farewell the
Madden dame. Guests met at the
Madden undie shop and evei-ybody drank tea
and ate fruit-salad in the surroundings of
lovely flimsies. The Madden dame looked
pale and handsome in her widow’s weeds.
She has grown slender and wistful.
Daughter Lesley was dressed in grey and
snooded in blue while Mrs. Willard
wore a turned-up golden sailor-bat like a
halo.
The presentation of a set of Australian
opals to Lady Stanley by the Led Cross
Society was made an excuse for a pro-
tracted bout of tiresome oratory. After
listening to half a dozen speeches, extending
over three-quarters of an hour (including
one from a suburban mayor who arose and
spoke a piece off his own bat just when the
presentation was about to be made), the
audience was too limp even to furnish the ex-
pected laugh when John Grice, who handed
over the gauds, coquettishly announced that,
“despite the presence of his Excellency,” he
was going to refer to “dear Lady Stanley.”
The only burst of mirth happened when a
voice from one of the side galleries, almost
before the chairman’s last observation had
been made, pleaded “Can we take the photo
now?” The interlude gave the much-tried
little lady in the centre of the stage an oppor-
tunity to get control of her emotions.
The Rowes Kellys are still in Tasmania
owing to the quarantine regulations. Sister
Mrs. Miller has been looking after Moor'a-
kyne in their absence. The travellers have
been entertaining the vice-regal Newdegates
at their Apple Island house.
Mrs. Wanliss prepared a big farewell
party for the Irvine dame in Hobart while
waiting for a quarantine pass to the main-
land. Mrs. Norman Bayles is another
marooned Victorian in Tassy. She has been
putting in her time “up North,” and went
out Deloraine way to be at the Arthur sale
of cut-glass and other treasures.
Mrs. Percy Russell is again leading her
regiment of V.A.D.’s. The “shop” at the
base of the Equitable Buildings is to be
opened again—not for its original Red Cross
purposes but for ’flu hospital needs.
Mrs. Hackett-Moulden, wife of Adelaide
lawyer Moulden, and widow of the late
Westralian knight (lienee the Hackett), is
coming to Melbourne to place her elder
daughters at the Geelong Girls’ Grammar
School. There are four Hackett daughters
and one son.
Miss Tucker, a niece of the late South
Yarra cleric, is steaming off to England with
the Blackwood children, whose young minds
she has moulded for the past three years.
The medical Reggie Weigalls have post-
poned their projected American-English tour
till 1920.
Prof. Meredith Atkinson, who spends a lot
of time trying to improve the mind of the
toiler by arranging lecture courses, has gone
off to take the chair at the annual conference
of the Workers’ Educational Association in
Queensland. Atkinson still has hopes of
ultimate something-or-other, whatever that
may be; but nine bricklayers out of 10 don’t
know that the W.E.A. exists, or affect not
to. The association gets in most of its
work in the winter-time, when the prole-
tariat finds fewer counter-attractions and
likes to get together in a warm room. Be-
sides, summer-time is fishing-time for pro-
fessors.
Major Alison Walker has scored the second
Military Cross in his family. The other
went to Capt. Stewart Walker, killed in
France. Of the five soldier-sons of Presby-
terian Moderator-General John Walker, of
Ballarat, Major Alison is one of two sur-
vivors. His brother Maxwell is still with
the R.F.C.
Colonel Springthorpe, who has had a
couple of war-trip»> overseas and lately re-
turned to Aussie, is in Melbourne again after
a breather down the Otway coast with the
budlike Mi’S. Springthorpe 11.
Jack McComas, who follows the Law in
the intervals of plunging around in the
social pool, is down with appendicitis at
Toorak.
Portsea, which has lately blossomed forth
into a Mecca of week-enders and holiday-
fortnighters, has had its ’flu scare like most
other seasideries. A soldier came down to
stay over the Sabbath with one of the shack-
parties. He left for Melbourne on the
Monday and next day went into hospital—a
bad case of ’flu. At once the neighborhood
was skinned of butter-muslin and eucalyp-
tus, and everybody talked nothing but ’flu
for two whole days, though nobody went the
length of appearing in public behind a mask.
Then the matter was dropped and conversa-
tion re-opened about Mrs. Fatler’s extra-
ordinary knees and Jane Skimpleigh’s bean-
poles, while a lot more was heard about the
case of Mrs. Pingpong, who never went in
swimming when her husband was down from
town, except once only, and afterwards —
but that is another matter; and nobody
really knows, anyhow.
The Bowes Kellys’ tall eldest son, Capt.
Monckton Kelly, who has had over three
years’ service, has sent word from London
that he has got a French War Cross. It is
the third decoration in the clan. The Alec
Kelly son Wallscou’rt got his Croix
de Guerre in 1918. The George Kellys’
eldest son Charlie wears the ribbon of the
Military Cross on his Territorial tunic.
William Davidson, of Hawthorn, formerly
Inspector-General of Public Works, has had
a cable telling of the death from influenza of
his son-in-law, Sgt. John Lyon. The dead
soldier was a brother of Charlie Lyon, who
married handsome Vair Dodds and is just
back from a Government shipping job in
India.
Adelaide’s revelations: —
The Josiah Symons are preparing- to welcome back
their son Oliver, who has been loaned by the
British to the Australian navy, and will help to bring
in the Australian flotilla. He was in both the Heli-
goland and Dogger Bank actions, served in a
destroyer in the North Sea, and was for some time
in the Mediterranean and at Salonika. From his last
ship, the Dartmouth, he saw the Austrians hand over
their men-o’-war after the armistice.
On Friday a new Rest Room for soldiers was
opened at Keswick. It has been built and furnished
by the Children’s Patriotic Fund, and was handed
over to the Governor, as the representative of the Red
Cross Society, by Minister of Education Harvey. His
Ex. promptly passed it on to the Military authorities
with a bouquet of praise for the children and their
organisers, Miss Hyett, Miss Meithke and Miss
Watson.
We’ve had the first Button Day of the year, pro-
ceeds to the Soldiers’ Home at Fullarton. Receipts
showed a drop on last year’s averages. The Chil-
dren’s Patriotic Fund takings for March (about
£1000) are to be given to the home.
At a reunion of members of the Ist Stationary
Hospital, a small Soustralian unit that left here in
1914, a list was made of the distinctions won by
its members: a C.M.G., three D.S.O.’s, one M.C.,
many D.C.M.’s and M.M.’s and innumerable mentions
in despatches. At Lemnos, with seven medical
officers, 70 trained men and 80 or 90 untrained men
borrowed from the British forces, they put through as
much work per day as did a. later hospital with 30
doctors, 100 nurses and 250 orderlies; and at Anzac
they were the only hospital established within some
hundreds of yards of the enemy front trenches.
The women of the State have decided to erect a
memorial to the soldiers and sailors who have fallen
in the war. So far only the preliminaries have been
discussed.
Ruby Lindsay, sunny Australian girl and
brilliant black-and-white artist —the “Ruby
Lind” of hundreds of Bulletin drawings—
has died of influenza in London. She was
the charming and talented little-sister of the
famous Australian family and the wife of
cartoonist Will Dyson, with whom she has
been working in London for the past few
years. The two young Australians have
built up international reputations: Haldane
Macfall, in his “History of Painting,” says
of the girl for whom the local art-world is
now mourning, “She is the most remarkable
woman with the pen-line now living.” High
praise, but well eaimed. Melbourne studio-
visitors of years ago knew Ruby as a pretty
fair-haired girl, entirely unaffected, whose big
blue eyes - twinkled with gaiety and good-
fellowship—-the very embodiment of joyous
youth. It is hard to believe she has gone.
Two Bananalanders, Enid Sydney Jones
and her mother, -were delayed in Melbourne
by the ’flu quarantine and had to put up at
Menzies’ instead of journeying home. While
they were there Captain Arnold Weinholt
came back from the wars. He also is a
Bananalander, and mutual condolences led
to sympathy —and the engagement which
The Bulletin announced a week or two
ago.
. Paragraphs in the dailies about the mar-
riage of Lord Dudley’s eldest son to one of
the blue-blooded Leveson-Gower ladies made
no mention of the bridegroom’s parents being
present. It may be presumed that they
didn’t both take part. If only one w T as
present, and we had to guess which, we
would say Mother. Even the coldest of
countesses is apt to warm at the thought of
a wedding, whereas Dudley would be more
likely to scratch his head and wonder whether
the boy wasn’t looking for trouble.

Tim Keigwin, that incorrigible bachelor,
has gone to the altar at last. lie was mar-
ried at Holy Trinity Church, Brompton,
London, to Gwendoline Kniglit-Smith,
known behind the footlights as Gwen
Hughes. This tall, pretty English girl did
minor jobs in such productions as “High
Jinks.”
Mrs. Tobin has arrived back from the Fog
with a mountain of baggage. The St. Kilda-
road home is being made ready for the
daughter who married a titled British sol-
dier.
The Stork has been working overtime
lately. The latest bundle is an infant
daughter to Mrs. Walker —once Aileen Whit-
ing. Her husband, Capt. G. B. Walker, is
soldiering at Quetta, in India.
Gus. Dudgeon left over £42,000, practically
all of which goes to his widow, formerly
Frances Innes, sister of the evening Herald’s
editor.
Melbourne Shakspeare Society begins its
year rather late. The first monthly meet-
ing happened only last week —at the Austral
Salon—when Bose Quong headed the bill
with a recital from “Anthony and Cleo-
patra.” A charming elocutionist of the
gently dramatic order is Miss Rose Quong.
From April 4 onward the society will never
miss a month, and on April 25 the Bard's
Birthday celebration will be thrown in.
Baldwin Spencer has decided to sell in
May all his treasures of Australian art.
probably the finest private collection in the
Commonwealth. In addition to its artistic
worth it has historical value, for it contains
the best of tibe earlier works of Streeton,
David Davies, Fred McCubbin and others of
the older school. All the worth-while
moderns are represented, Hilder by several
things of rare beauty, and Norman Lindsay
by some notable work.
On Saturday night the G.-G. and Lady
Helen went to the Auditorium to look at
the film showing the surrender of the Ger-
man fleet. The orchestra Ruled Britannia
until the hind-leg was nearly blown off the
trombone. A lecturer on the platform, with
his boiled shirt looking like a large egg in
the gloom, tried to keep pace with historical
explanation of the moving pictures. Fred
Collier sang a deep-sea song with live fish
in it. The vice-regal pair sat in the dress-
ch’cle with a bunch of aides. Doris Black-
wood was a dream in sea-green silk and frills,
with a red band round her hair. A pictu-
resque old sailor sat behind the party and
held up a silk Union Jack slung from a glit-
tering trident. The Naicest were grouped
round in the gloom in gleaming evening togs.
Australian Dorothy Cumming is being
much pictured and paragraphed in Ameri-
can papers. She has been Cyril Maude’s
leading lady for two years. Her first New
York appearance happened recently, when
she played in David Belasco’s production of
“Tiger, Tiger.”
May de Sousa, principal boy of the J.C.W.
panto., put in her ’flu vacation writing a
book. It is to be published soon and is a
theatrical story located in Australia and
America.
Jennie Brenan is back from her American
joy jaunt, wearing a blue feather in her cap
to match the Irish blue of her eyes. She
has seen all sorts of dances and is already
busy teaching Toorak the latest.
A big audience was allowed to pick out
its favorite kilt at the Princess’s on Satur-
day. All the stage Macs flaunted their
fighting colors for the benefit of Lucia, and
some were arrayed in home-made Tam ’o
Shanters advertising the tartan of the clan
MacAroni. The ill-fated Lucia (Gertrude
Johnson) first met her Edgar in an unbe-
coming frock of bottle-green: this probably
gave the preliminary push to her reason,
which was afterwards unseated by her
damaged romance. Georgina McCarthy, as
her supporter,' Alice, would have shouted her
presence in sky-blue and crimson if the chro-
matic curios in the chorus had permitted her
gowns to be heard. The sensitive eye, deeply
outraged by these horrors, was compelled to
find a sedative in the costumes of the men.
Young Lois’s black silk doublet, lace collar
and pearl-grey boots were very restful to
gaze upon. Also the rich black of Cacialli’s
appointments as Raymond were a rest-home
for a wounded eye. Incidentally Raymond
resembled contemporary descriptions of
Talleyrand, except, perhaps, about the waist
and intellect.
Phil Finkelstein, the affable J.C.W. press-
agent, is mourning the loss of his mother.
Mrs. Sol Finkelstein, who came to Australia
40 years ago and lived at St. Kilda. To
the end she was a prominent worker for
Jewish philanthropic projects.


FIVE MILLION
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
Sold During' February.
The public may be interested in knowing
some of the particulars regarding the part
played by Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
in the present epidemic. They were not boomed, such
as was done by many, to use the influenza epidemic to
attract attention to their commodities, for which they
were of little use. Straight announcements were made
advising the public to keep their systems in order by taking
an occasional dose of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills together
with 10 grains of Aspirin or Quinine, but on no account to
try to doctor themselves should they show any signs of the
real Influenza, but to send for the doctor and to go to bed.
This advice has been followed to a wonderful extent. Tens of thousand
of bottles of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills have been sold since the
epidemic started. One retail company alone has passed across its counters
five thousand bottles in thirty days. Many wholesale houses have
exceeded this quantity in Sydney, Melbourne and Adelaide. More than a
ton of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, was sold during February. There
are about two thousand two hundred pills to the pound (anyone can work
it out), which makes over five million pills sold during February in
Australia, or at the rate of about a pill for every man, woman and child on
the continent. The wonderful character of this sale is further emphasised
when it is known that no salesmen were employed whatsoever. Orders
came through the mail or over the telegraph and telephone wires, and were
in response to the genuine demands of the trade. It can therefore be seen
that DR. MORSE'S INDIAN ROOT PILES have played their
part, as already stated, in fighting the epidemic.
It Is not claimed they are better than anything else made for similar purposes, but it is claimed that
they are far better than most, and equal to the best made in this or any other country—and why not?
The Proprietors have been making pills for nearly a century, and for twenty-five years they have been
in constant use in Australia. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are like Ford Cars. It is quantity
production that makes them possible at the price. If the formula were taken to a chemist he would
charge five shillings to make it up, and there would be no guarantee that the ingredients had not lost
their .efficiency. That cannot happen in manufacturing Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. The ingredients
are bought in large quantities and rapidly used up, and the activity of this pill is certain. They are
sold to the public at a shilling, or a little more, subject to local conditions, and in any case the public
gets good value. These are the reasons for their immense popularity, which can be summed up in the
two words, “Absolutely Dependable.”

DO THE JOB
PROPERLY
First impressions of the Newmarket Han-
dicap, as viewed from the grandstand, were that the
far end of Flemington straight was laden with water,
and that the heels of the flying field were kicking
up a spray. The effect was afterwards said to be
due to smoke from the abattoirs. It followed the
flying gees like fumes from a motor-car. The V.R.C.
track was certainly very damp, so the winner’s time
for the Newmarket —linin. 15sec. —was highly credit-
able. Of the 25 starters not more than eight or nine
ever looked dangerous, and of these Molly’s Robe
and Greenstead were practically in front all the time
until Sir Ibex, the top weight, came with a late rush
and split the pair. Previously Mistico and Haud
had looked probabilities, especially old Mistico, who
was going like a steam-engine in the centre of the
course. Save for the poor performance of Lucknow —
the English horse having obviously staled off, or
otherwise gone wrong, since the Tuesday when he
won the Futurity—the race was a striking vindica-
tion of recent form. Molly’s Robe had only missed
the Oakleigh Plate by a few inches. Sir Ibex, Green-
stead and Haud were second, third and fourth in the
Futurity, and they were second, third and fifth this
time, Mistico being fourth.
The odd name of Molly’s Robe would suggest
something of domestic interest in the christening of
the filly, seeing that she runs in the nomination of a
lady—Mrs. Ilillcoat. But the pedigree seems to
explain the name. The winner's dam was Microbe,
and if you pronounce it Mikerobe you get an idea as
to why the filly was saddled with her weird cog-
nomen. But why not just Mollyrobc?
Some of the glamor departed from the easy vic-
tory of Mistico in the w.f.a. race at Caulfield when
it was discovered that Magpie had been suffering
from rheumatism or kidney trouble when the ex-
Westralian horse beat him. Nevertheless Mistico
ran well enough in the Newmarket to show that he
was a good one when Magpie, Eusebius and Star
Comedian finished far behind him. Eusebius last
Saturday emphasised the incorrectness of his running
a week previously by winning the Leger from a weak
field in the easiest possible style. The slow pace at
the outset enabled the favorite to lie back at his
leisure until it was time to rush to the front and
win. As soon as he had passed the post Eusebius
Was slowed down to a canter. Star Comedian
appeared to have an even easier job in winning the
Essendon Stakes, so the plungers who lay odds on
the “good things’’ for w.f.a. events had two happy
times during the afternoon. The longest odds on the
two certs, were 9 to 4 and 5 to 2, and neither of
them gave any cause for real anxiety during their
respective picnics.
Precious little chance nowadays of securing a long
price about the “good thing” by getting on to it
early. In the bookies’ lists issued weeks before the
Newmarket, when there were still about six dozen
horses left in the race, not one of the ultimate first
eight was quoted at more than 20 to 1. Molly’s
Robe, Sir Ibex and Greenstead, which filled the
places, and stood at 6’s, 16’s and B’s in the market
at starting time, were quoted at 16, 16 and 12 three
weeks earlier. Two of them certainly did shorten in
price; on the other hand, a good many of the early
fancies dropped out of favor or were non est on the
day. For instance: —■
12 to 1 Tragic Sta-r .. Receded to 25 to 1
16 to 1 Haud.. .. ~ 33 to 1
14 to 1 Petracia .. ~ 25 to 1
10 to 1 Outlook .. „ 33 to 1
14 to 1 Probation .. Scratched
20 to 1 Yoorala . . ~
.20 to 1 Wedge .. ~
20 to 1 Col. Chutney..
20 to l Mareca .. ~
20 to 1 Hoprig . . Receded to 33 to 1
20 to 1 Headwind .. ~ 33 to 1
The inducement to back horses early for hedging
purposes is not nearly as strong as the warning to
refrain from having- a punt until the last moment.
How sadly ridiculous is the position of the punter
who finds on the day that his wager is already lost,
or that he could have saved half his investment by
waiting till the starters had weighed out!
New Tipperary, a 20 to 1 outsider, took the Aus-
tralian Cup, giving Earlborn (7st. 71b.), who was
second, 31b. Menin filled the other place.
The Welkin, sire of Cielo, the Oakleigh
Plate victor, is having- a brilliant season. To that
date, since August 1, his stock has won close on
£20,000 in stakes. They have annexed A.J.C.
Derby, Eclipse, Chelmsford and Debutant Stakes,
M.L. Derby. Great Northern (M.L.) Derby, Carring-
ton Stakes and Oakleigh Plate.
* * * *
Clark and Robinson seemed to get much
the same luck in winning the Futurity Stakes with
Lucknow that they had with imported Flash-of-Steel
in 1915. in both'cases the winner was being rapidly
overhauled at the finish. But, whereas Flash-of-Steel
was a 3-year-old carrying the bottom weight, Luck-
now is a 4-vear-old who humped 7st. 71b., and earned
a penalty of 71b. for the Newmarket, under a condi-
tion which has been added to the list of Futurity
rules since the youngster did the trick. Moreover
Lucknow suffered considerable interference—more,
perhaps, than Sir Ibex, who chased him home. It
happened that Clark was in Melbourne when the
younger nag took the rich prize. He backed the
horse heavily to win the double, and dropped it at
the second time of < asking. Yoorala’s fall in the
Futurity Stakes wakened recollections of how Ernest
Clarke’s Emir (as a 2-year-old) tumbled over in the
same race long years ago. Emir’s mishap occurred
inside the distance. Yoorala was rather further from
the post when she fell as the result of striking Luck-
now's heels.
* * * *
Died in Adelaide Hospital, John Hales,
only surviving brother of Tom, Australia’s one-time
brilliant horseman. In the ’nineties John, located at
Millieent (S.A.), trained a small string. He couldn’t
compare with Tom as a rider, but he could beat him
at billiards. Despite the failing of his sight of late
years, he could still dazzle the -youngsters, being a
fine exponent of Grav’s losing hazard.
* * * *
“0.R.” :Re jockeys’ rings. Here is my
remedy. If the racing clubs are to control jockeys
at all let them do it logically—that is to say, com-
pletely. In the first place, none should be allowed
to ride in a race of importance till they had been
carefully trained and examined for merit. Riders
should be required to ballot for their mounts imme-
diately prior to each race. Just before mounting, the
owner’s instructions should be handed in to the
racing authorities, under seal, to be opened and given
to the jockey, in the owner’s presence, at the last
minute. Trainers should be compelled to keep
apprentices in proportion to the number of horses
trained by them; and, in order to keep up the
supply of qualified horsemen, there might be one race
per day of every meeting for apprentices only. But
even with these precautions the bookie and the
swindling punter would doubtless find a way. The
proposed remedy would have its best chance of
success under a purely totalisator system, with
McNoses entirely abolished.

alongside this, was to have met Barney Thompson,
the Vie. welter cham-
pion last Saturday
night. The latter
missed his train, so
the bloodshed had to
be postponed for a
week. The position
at Sydney Stadium
next Saturday will
'be something the
same as it was on
the night Les,
Frank’s brother, .first
met Fritz Holland
there in the long
ago. Les (like the
present aspirant)
was raw, and almost
unknown; Fritz
(like Thompson) was
a seasoned, warrior.
Holland gave Darcy
a good beating,
though the Maitland
crowd that had come
down to see their
man win wouldn’t
believe it, and to
prove the fact tried
to set the Stadium
on fire. Thompson
may defeat the cham-
pion’s young brother,
but it is safe to
prophesy one thing—■
he will find it as hard
to knock Frank as
Holland found it to
knock Les. The
family is a remarkably tough one.
* * * *
R. L. Baker has started a physical culture
business on a vast scale in Oxford-street, Sydney.
Harold Baker is still suffering from the spinal
injury which he got on a transport over two years
ago, but the other brothers are fit and will assist.
“Snowy” is to manage the show, which claims to
provide every bodily exercise within the knowledge
of mankind, and in the intervals he will go on with
hi* film business.
Jack Johnson, who has just challenged
Jess Willard, was 34 when he beat Tommy Burns on
Boxing Day in 1908. That is, he thought he was—
there is no great certainty about the average nigger’s
age. His prime was past, even then, and since those
days he has lived hard. He puts forward, persua-
sively, the allegation that he faked the Willard fight
—that he was squared to lie down. On the evidence
of the pictures it is a lie. He outboxed Willard for
20 rounds and then Anno Domini and the other
man’s giant bulk got to work, and he was exter-
minated.
Johnson may have been encouraged in his bluff by
a match that was arranged last month between Jim
Driscoll and Pedlar Palmer. It came off a week
ago, and Driscoll stopped Palmer in four rounds.
Driscoll must be years younger than Palmer, assum-
ing the person mentioned in the cables is the great
original. Pedlar Palmer, while at the height of his
fame, was journeying to Ascot by train. He was
provoked by a man in the carriage, and hit him.
The man fell dead, and Palmer got 10 years, which
he completed, with remissions for good conduct, just
before the war started. He Would be about the same
age as Johnson —nearly as old as Fitzsimmons was
when Lang outed him in Sydney.
* * » »
Jack Green is still bantam champion of
N. S. Wales. He met Bill Tingle at Sydney Stadium
on Saturday night, and for over half the battle the
flyweight was a winner on points. But Green is
game and a stayer. By the seventh round he had his
clever adversary tired and apprehensive, and in the
thirteenth he put him out. The lethal hits were a
right to the head and a left to the body, immediately
followed by a smashing right on the jaw.
* * * *
Roamer, the great Yankee miler, is beauti-
fully bred—by Knight Errant (Trenton —St. Mil-
dred, by St. Simon) from Rose Tree II (Bona Vista—
Fanny Relph, by Minting). He has been racing for
six seasons, has faced the starter 80 times (first on
37 occasions), and has collected £18,542 in-stakes —-
and the stake-money is small in the U.S. His
recent mile in lmin. 34 4-ssec. is a record fc)r
America. He would be the ideal article for a
National Stud, only, like so many others in the sport
which is designed, primarily, to improve the breed of
horses, he is a gelding.
J. C. Harrison, the ex-bookie who collected
six years in M.L. last week for an assorted lot of
frauds, was well known in - Sydney and Melbourne.
He is one of those strange characters who, while
capable of making a fortune legitimately at most
forms of business, go crooked for the love of it.
He had all the qualities that bring success in the
Ring—nerve, judgment and a head for figures. In
the days when he was living upon the punters of
the Commonwealth it was the practice of the police
to periodically raid certain bookmakers’ betting-
shops. The raids were frame-ups for the edification
of the wowsers. By an error Harrison’s establish-
ment was entered instead of the one which had made
arrangements for an official visit that week, and a
fortune in ready cash was impounded. He claimed
at this time to be worth £lOO,OOO. He did a con-
siderable business in the confidence line, was an
expert card-sharper, and claimed to have stiffened
more jockeys than any man in the Commonwealth.
* * * *
There is a sound reason for the sale
abroad of the A.I.F. horses and mules in France;
yet, somehow, it has not been stated in the cables.
A big percentage are suffering from an incurable
eye-complaint. First one eye, then the other, is
attacked, and without treatment the animal quickly
goes stone-blind. Latterly the V.O.’s were inject-
ing a serum which inflamed the eyes hideously for
a time. On the bandages being removed the
invalid proved usually to be better—temporarily.
But there was always a relapse, and except in
special circumstances the vets preferred to evacuate
these cases out of hand. The disease is highly in-
fectious, as was proved by the fact that the well-
fed and cared-for A.S.C, horses living in stables in
back areas suffered worse than artillery horses whose
waggon-lines were mostly in open fields. Elderly
Backblocks Diggers sometimes claimed to have seen
the disease, or one very like it, in Australia in the
’nineties; but even so it could not have taken the
epidemic form that it did on the Western Front.
* * * *
After the commercial aeroplane the aero-
plane for pleasure. Both should be here within five
years, and what a vogue the latter will have in Aus-
tralia —notably in the big cities! For Melbourne
or Sydney the water-plane. In both places there are
vast stretches of permanently smooth water to rise
from and to land upon. All down the coast, for
months at a time, there is a trade-wind which varies
hardly at all in strength, and which can be relied on
to spring up and die down at certain hours with the
regularity of clock-work. Instead of doing his Sunday
surfing at Manly or Cronulla, the rich Sydneyite
will board his ’plane at Rose Bay or—best of all
inlets for this propose—Mosmah, fly along the coast
at a thousand feetr or so, past Narrabeen and Broken
Bay, undress on board, alight off (say) the Ninety-
Miie Beach, get to the first breaker in a dinghy, go
on to Port Stephens for lunch and an hour’s tunny-
fishing, and so home before the southerly. Not that
the stiffest southerly would be likely to do more
damage than give the ’plane-owner a cold. The ’bus
type of machine is already practically fool-proof.
» * # »
Schoolboy Keith Kirkland is Sydney’s
swimming crack nowadays. A week ago he won a
100yds. heat in 57 2-ssec. ; on Saturday he did
the same stretch in 58see. and again in 56 3-ssec.
Morris Christie was another 100yds. winner last
week (62sec. was his time), and he followed it
up by taking the 440yds. senior-cadet championships
in smin. 57sec., and the first heat of the President
Cup handicap (one mile) in 26min. 23 4-ssec.
* * * *
W. Smith, a Horsham (Vic.) rifleman, who
owns up to 78, scored 13 consecutive bull’s-eyes at
500 yards the other day.
J. Bogle (Sydney Uni.) smote 119 v.
Central Cumberland on Saturday—his fifth century
this season. Roy Bardsley (Giebe) made 109 v.
Balmain, he and Warren (69) putting on 119 for the
first wicket. Arthur Mailey (for Middle Harbor v.
Gordon) took nine wickets for 53. Amongst the
wicket-keepers Andy Ratcliffe (Balmain) didn’t give
a run in Glebe’s 245, and H. Carter (Waverley)
allowed but three in 215 and he caught two and
stumped one.
* * * »
The ancients did well in the Old v.
Present Boys Brisbane Grammar School match. They
started with 604 (Evans 175 and Boyce 101), and
then got seven of the youths out for 189.
* * * *
There was sensational bowling at Christ-
church (M.L.) when Auckland tried to tear the
Plunket Shield from Canterbury. The holders had
smacked up 291, and Auckland, being a strong bat-
ting side on paper, was feeling good. Then Canter-
bury tossed the ball to Mcßeth. * Within half an hour
or so he had bagged seven for 56. Then he captured
two more with successive balls, making 9 for 56
eight clean bowled. The last man arrived. Mcßeth
sent him down a mixed concoction, and lie spooned
it into mid-on’s hands. But mid-on dropped it and
robbed Mcßeth of the glory of getting all the wickets
and finishing with the hat-trick. Next innings
Mc-Beth got six for 112 on a wicket that didn’t suit
him so well. Canterbury won by close-up 200.
* * * *
“S.T.S.”: The “Sheffield Shield”—about
which there is a discussion whether it shall go on
indefinitely, or pass to the winner of the next two
or three matches after competition is resumed —was
given to Australia by the Earl of Sheffield when he
brought an English XI, with W. G. Grace in it, in
the summer of 1891-2. Giffen, Lyons and Jarvis in
those days had put S. Aus. cricket right in the
front rank; in the only two games it played that
season it beat N.-S. Wales and Victoria in turn by
an innings. So naturally the Shield was offered for
competition by those three. S. Aus. held it in the
second year, Victoria five times in the first nine
seasons. At the beginning of this century came a
big spurt by N. S. Wales, which won six times run-
ning, nine out of 11. When war stopped the com-
petition, the figures were N.S.W. 13, Vic. 7, S.A. 3.
The present idea of getting rid of the Shield as
soon as possible is not likely to commend itself to
the old players who have battled for it. It was
intended and accepted as a permanent trophy, and
there does not seem to be any justice in giving it
outright to the side that happens to get going
quickest after the war.

Doust and A. M. Jones (N.S.W.) and R.
Lycett (Vie.) are all billed for the English tennis
championships in May next.
* * * *
Jim Anderson and Horne Rice stood out of
the Sydney tennis singles championships last week. And
R. W. Heath (holder —who won in 1914) is on
active service. So the way was easy for Norman
Peach. He beat R. Neil by three sets to two (25
games to 23) in the final. He also won the chal-
lenge pairs (with Miss St. George) in straight sets —
6-4, 7-5. In the men’s doubles Rice and Pocklev
downed Hicks and Utz by three sets to one. Mrs.
Ford won the ladies’ singles, and she and Miss St.
George the doubles.
* * * »
At the annual meeting of the National
party in South Aus., Blundell, M.P., got laughed at
by people who didn’t know as much for urging that
amusements must be provided for country dwellers
if the young men are to be kept from crowding into
the towns. The returned soldiers on the Murray, he
said, had had to be given gymnasium and billiard-
hall, or they went off to the nearest town for recrea-
tion. The meeting found it “too bold and indefinite,”
called it a vote-catching scheme, and laughed it down
with a stupid suggestion that it meant kiss-in-the-
ring and kindred “sports.”'
* * » #
The two old rivals who started bowls in
Adelaide, the Oval and the “Drive” (the Adelaide
Club having its green on Victoria Drive), are still
at the head of affairs or thereabouts, though every
suburb now has a green, with a team that can give
the two central ones a hard game. These two, both
unbeaten so far this season, have at last met, and the
Oval won with unexpected ease, 126 to 78. Oval
players included Blimuan and Pettinger, both famous
batsmen of the past, with Clem Hill, his two brothers
and his father.
* * * *
An all-round bowling “carnival” arranged
by the South Park Club, Adelaide, brought all kinds
of well-known citizens into the field. The Chamber
of Commerce (Riley, Tassie, Prosser, Allnutt) was
beaten by the Civil Service. Governor Galway’s
military four beat the “executive” horribly. Archi-
tects (Smith, Milne, Counsell, Good) just lost to
the Church (Johnson, Casley, Haynes, Hughes).
Banks got home by one point from the Law (Solo-
mon, Evan, Thomas, O’Halloran). Insurance beat
Stock Exchange (Taylor, Sands, Pearce, Burner).
Another executive team, which included president
Sam Jacobs and cricketer Clem Hill, won from
another military four; and the Press was too good
for Parliament, the sides being the Sowdens (father
and son). Hadrian Moody and Mildren, against
Varden, Blackwell, Hague and Cowan.
» * » *
Those old sea rivals, Sayonara and Bona,
met again after 15 years in Sydney Harbor last
Saturday, under the flag of the P.A.Y. Club.
Sayonara had a topsail and Bona hadn’t, and after
a great race the ex-Victorian led Bona home by
25see. Aoma was third.
* * * *
The veterans of Adelaide Polo Club are
reviving the game, so as to have it in good going
order when the younger men return from the war.
They are a willing lot, but naturally on the mature
side. When their old supporter, Richard Law Smith,
now confined to his room by illness, was told the
names of the players on a recent Saturday, he is said
to have asked pleasantly: ‘‘Why don’t you get E. T.
Smith and Charles Goode in ?” The sarcasm lies in
the fact that those well-known citizens are 89 and
92 respectively.
* * » *
The Naval authorities at Fremantle have
notified Lyoh Wise, who has invented a method of
swimming which is called the self-rescue creep, that 20
naval-reservists who could not previously swim were
able, by adopting this system, to propel themselves
50ft. at their first attempt without aid. Hefe are
the directions; but it is not advisable to wait until
there’s a chance of drowning before putting them
into practice: “Bend your body well forward, make
under the surface of the water the same natural,
simple, easy movements that you would make on
land if you were slowly and steadily creeping with
your open hands, knees, feet, etc., up a sloping sand-
bank. Keep your eyes open, mouth shut, breathing
through your nose. Do not raise any part of your
body, except your nose, lips, eyes, etc., out of the
water.” By following these directions it is claimed
that non-swimmers, dressed or undressed, can sup-
port themselves for hours, and by simply turning
their shoulders to the right or left can swum in the
direction they wish.
BALLADE OF BYGONE PUGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jack Johnson has challenged Jess Willard for the
heavyweight championship of the world.
The work of Time is passing grim,
The obsequies of stiffened kine
Are now the chief concern of him
Who stiffened men in nineteen-nine.
Gone is the figure strong and straight
Whose perfect lines caused artists bliss;
11 is waist is in a ruined state.
How many pugs have coiue to this!
He who earned fortunes by a hit
Now draws a Dago hireling’s screw,
And is extremely glad of it,
Since jobs for has-been pugs are few.
Irt countless mean and petty ways
His life is the antithesis
Of what it was in bygone days.
How many pugs have come to this 1
His monarchy, the hempen square,
Wherein lie swaggered like a king
Is gone. He does his humble share
As super in another ring
Without an enemy or friend,
With none to cheer and none to liiss—
And so it shall be to the end.
How many pugs have come to this!
Envoy.
Yet lo! How Time may hint in vain!
Pat, old, with limbs and wind amiss,
K'i challenges the world again.
How many pugs have come to this!
K.S.’.t.

AVIATION.
New South Wales School of Aviation, Richmond.
H 1
ITHERTO the facilities of the New South Wales School of Aviation at Richmond have been utilised
solely for the training of pilots for war purposes. The need for these has passed with the suspension ot
hostilities. It has now become possible to train men who are interested in Aviation privately or wish to
engage in it commerciallv. It has, therefore, been decided that for the future the Aviation School shall
be carried on as a branch school of the Technical Education Department, providing two separate courses
of instruction—One for Pilots and the other for Mechanics.
The course of training for Pilots will cover a period of not more than 13 weeks, and students will
receive practical instruction in flying, the principles of aviation and practical instruction m adjustmen s.
The Mechanics’ course has been specially c 1 esigned for those who are gaining experience
in one or other of the various trades connected with engineering or woodworking, and who wish to become
aviation mechanics. These students will be given instruction in the adjustment and maintenance ot
aeroplanes, and will have special instruction in engine work and rigging.
The Mechanics’ class will be carried on so as to enable students to get the instruction by
attending on two week afternoons or for a full day on Saturdays, the latter provision being intended to
meet the special case of apprentices who can get off from their work on Saturday mornings. 1
latter course will commence at 10.45 a.m. and will last till 5 p.m., and the afternoon courses will he
from 2.30 to 6 p.m. in Summer and 2.30 to 5 p.m. in the Winter. All these times have been made to
suit Railway Time-tables.
Persons desirous of availing ihemselves of either of these courses of instruction should apply to
the Superintendent, Technical College, Ultimo, where full particulars as to fees, etc., may be obtained.
3b 4 8.
JAMES NANGLE,
Superintendent of Technical Education.

P.C.N.: Lyrical version of the Argyllshire foray
herewith-
They collared every boat,
They soon were all afloat,
But all were caught as you haye seen
And then sent down to quarantine.
All night they were awake,
Dreading the hated snake,
So they rebelled the very next morn,
And marched away in uniform.
Out to the Cricket Ground marched they,
All the way in the good old sway,
They hadn’t the ’flu, so they couldn’t see
Why they shouldn’t have their liberty.

Do any of the “Freetrade” journalists
believe in their papers’ joss? T. W. Heney,
being no longer editor of the S, M. Herald,
has been talking to the Millions Club about
“Problems of Peace.” Among other things :
If through the competition of Japan the ground
was cut from under the feet of Australian manufac-
turers, she would be reduced to the position she
occupied before Federation.
“Federation,” of course, means the Federal
tariff —Kingston’s more or less Protectionist
tariff which, so far as N. S. Wales is con-
cerned, superseded the Yes-no tariff of G.
ReidT Heney sees, in spite of his long years
in the S. M. Herald’s chair —or because of
it —that the new competition calls for still
higher duties if we are not to be “reduced”
to the coolie position we were in under his
old paper’s Freetrade joss.
Bushell, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), declares that
“the Nationalists are paying Laborites to
encourage Bolshevism throughout the State
so that the Labor party will receive the
blame.” It is a trifle far-fetched, for it is a
rare sort of man who will pay another man
to kill him and burn his house so that certain
third parties may be suspected after he is
dead. There has long been a suspicion, how-
ever, that many Labor leaders and Labor
papers were subsidised by the importers. In
return, presumably for consideration re-
ceived, they have “sunk the fiscal issue,”
and directed all the fury of their party when
madness came upon a section of it to “taking
over” {i.e., confiscating) the factories and
workshops, with never a word about taking
over the importers’ warehouses, thus doing
their best to scare people from investing in
local industries. But real Bolshevik riot, in
the course of which an importer might be
hanged as high as an employer of Australian
artisans at union wages, was almost cer-
tainly not on the programme. The man who
buys a devil always reckons that it will be
an obedient devil and not go into business for
itself.

Government Notice.
QUEENSLAND.
Applications arc* invited from per-
sons Qualified to fill the position of
General Manager of the Chillagoe Smelting
Works, about to be re-opened and Worked
under State control,
The salary of the position will be £l5OO
per annum, with house, light and fuel and
travelling expenses when travelling is neces-
sary on the same conditions as provided for
positions of similar salary in the Public
Service,
Applicants are required to have had recent
practical experience in the management of
metallurgical Works at which silver, lead and
copper ores were treated.
The Successful applicant must be prepared
to take up his duties without delay.
Applications must reach the undersigned
not later than 26th March, 1919,
HENRY MARSHALL,
Under-Secretary.
Department of Mines,
Brisbane, 4th March, 1919.
, Public Notices,
OTICE TO GOLD-PRODUCERS.
For the purpose of enabling Australian gold*
producers to realise the best price obtainable for
their gold, the Acting Prime Minister has agreed to
PERMIT the EXPORT of NEW GOLD from Aus-
tralia for a period of three monthsj This permission
Is conditional on (inter aim),
(a) The formation Of a Gold-producers' Association ;
( b) The admission of all bona-fi4c producers of
gold within the Commonwealth to membership
of Association if they so desire.
The memorandum and articles of the Association
■were prepared under the directions of the committee
appointed by the recent Gold Producers’ Conference,
at which all States were represented, and the Associ-
ation has been registered as a limited liability com-
pany with a nominal capital of £1250 in 25,000
shares of one shilling each. Each applicant for
membership will be required to take up one share
in respect of every four standard ounces of gold
produced in the month immediately preceding the
date of his application.
All bona-fide gold-producers are eligible for mem-
bership, and those who become members on or before
SOth April, 1919, will be entitled to participate in
the profits of the first half-yearly accounting period.
The business of the Association will be conducted
by a Board of five directors, two of whom will he
elected by the members resident in Western Aus-
tralia, one each by the members resident in Victoria
and Queensland, and one by the members in New
South Wales, Tasmania and South Australia com-
bined. The following are the provisional directors
appointed to act until the first half-yearly general
meeting of the Association, viz., E. C. Dyason, G. C.
King, T. W. Wellsted, J. L. Wharton and H. L.
Wilkinson.
Permits to export gold will only be issued by the
Commonwealth Treasury on the production of Mint
certificates for gold lodged by individual members,
and participation in any profits realised will be pro-
portionate to the amount of gold represented by
such Mint certificate. The Association will in no
way interfere with members’ existing arrangements
for realisation of their gold.
Intending applicants for membership should com-
municafe with the undersigned, from whom full
information may be obtained.
THOS. MAUGHAN, Acting Secretary.
Gold Prodiupers’ Association Limited, *4B Queen
Street, Melbourne, March 6, 1919.

“Edd.vson’s” tale of man’s inhumanity
Norton* had driven his car over the same track
several times, but in the gloom he failed to make
allowance for recent rains having undermined the
load from the big, self-made drain running parallel
with tiie track on the left, and presently found him-
self hurtling into a great, neglected furze-hedge,
striking a feeble post-and-rail fence hv the way,
while his car keeled over into the gutter and kicked
its wheels in the air like an expiring hen. But a
Good Samaritan was at hand. His name was
Harridan, and he was a shb'rt, copiously-bewhiskered
man in old black clothes. He threw his portman-
teau under the hedge, hauled Norton out of the
prickles and gave him drink from a corked soda-
water bottle he carried in his hip-pocket; whereupon
Norton opened his ey*. and feebly requested enlight-
enment—where was he? “Shire of Dunrobin, sub-
section 2,” said Harridan. Across a paddock to the
left a white house was visible in the dying day.
Up 'the road a quarter of a mile further a red house
was not visible at all. Towards the latter Harridan
began to pilot Norton, leaned heavily and
groaned. He was very sore. It, was quite dark when
they knocked at the front door of the red house.
Norton noticed that the windows were boarded.
Harridan knocked at the front door yet again. No
answer. He knocked for a third time. No answer.
Norton groaned eloquently. Harridan conveyed the
sick man to the back door and knocked again. He
had knocked for perhaps five minutes, when a coarse,
hard voice inside demanded what in Tophet he
wanted, “I’ve got a very badly-hurt man here,”
replied Harridan in thin, plaintive tones. “He has
been injured in a motor accident.” “Take him outer
this!” replied the hard voice. “But I tell you he is
badly hurt.” Swish! Somebody had opened a side
window, and swiped out a pail of very hot water.
Norton 'got some of it, Harridan got the rest. “Are
you goin’?” inquired the hard voice. “But what
am 1 to do with this poor| man?” demanded Harri-
dan. Aside to Norton he said: “Groan, d n you!
Why don’t you groan?” Norton groaned. “I’ve
got a bit of a dog in here,” said the voice. “I’ll he
giving him a run presently.” “But this is most
inhuman. This poor fellow may die.” The person
inside let the dog out for his run. He was a very
nasty dog. Fortunately he gave the best of his atten-
tion to Harridan, but Norton sustained three bites
before he could limp off the property. Then Norton
sat down; and so did Harridan. The latter took
his whiskers off and put them in his pocket.
“You'd better try Bates's, the white house down the
road,” he said. “This is the fifth attempt I’ve made
to get into that red house.” “Why, what the devil
arc you at?” demanded Norton. “Me?” replied
Harridan, dressing his bitten calf with a handker-
chief. “I'm a bailiff!”
“Jerry B.”: Last year Melbourne build-
ers started on a sort of strike, chiefly on
account of the high price of timbers. Then
the* armistice happened in Europe, and the
timber people took heart, believing that the
glad news would bring on a rush of orders.
It didn’t. Apparently everybody with a
building in view reckoned he would wait and
watch prices fall. The timber people kept
their prices firm, and the builders kept on
doing nothing. But at last there are signs
that some people are getting tired. Prices
for building sizes are on the down-grade,
with a number of unorganised timber-men
underselling the association. Even now.
however, builders are shy. The man who
builds houses to sell knows what is likely
to happen to his new property when another
10 per cent, comes off the cost of materials.

Bert Leywood, an energetic Australian
who has travelled the world over as a
vaudeville artist, has just left for India with
the idea of stringing the principal cities
together in a moving-picture advertising cir-
cuit. Leywood is going to barrack for Aus-
tralian goods by means chiefly of cartoons,
etc., prepared by Harry Julius in Sydney,
and he reckons that the enterprise will soon
produce enough cash to enable him to bio-
graph the virtues of Australian products
throughout Eastern Asia.
